Quality control for an LED company was not a thing Joel had imagined anyone losing sleep over until Will lost it. Here, in camp, Will still had the shape of a man who knew how things were supposed to work. Filters. Knots. Rod guides. Maps. The smaller systems were available. The larger one had let him go.

Mike and Alex argued their tent into position. The ground looked flatter than it was. Every place that seemed level had a root, a knob of rock, or a shallow runnel where rain would collect if the weather changed.

"Door facing water," Alex said.

"Door out of the wind," Mike said.

"I want a view."

"You'll be unconscious."

"I dream visually."

Mike stared at him.

Will, crouched over his hammock sack, said, "Put his side facing the water. If a bear comes, it gets him first."

Alex nodded. "Finally. Logic."

Joel fed one guyline around a trunk and tied it off. The line tightened with a small creak. Behind him, Mike zipped the tent door open, then closed it, testing it the way men tested zippers in the woods: not because they distrusted the zipper exactly, but because a zipper that failed at night became a moral problem.

The zipper ran around the curve with a dry insect sound.

Mike looked at Alex. "Don't yank on it."

"I have used zippers before."

"Not always well."

Alex spread his hands. "Quit mothering me."

Joel glanced over.

That one had been said enough now. It belonged to Alex fully. He did not have to think before handing it out. Later, Joel would remember how carelessly the line had passed through camp, not as a warning or an omen, just a bit of him moving from mouth to air and gone.

They staked the tent, tightened lines, and set sleeping pads inside. The pads squeaked and sighed as they unrolled. Joel shook out his hammock, clipped it, sat once to set the sag, and stood again. He pitched the tarp above it even though the sky was clear. Early September could change its mind without warning, and the first night of any trip was not the night to trust a blue sky.

Will wandered toward the water with his rod before deciding whether he was sleeping in nylon or in the air.

Mike saw him. "Water first."

"I'm at the water."

"For drinking."

Will stopped, considered whether he could argue it, and came back for the filter.

They divided chores without formally dividing them. Mike and Will handled water because Will had opinions about filtering and Mike had opinions about where the bag should hang. Joel handled dinner because he usually did. Not every dinner, not always the cooking, but a lot of the prep. He had a dehydrator at home and had become the kind of man who could talk about sauce leather without shame. Jerky at a hundred sixty for five or six hours. Sauce lower and longer. Sweet potatoes if he remembered to slice them evenly and if the trays were not already full.

The others made fun of him until they ate what he brought. Then they asked how much was left.

Tonight was dried tortellini with Joel's dehydrated red sauce, plus parmesan packets and a little oil. Easy first-night food. No fish required, no coals, no timing beyond boil, soak, simmer, stir. The more ambitious meals could wait: quesadillas with chorizo and red onion, lentils and yams if Will actually caught fish, fish fry if the lake gave them enough, Zatarain's with sausage when nobody wanted to think, mac and cheese with bacon when morale needed to be purchased cheaply.

Joel set the sauce pieces in a pot with water to soften. They looked like dark red bark.

Alex came over and peered in. "That food?"

"Eventually."

"It looks like something peeled off a canoe."

"You'll like it."

"You always say that."

"I'm often right."

Will called from the water, "The jerky's already open, by the way."

Joel turned. "It was sealed."

"Was."

Mike came up from shore chewing. "Good batch."

"You opened my dinner-adjacent morale system?"

"It was labeled jerky."

"That is not consent."

Alex walked to the food bag with sudden interest. "Is this the Doc's stuff?"

"Doc's-ish," Joel said. "I changed it."

Mike took another piece. "Don't change it more."

Will appeared with the gravity bag, water dripping from one elbow. "Agreed. This is one of your useful traits."

Joel looked at him. "My useful trait is meat dehydration?"

"One of them."

"I'll update the wedding vows."

Alex bit into a piece of jerky and nodded with genuine seriousness. "Okay. That's good. That has appropriate intensity."

"Food can have opinions now?" Joel said.

"Jerky is allowed. Tuna is not."

The stove came out when the water was ready. Mike set it on a flat patch of granite near the fire ring, screwed the fuel canister in, and turned the valve. The first click did nothing. The second click did nothing. The third click gave a blue flutter of flame.

"Modern civilization," Alex said.

"Don't get attached," Mike said.

Will hung the gravity bag from a low limb. "That limb is sketchy."

"It's holding," Mike said.

"Lots of things hold until they don't."

"Put that on your own memorial shirt," Alex said.

Dinner took its time coming back to life. Joel stirred the sauce as it softened and reddened the water. The smell came up slowly: tomato, garlic, dried herbs, a faint sweetness from hours in the dehydrator. Not home exactly. A camp version of home, thinner and smokier and more valuable because it had crossed water.

The mosquitoes came hard at dusk.

They came out of the spruce and up from the damp places between rocks, first a few, then a whining net around ears and wrists. The air had been comfortable and then it was not. Everyone put on long sleeves. Will found a head net and wore it like a man proving a point. Alex mocked him until one mosquito got him on the eyelid.

"Head net me," Alex said.

Will looked at him through mesh. "No."

"I apologize to the technology."

"Too late."

Joel poured the sauce over the tortellini and stirred. The pot got passed around with bowls. Mike ate first because he had cooked the noodles and because he was holding the spoon when it became food. Alex approached his bowl with caution and then ate quickly, which was the highest compliment he gave anything with visible seasoning.

"Too much?" Will asked.

Alex shook his head. "No. This has discipline."

"Food review accepted," Joel said.

Mike scraped his bowl. "Make more next time."

"I made enough."

"You made enough for the men you imagined us to be."

Will said, "We also have tuna and Triscuits if you want to ruin your own evening."

"Lunch food stays lunch food," Mike said.

Alex took another piece of jerky. "Unless society collapses."

"Day one," Joel said.

"Plenty of time."

They cleaned bowls with hot water and scraps of tortilla because Will claimed that was efficient and Mike claimed it was disgusting until he did it too. The stove cooled on granite. Someone set a mug down beside Joel and it made a small ceramic tick.

"Careful," Joel said. "That one's mine."

Alex glanced at it. "I know. It looks judgmental."

"Everything I own can't be judgmental."

"I have evidence."

After dinner, Will went back to the shore with his rod. Nobody told him not to. Everyone knew by now that Will would fish if there was water, light, and enough motor function left in his arms. It was less a choice than a tide.

The lake darkened by degrees. Across the water, the opposite shore went from green to black. The first stars appeared between the pine tops before the sky was fully night. The chill came up from the stone. It entered through damp sandal straps and wet cuffs and the places where sweat had dried into salt.

Will cast from the edge of the granite shelf. The lure made a small whip in the air and a softer sound when it touched water. He reeled slowly, patiently, his head net pushed back now that the bugs had thinned in the open breeze.

Alex sat near the fire ring with his fleece zipped to his chin. "You actually enjoy that?"

"Yes."

"You know there are fish at stores."

"They're not surprised to see you."

"That's the appeal?"

Will cast again. "Part of it."

Mike had built a small fire because the ring existed, the wood was there, and the evening had dropped cold enough to justify flame. It was not much. Thumb-thick sticks, pine curls, a few split pieces from the dry stack. The hatchet bit twice into one stubborn piece before Mike gave up and fed it half-burned ends from the old ring. The fire took with a low crackle, then settled into itself.

Alex came alive in firelight.

He could not help it. The day's fatigue left him, or else the fire gave him a place to spend it. He told the story of a backpacking trip in the Cascades where a friend had packed six pounds of cheese and no rain jacket. He told it too long and too well, with voices, with pauses, with a whole middle section about the cheese sweating through a stuff sack. It should have been cut and was not because everyone was tired and the fire was warm and Will, despite himself, kept asking questions.

"Six pounds?" Will said.

"Minimum."

"That's not physically plausible."

"It was a brick. A dairy ingot."

"What kind of cheese?"

"Orange."

"That's not a kind."

"It was by day three."

Joel watched Alex hold them there. Even Mike listened, though he pretended not to by using a stick to worry the fire. Alex had a talent for making annoyance indistinguishable from attention. He required a room, a landing, a fire ring. He made you give it to him, and sometimes the gift became real after you surrendered it.

Mike sat back against a log. "People don't know how to be uncomfortable anymore."

The line went into the small space around the fire and stayed there.

Will looked at him. "People who pack six pounds of cheese know discomfort intimately."

"That's not what I mean."

"I know what you mean."

"You don't."

"I do. You mean everybody's soft except whoever happens to be talking."

Mike's jaw moved once. "I mean people want the story without the part where things suck."

Alex opened his mouth, then closed it. For once, the story had been taken from him and used for something heavier.

Joel fed a stick into the fire and let the end catch. He knew the remark was not about cheese or backpacking or even the trip. It was about the way time had made the group harder to assemble. About jobs, partners, houses, money, knees, the little negotiations by which adulthood reclaimed a man. It was about Mike's life staying loose while everyone else's had tightened. Or it was about nothing that neat. Mike liked to make his bitterness sound philosophical because complaint was easier to defend when it pretended to be principle.

Will said, quieter, "There's plenty of discomfort."

Mike snorted. "Sure."

The word found Will more directly than Mike seemed to intend, or exactly as directly. Will looked at the fire. In the orange light the hollows under his eyes showed darker. Joel thought of the late call again, Will laughing too hard about being between opportunities, about LinkedIn turning everyone into a motivational hostage, about how there was a special shame in knowing the name of every failed diode on a board no one wanted anymore.

Joel waited for Will to answer. Will did not.

The fire settled, and one piece of pine clicked as sap heated inside it.

Alex rescued the evening because that was one of his uses.

"Speaking as a man who voluntarily packed powdered eggs after the Incident," he said, "I have known the valley of suffering."

Will coughed a laugh.

"We all agreed never again," Joel said.

"They were not eggs," Mike said.

"They were egg-adjacent," Will said.

"They were yellow punishment."

Alex nodded gravely. "We learned. We grew. We became oatmeal men."

"Pancakes tomorrow if morale requires civilization," Will said.

Mike looked at him. "Pancakes take time."

"Joy takes time."

"Breakfast bars take thirty seconds."

"Despair also takes thirty seconds."

Joel smiled and looked out toward the water.

The canoes were pulled high above shore, turned slightly on the slope so rain would not sit in them if weather came. The paddles lay underneath, blades tucked out of the open, shafts parallel in the shadows. One painter rope hung loose and touched the hull when the breeze moved it. Tap. Pause. Tap. A small sound, ordinary enough to vanish unless you were listening for ordinary things.

Will came back from the shore without a fish and pretended not to care.

"Nothing?" Mike said.

"I had one follow."

"Of course you did."

"It's a real phenomenon."

"So is lying."

Will set the rod against a tree and took his mug from the rock. Ceramic on granite. A bright little sound. He drank and winced. "Cold."

"Drink while it's hot," Mike said.

"I can't drink it while it's hot if I'm fishing."

"That sounds like a you problem."

"You can't just reassign physics to character flaws."

"I can do whatever I want in camp."

Alex stood and stretched. His joints popped. "I'm taking a leak. Nobody write a poem about it."

Joel looked up. The line was close to something and not it. Close enough that it almost mattered, then did not.

"Take a headlamp," Mike said.

"It's ten feet."

"Take a headlamp."

Alex patted his pockets, found the lamp, and put it on without turning it on. "Quit mothering me."

He walked to the edge of camp, past the tent and toward the darker line of spruce. His sandals scraped once on rock, then went quiet on duff. A moment later his headlamp clicked on, throwing a white band through low branches.

From the trees came his laugh. Not loud. More private than usual. Maybe he had nearly tripped. Maybe he had amused himself. Then the light shifted, and he said something they could not make out.

Will looked toward him. "You fall in?"

"No," Alex called. "I'm communing."

"With what?" Mike asked.

"None of your business."

"Don't wander."

"Quit-"

"We heard it," Will said.

Alex laughed again. The laugh came through spruce this time, broken by trunks and needles, not by a motel wall or bridge concrete or bar noise. It sounded like him and less like him for the way the woods handled it.

Joel turned his mug in his hands.

The lake gave a soft slap against the rocks below camp. Somewhere far off, a loon called once. Then again, too soon or only seeming too soon because the first note had not fully left Joel's ear. The second call bent thinner and lower, as if the lake had tried to return the first and failed to put it back together.

Will stopped with his mug halfway to his mouth.

Mike said, "Loon."

"I know," Will said.

Nobody said anything else.

Alex came back a minute later, headlamp still on, beam hitting their faces until Mike told him to point it down. The ordinary world returned with him: fleece, sandals, bad peanut-butter breath, the need to brush teeth and secure food and keep sparks out of nylon.

They cleaned camp.

The food barrel was latched and carried back from the tent and hammocks, not far enough to satisfy a ranger and far enough to satisfy tired men. Mike wanted it wedged between two rocks where it could not roll. Will wanted it farther downwind. Joel helped move it once and then stopped helping the argument. The latch clicked again when Mike checked it.

Dry bags went under the tent vestibule or under Joel's hammock tarp. Paddles stayed stacked under the canoes. The stove cooled on granite. Someone's mug tipped and rolled until Joel caught it with his foot. Toothpaste spit flashed pale on the edge of the firelight and vanished into dirt.

The fire burned low. Mike poured a little water over one edge, then stopped because there was still heat enough to sit with. Steam rose and carried the smell of wet ash. The night pressed in around the small ring of men and gear.

When Joel finally went to his hammock, the zipper on his bug net sounded louder than it had in daylight. He slid in carefully, felt the fabric take his weight, and lay still while the hammock found its shape around him. The tarp above him was a low dark roof. Beyond its edge he could see a strip of sky between pine branches.

Will came over and stood near his own half-hung hammock, looking between it and the tent.

"You actually committing?" Joel asked.

Will looked at the trees. Then at the tent. Then at the lake, where a ring spread and vanished. "No."

"Coward."

"Strategist."

Will gathered his quilt and went to the tent, bringing cold air and the smell of lake water when he opened the door. Mike and Alex were still talking in low voices near the fire. Mike said something about tomorrow's first narrow section. Alex answered with a wordless sound of disbelief.

Will whispered from inside the tent, "He's going to push early."

"Mike?" Joel said.

"No, the loon."

Joel smiled in the dark.

"He wants to make the big lake before wind," Will said.

"That bad?"

"No. Maybe. It's fine if we get there clean."

"Fine according to you or fine according to Mike?"

Will was quiet a moment. "Exactly."

Joel listened. Fire ticking. Water against rock. The faint scrape of Mike's log shifting under weight. Alex's voice, muffled by nylon and distance, telling the end of something he had already told once.

Then silence came down, not all at once, but by layers.

Will stopped talking. Mike and Alex put out the fire. Their tent zipper opened and closed. A headlamp beam crossed Joel's tarp and disappeared. The food barrel latch clicked once more, or Joel thought it did, though it could have been a buckle on Mike's pack.

He lay awake long enough for the first night to become itself. The body did not yet trust the hammock. His shoulder wanted to be elsewhere. His feet stayed cold in the damp socks he had meant to change earlier and had not. From the tent came Mike's heavier breathing, then Alex turning on a sleeping pad, then Will's small dry catch every few breaths. Each sound had a source. That was the comfort. A body, a zipper, a buckle, a rope, a hull. The mind could name a thing and let it go.

He woke later without knowing why.

The hammock held him in a shallow curve. His shoulders ached. His feet were still cold. For a moment Joel did not move.

Outside, the lake was quiet. The camp had the dense stillness of first night, when every object was in the place men had put it and had not yet become familiar enough to ignore.

From the shoreline came a soft knock.

One sound. Hollow. Wood or hull against stone.

Joel opened his eyes fully.

He waited.

Nothing followed. No voice. No footstep. No second knock. Only the lake moving lightly below the granite and the wind worrying the top of one pine.

He pictured the canoes pulled high, angled on rock, paddles tucked beneath them, painter rope loose enough to touch. Wind could do it. Cooling hull could do it. Water lifting a branch could do it. There were a dozen ways for the night to make one ordinary sound.

From the tent, Will breathed in and out. The dry catch came again, and then the cleaner breath after it.

Joel rolled slightly in the hammock, careful not to shift too hard and wake the trees with strap noise, and closed his eyes again.

The camp held. The lake held. The dark kept its own counsel.

After a while, Joel slept.


# Chapter 5 - Beaver Work

Joel woke to the sound of Will's zipper.

Not the tent zipper at first. Smaller. A bag, probably. Then the tent door itself, slow enough that whoever opened it thought slowness made less sound. It did not. The teeth came apart one by one in the gray morning, dry and careful and loud.

Joel opened his eyes.

The hammock held him in its shallow curve. Above him, the tarp was beaded with dew. Beyond its edge the sky had gone pale over the lake, not sunrise exactly but the idea of it. The air was cold enough that he had pulled his beanie over his ears sometime in the night without remembering doing it. His nose was cold. His feet were warm inside the quilt until he moved them and found the damp in his sandal straps beside the hammock.

Will came out of the tent bent over and half-zipped into a fleece. He stood there for a moment, looking at the lake like a man who had expected it to do something overnight and was checking the result.

"You alive?" Joel said.

Will flinched and looked toward the hammock. "Jesus."

"Good morning."

"You look dead in that thing."

"That's the appeal."

Will rubbed his face. His hair stood up on one side. "I thought about sleeping in mine."

"You thought bravely."

"I made a weather-based decision."

"You slept in the tent."

"I made a tent-based decision."

The fire ring was gray and cold. The tent had not moved except for one side panel sagging where Alex had probably pressed into it. The canoes lay above the shoreline, dark with dew, paddles tucked underneath where they had left them. A loose painter rope touched a hull once in the faint breeze.

Tap.

Then nothing.

Joel looked at it and then away. A rope tapping a hull was a rope tapping a hull. He had been here long enough, in this kind of morning, to know how many objects could become briefly articulate when air moved through camp.

Will had already seen the rods.

"They're wet," he said.

"Everything's wet."

"Not everything is a precision instrument."

"Your rod is a stick with jewelry."

Will looked offended but too tired to answer well. "That's not even worth correcting."

Mike's voice came from the tent. "If you two are talking about fishing before coffee, I'm leaving you here."

Alex groaned from inside. "Is the wilderness always this administrative?"

Joel swung his feet out of the hammock and sat up. Cold came through his base layer and made him move faster. He pulled on his fleece, then his wet hiking sandals, grimacing as the straps took hold of his feet. He had camp shoes buried somewhere but did not want to open the pack yet. The first step on granite was sharp and cold.

Morning camp took shape without ceremony. Water first. Stove next. Breakfast by negotiation.

Will wanted pancakes because he had remembered them into existence the night before. Mike wanted bars and coffee because he wanted miles. Alex said he supported pancakes in principle but not if the batter had texture. Joel found instant oats near the top of the food bag and held them up.

"This is the compromise."

"That's not compromise," Will said. "That's surrender with hot water."

"Brown sugar."

"Apology sugar."

Mike was already filling the pot. "Oats."

Alex sat up in the tent with his hair crushed flat on one side. "Do oats have seasoning?"

"They have whatever you put in them," Joel said.

"So no."

"Peanut butter."

Alex considered that. "That's a condiment with ambition."

"Oats," Mike said again, because repetition was how he ended meetings.

They boiled water and made coffee with Folgers packets. The packets always seemed like a bad idea at home and a good idea in camp. Tear, pour, stir, drink something hot enough to correct the body. Joel ate oats from his mug and watched fog lift from the lake in low pieces. It moved like smoke that had forgotten fire.

The far shore came in and out behind it. Pine, then nothing. Granite, then blank gray. Sound arrived cleanly through the damp: Will's spoon against his mug, Mike folding the map case open, Alex coughing once into his sleeve, the stove valve ticking as it cooled. In the ordinary world, everything announced itself by touching something else.

Mike checked the map while standing. He did not like sitting before the route was decided, even if the route had been decided months ago.

"We want to be moving by eight," he said.

Will looked at the sky. "That means nine."

"It means eight."

Alex, still inside the tent, said, "It means someone will be looking for his headlamp at eight-ten."

"I know where my headlamp is," Mike said.

Nobody said anything.

Mike looked up. "I do."

Joel sipped coffee. "Then we're ahead of schedule spiritually."

They broke camp in cold stages. Sleeping bags stuffed. Pads rolled. Tent shaken, fly wet enough to be annoying but not soaked. Joel took down his hammock last because he liked how quickly his whole bedroom became a fist-sized bundle of fabric and straps. Will packed his unused hammock with the expression of a man putting away a tool he had not deserved.

The food barrel latch snapped closed. The stove cooled, clicked once as Mike handled it, and went into its bag. Paddles came out from under the canoes. Wet blades, grit on the shafts. Will wiped his rod guides with a cloth and pretended no one noticed.

"Fishing while we move?" Alex asked.

"In spirit," Will said.

"No," Mike said.

"I said in spirit."

"I heard the part you didn't say."

"You often do, and usually badly."

They loaded the boats. The morning light grew cleaner but not warmer. Mosquitoes were thin near shore, slow enough that they seemed confused by their own hunger. The lake held the canoes without wave. Whatever had knocked in the night had left no sign. No shifted hull. No loose paddle. No branch caught on the rocks. Joel checked because checking was easier than wondering, and because the body believed a thing more readily when the eyes had done their work.

By a little after eight-thirty they pushed off.

The first part of the morning was easy water. The lake stretched north in a long reach, then narrowed around a low point where black spruce crowded the shore. Mike and Will led. Joel and Alex followed. Alex paddled better after breakfast than he had near the end of the day before. Maybe sleep helped. Maybe the cold made him want to be useful. Maybe he had reached the phase of complaint where the only honorable thing left was competence.

They passed the point and entered a channel not much wider than the one from yesterday, but lower and flatter. Alder came down to the water on both sides. Grass leaned out over mud. The water lost the clean dark of lake depth and turned brown and slow. It smelled of wet bark and old leaves, the sour richness of things busy rotting into something useful.

"Here we go," Mike called back.

"Here we go good or here we go bad?" Alex said.

"Beaver work."

Will turned his head. "Plural?"

"Could be."

"Great."

Alex looked back at Joel. "Define beaver work."

Joel said, "Sometimes you get out and drag."

"That's work for us."

"Correct."

"Misleading name."

"They did their part."

Alex looked at the low banks sliding past, the cut stems bright as bone near the waterline. "I grew up around animals that could kill you. Beavers feel insulting."

"Beavers can ruin your day," Will said.

"Lots of things can ruin my day. Plain oatmeal. Small talk. Men named Todd."

Mike looked over his shoulder. "Paddle."

Alex paddled.

The channel kept narrowing. The alders leaned until their upper branches crossed over the water and made a low, jointed roof. The paddles clipped drowned stems. Once Joel's blade struck something under the surface that gave back a hollow wooden note, and Alex looked at him.

"Rock," Joel said.

"Rocks don't sound like doors."

"Submerged branch, then."

"Better."

Ahead, Will said something to Mike that Joel could not catch. Mike answered with one hand lifted off the paddle, palm down, the universal sign for enough. Will turned forward again. He had his rod across his lap in a way that pretended not to be ready.

The first dam came twenty minutes later. It crossed the channel in a low brown ridge of sticks and mud, water shining over the top in thin sheets. On one side a narrow chute had been opened by previous travelers and then half-closed again by the animals, as if the dispute were ongoing and neither party had the authority to settle it.

Mike guided his canoe toward it, stepped out in knee-deep water, and held the boat while Will got out and pulled from the front.

"Looks simple," Alex said.

"Don't say that," Joel said.

"Why?"

"Because the river hears confidence."

Alex smiled. "That sounds like something from your Wisconsin cult."

"I'm from St. Louis."

"Madison changed you."

They came in on the right. Joel stepped out first. Cold water went up over his sandal straps and around his calves. The mud under the surface accepted one foot, then the other, and did not want to give them back. Alex got out and immediately sank deeper.

"Oh, come on."

"Don't fight it straight up," Joel said. "Rock your foot."

"I am negotiating with it."

"You're losing."

The canoe slid halfway onto the dam, then stopped. Joel pushed from the back. Alex pulled from the front. The hull rasped over wet sticks. Something under it cracked. Water ran around their legs with more force than it had seemed to have from the boat, all that brown quiet narrowing itself over the dam and remembering it could move.

"Hold up," Alex said.

Joel stopped.

Alex bent and freed the painter rope from a branch woven into the dam. He held it up with theatrical patience. "See? Useful."

"I didn't say otherwise."

"You were thinking it."

"I was thinking many things."

Mike, already on the far side, called, "You coming?"

Alex looked back at him. "We're preserving infrastructure."

"You're standing in beaver trash."

Will said, "Technically impressive beaver trash."

"That's your review of everything in nature," Mike said.

They got the boat over. It dropped into the water beyond with a hollow slap and a little roll that sent water against Joel's thighs. Alex made a sound between a laugh and a curse.

"Refreshing," he said.

Joel steadied the canoe and let the mud take back the footprints they had made. It closed slowly, dark water filling the shape of their legs. The dam stayed where it was, indifferent to effort, already repairing itself in the mind if not yet in the sticks. Men crossed things and called it progress. Animals crossed them back.

The second dam was worse.

The channel bent left and disappeared behind alder. The dam sat just beyond the bend, higher, newer, still wet with the look of recent labor. Water backed up before it in a shallow pond full of drowned grass. A lodge rose on the left, sticks piled against a bank where fresh cuts showed pale. The chew marks were clean and angled, each sapling taken down by a mouth that knew its work.

Will said, "Somebody's active."

Mike said, "Beavers usually are."

"I mean recent."

"Are we filing a complaint?"

Alex lifted his paddle. "I support labor."

The pull-over took longer. They unloaded a few bags because the boats were catching on the crest. Food barrel out. Day bags out. Rods handed forward like fragile diplomatic gifts. Alex took one rod and held it away from himself.

"This thing makes me feel irresponsible."

"It should," Will said.

Joel pushed and slipped. One foot went sideways between two submerged branches. For a second the mud held him and the current leaned against his knees. He caught the gunwale with one hand and steadied.

"You good?" Will called.

"Yeah."

"Don't break Madison," Alex said.

"Madison bends," Joel said.

Mike was on top of the dam, pulling his canoe by the front line. "Lift, don't drag."

"Buy lighter boats," Joel said.

Mike looked over his shoulder. "That line's less funny when you're wrong."

"It wasn't meant to be funny."

"It never is."

They got the boats over one by one. By the end, everyone was wet to the knee or higher. Alex had mud up one thigh. Will had a leech on his ankle, which he noticed only after he had already described leech behavior in too much detail.

"No, no, no," he said, more annoyed than scared. "Absolutely not."

Mike looked down. "It's tiny."

"It's attached."

"That's how they work."

Will flicked at it with a fingernail and missed. "That's not how I want it to work."

Alex crouched beside him. "Allow me. I grew up with real parasites."

"You grew up in Alaska, not a medieval swamp."

Alex pinched the leech off and held it up for half a second before flinging it into the grass. "Nature defeated."

Will stared at his ankle. "I hate that I needed you."

"You'll heal."

They moved again.

The morning warmed. The chill lifted from their clothes but left them damp. The sun came through breaks in the alder and lit the water in copper patches. Every few bends the channel opened into a marshy pool where lily pads held yellowing edges and dragonflies stitched the air low over the surface. Then it tightened again and made them work.

Joel felt the route changing the men. Not dramatically. It was too early for drama, and the day was still bright enough to make seriousness feel theatrical. But the first-day jokes had less gloss on them. Everyone had slept, but not enough to erase the weight of portage and water from their bodies. Mike's instructions were shorter. Will still talked, but more of it came between breaths. Alex performed, then forgot to perform, then remembered when silence lasted too long.

The route had its own method. It gave them a clear stretch, then a snag. A little current, then mud. A turn that looked open until the boat entered it and the alder took both sides of the canoe in its hands. Joel began to understand how a map could be true and still leave out the truth. A blue line did not tell you how many times you would step into brown water and feel your foot disappear.

At a shallow gravel bar, Mike stopped to check the map. Will cast once while standing in ankle-deep water, because his body had been waiting for the chance before his mind approved it.

Mike said, "Really?"

"One cast."

"You said that yesterday."

"And I have grown as a person."

The lure landed near a sunken branch with a small plip. Something moved in the shadow. Will's whole body changed.

"Fish?" Alex said.

"Maybe."

"Useful fish or emotionally symbolic fish?"

Will reeled slowly. "All fish are both."

The lure came back clean.

Mike gave him five seconds of silence. "Done?"

Will clipped the lure to a guide. "Done."

"Miracles happen," Mike said.

They ate lunch out of the boats because the ground was mostly mud and grass. Tuna packets, Triscuits, trail mix, jerky already diminished by theft and democracy. Alex examined a salmon packet as if it might argue with him.

"Just eat the crackers," Mike said.

"I'm branching."

"You branch like a dead tree."

Joel handed him a strip of jerky. "Here. Safe food."

Alex took it. "Your usefulness continues."

Will said, "Don't praise him too much. He'll dehydrate a whole cow."

"I've considered it," Joel said.

"Of course you have."

The channel after lunch became less forgiving. Beaver cuts narrowed the turns. Drowned branches reached up from underwater, invisible until a hull scraped them. Mike called back directions in clipped pieces. Left. Stay right. Shallow. Watch the snag. Joel relayed what mattered to Alex when Alex was looking at the wrong thing.

The work made a kind of language: blade drip, hull scrape, breath, boot pulled from mud, branch released and whipping back. A man could listen to it and know where the others were without seeing them. Mike's canoe ahead by the harder knocks. Will's rod tip ticking once against aluminum. Alex's paddle coming out too high and scattering water beside Joel's knee. Their passage entered the channel in pieces and remained there behind them, as if the route kept a memory of touch.

A branch cracked in the alder to their left.

Not loud. A clean snap, followed by brush moving low and fast.

Alex stopped paddling mid-stroke.

The canoe turned slightly.

"Hold up," he said.

Joel put his paddle in to correct. Ahead, Mike's boat kept moving three more strokes before Will turned.

"You guys hear that?"

Mike looked toward the alder. "Animal."

"What kind of animal?" Alex asked.

"The kind in bushes."

"That narrows it."

Will held his paddle across his knees and listened. The brush moved once more, farther back. Leaves shook. Then nothing.

"Beaver?" Joel said.

"Could be," Mike said.

"Could be bear," Alex said, but lightly.

Mike turned his head just enough. "Don't start."

"I'm not starting. I'm identifying risk factors."

"That's Will's job," Joel said.

Will, still listening, said, "Bear would probably sound heavier."

"Probably," Mike said. "Everybody paddle."

The word everybody did its work. They moved.

Joel looked once into the alder as they passed. He saw leaves, mud, the dark arch of roots where the bank had undercut, a few fresh chips on a cut sapling. No animal. No shape. No reason to remember it except the way Alex had stopped before anyone else did, his shoulders tightening under the straps of his PFD, paddle held over water.

A person could grow up in Alaska and still become alert to brush. Maybe especially then. Maybe that was what growing up around larger danger gave you: not bravery, but a better ear for the first small thing before the large thing decided to become visible.

The channel opened after that, though not into easy water. It widened into a marshy run where the current slowed and the wind found them from the side. The sky had changed while they worked the beaver dams. It was still clear overhead, but a smear of cloud sat low in the west. The light had gone flatter. Far ahead, a wider lake showed between low banks.

Mike checked the map without stopping, one hand on the paddle, the case open against his knees.

"Bigger water after this," he said.

"How big?" Alex asked.

"Big enough."

Will made a sound.

Mike said, "What?"

"Nothing."

"Say it."

"I'm just saying, 'big enough' is not a measurement."

"It's not the problem lake."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the lake before the problem lake."

Alex turned around. "Do the lakes know which one is the problem lake?"

Joel said, "Mike tells them."

Mike ignored that. "We get across this, short ugly bit, then camp."

"Second camp?" Alex said.

"No. Lunch two-point-five."

"Don't name things."

"I'll name whatever I survive."

They paddled into the wider water.

The wind was mild but steady, enough to press Alex's lazy strokes into consequence. Joel had to correct more often. Mike and Will angled left toward a low shore where the map showed the next narrow exit. Will kept looking back toward the marsh.

Joel saw it and did not ask.

The branch crack had gone. The brush had stopped moving. Nothing followed them but small waves and the sound of water dripping from lifted paddles.

Still, for a while no one joked.

That did not last. It never did.

Alex finally said, "For the record, if a beaver kills me, lie."

Will turned in the front of Mike's canoe. "About which part?"

"All of it."

"Say bear?"

"Say moose. Say I fought a moose for the honor of the group."

Mike said, "I'm saying you fell in mud and got unionized."

Alex laughed, and because the water was wider now the laugh carried out from him and came back from nowhere in particular. It skipped once against the low shore and thinned into the marsh behind them.

Joel paddled and watched the lake ahead. He could feel the day going long. Not late yet, but moving that way. The first camp was behind them. The second camp was somewhere past work they had not done.

Mike had rounded the route down. Maybe not by much. Maybe by the exact amount all competent men rounded discomfort down when selling it to themselves first and others second. The lie did not have to be large to matter. It only had to be built into the plan so neatly that no one could point to the moment it entered.

They reached the far side later than Mike wanted and earlier than worry required. The exit was a gap in alder, half-hidden by grass. Water moved through it in a narrow brown pull, just enough current to show the way.

Will lifted his rod and looked at the widening water behind them.

Mike said, "No."

"I didn't say anything."

"You thought it loudly."

"There could be pike."

"There could be time."

Alex looked toward the alder gap. "Does it open up after this?"

Mike gave him a hard look.

Alex smiled. "Asking for morale."

Mike cleared his throat. The sound was small, almost lost under the wind and water.

"After the next stretch," he said. "Just keep moving."

Will glanced at Joel. Not alarmed. Just registering the phrase the way he registered all of Mike's route talk.

Joel dipped his paddle and followed the first canoe into the gap.

The alder closed around them. The light narrowed. Water slid under the boats with a dark, steady pull, and from somewhere ahead came the faint sound of it dropping over stones.

Not danger. Not yet.

Just the route, doing what routes did after men had described them too easily.


# Chapter 6 - The Rough Carry

The alder gap did open, but not into relief.

It let them out into a run of water broken by stone. The current gathered itself in narrow places and slipped fast over gravel, then loosened into brown pools where foam collected against grass. The boats scraped twice. Mike raised a hand from the back of his canoe and pointed them left, then right, then left again, as if the channel were a sentence he was reading aloud one word at a time.

Beyond the run, the water widened.

The lake did not look large on the map. Nothing about it had looked large when they were drinking coffee at Will's kitchen table three weeks earlier, Mike tracing the route with one thick finger while Will corrected scale and Alex asked whether there would be fish he could morally refuse to eat. On paper, this lake had been one more pale shape between black lines. In front of them it took the wind cleanly from the west and turned it into long dark strips.

Mike let his canoe drift just short of the opening. Will had the map case braced across his knees, one hand on it as if paper could blow away even through plastic.

Mike cleared his throat.

It was a small sound, almost nothing, but Joel had learned to hear the decision inside it. Mike made that sound before he merged into traffic, before he told a waitress the bill was wrong, before he moved a canoe against other people's judgment. The throat cleared. Then the world received the plan.

"We cross close to shore," Mike said. "No hero lines."

"I was saving my hero line for dinner," Alex said.

No one gave him much. The wind had already reached into the channel and found the front of Joel's canoe.

Will looked down at the map. "Camp is after the portage on the north side."

"Correct."

"How bad is the portage?"

"It exists."

"That is not a measurement."

"It's a portage."

"Also not a measurement."

Mike gave him a flat look over one shoulder. "Do you want me to say it's charming?"

"I want you to say if it's a half mile of polite dirt or a mile of murder roots."

"Those are not standardized units," Alex said.

"Thank you, Alex."

"I'm pro-science."

Mike pointed with his paddle. "We go now. We don't sit in the wind talking about vocabulary."

That was reasonable. It was also Mike. Those two facts often arrived together in a way that made arguing feel indulgent. Joel set his paddle low and said, "Ready?"

Alex rolled his shoulders. His sleeves were still damp from the beaver work, and there was a mud line dried along one forearm like a cuff. "Seattle prepared me emotionally for this."

"Seattle prepared you to complain in layers."

"Exactly. Emotional layering."

"Paddle."

"Yes, Dad."

Joel did not answer that, partly because the wind took the canoe then and partly because fatherhood, even as a joke, belonged to some other circle of men. Men with strollers in clean parks. Men whose weekends had been absorbed by soccer schedules and pediatric urgent care and a thousand ordinary duties Joel had only watched from the edges. He was engaged. He and his fiancee had talked about children in the careful half-specific way people used when they wanted a future but did not want to set a trap for it. Maybe, maybe not. Someday, if. The old words for a possible life.

Here the future had no grammar. Here there was only the next stroke, the next gust, Alex in front of him dipping too hard on one side, the canoe wanting to turn its face to the wind.

"Left a little," Joel said.

"My left or boat left?"

"The boat's left is your left."

"That's comforting in a limited way."

"Just a little."

Alex adjusted. He was better when there was something to fight. In easy water he let the paddle wander and turned his head toward every remark he wanted to make. In wind he became more useful, almost solemn. He leaned into the strokes. His shoulders worked under the PFD. Spray came over the front and darkened his sleeves again, making the morning's mud soften and run.

Ahead, Mike and Will angled along the lee side of the shore where the waves had less distance to build. Even there the lake slapped at the hulls. The spruce on the western shore bent all one way. Clouds had thickened in that direction, not storm clouds yet, just low gray freight moving over the trees.

"How much farther?" Alex asked.

"Across," Joel said.

"Great. Precise."

"Do you want a number?"

"Would it help?"

"No."

"Then I want a better lie."

Joel smiled despite himself. "Fifteen minutes."

"See? That feels like care."

It took longer than fifteen. It took the kind of time that made the body stop counting and begin bargaining in small pieces. Get to the leaning pine. Get to the rock with the white streak. Get past the opening where the wind came harder. Joel corrected and corrected again. Alex's paddle rose too high when he tired, scattering water back into Joel's lap.

"Lower," Joel said.

"I know."

"Then lower knowingly."

"You have a very managerial tone for a passenger."

"I'm in the back."

"Emotionally a passenger."

Mike called something from ahead. The wind tore the first half away.

"What?" Will called back, though Mike was three feet behind him.

"Stay in. Don't cut across."

"I wasn't cutting across."

"I know what your boat looks like when you're thinking about it."

Will said something that looked from a distance like a correction but did not carry. His hand moved in the air, two fingers and a little chop. Then he bent back to paddling.

The lake had no interest in their argument. It let them move, resisted them evenly, accepted the blades and gave nothing back except distance in stubborn increments. Joel had always liked that about water when he trusted it: the absence of persuasion. Water did not care what story men told about themselves. It took shape from wind, rock, depth, and pressure. It did not become kinder because someone had planned well.

The far shore came closer. Alder overhung a dark notch where the portage landing should be. The waves were smaller there but messier, coming off the rock and crossing themselves. Mike brought his canoe in first and badly. The front scraped sideways on slick stone. Will stepped out too soon, planted one sandal, and sat down hard in two inches of water with a slap that made Alex bark out a laugh before he could stop himself.

"Grace," Mike said.

"Physics," Will said, sitting there with both hands braced behind him.

Alex lifted his paddle like a microphone. "Ladies and gentlemen, the aquatic dignity of Will."

"Don't," Will said.

Alex did it anyway, because of course he did. He put one hand to his chest and announced to the trees, "A man. A map. A complete loss to surface conditions."

"Alex," Joel said.

"What? This is how legends happen."

Will pushed himself up, wet from hip to calf on one side. "If you make that a story, I will put a leech in your food."

"My food has standards."

"Your food is peanut butter noodles."

"Exactly. Standards."

Joel steered toward the same sloping rock, already irritated, already preparing to tell Alex to get out cleanly and hold the boat instead of turning the landing into a stage. The irritation was ready before Alex had earned it. That was the private unfairness Joel kept discovering in himself. Alex had made himself easy to judge, and Joel had taken the convenience.

"Front out," Joel said.

Alex put the paddle down, caught the painter line, and stepped into the water without flourish. His first foot slipped, but he recovered with a hard little hop and planted himself against the boat. "Hold up."

Joel held.

Alex bent, got both hands on the line, and took the canoe's weight before the next wave could turn it sideways. He did not look back to see if anyone had noticed. Joel stepped out, water cold over his ankles, and together they dragged the canoe high enough that it stopped knocking.

Will came back for his pack, trying to act dry enough to preserve authority. One of the shoulder straps had twisted and cinched wrong, the buckle buried under the load.

"Here," Alex said.

"I have it."

"You have it badly."

"That's different from needing help."

"Everything is different from everything if you talk long enough. Turn around."

Will stood there a second, suspicious on principle. Then he turned. Alex loosened the strap, fed it flat, tugged it through the buckle, and thumped the pack once with the heel of his hand.

"There. Try not to drown in the forest."

Will rolled his shoulders. The pack sat better. "That was almost useful."

"I contain multitudes."

"You contain crackers."

"Also multitudes of crackers."

Joel looked away before Alex could catch him looking. He had wanted the clean pleasure of annoyance and had been handed evidence against it. That was Alex's worst habit, maybe: not the performing, not the needling, but the way some small honest usefulness would appear inside it and make judgment feel stupid. A man could be exhausting and still good. A man could turn a landing into theater and then fix a friend's strap without asking for credit. The two facts did not cancel. They made a shape harder to carry.

The portage began behind the alder as a low track through wet grass. For twenty yards it seemed almost reasonable. Then the ground rose into spruce and rock, and the trail changed its mind.

They unloaded in heat trapped under trees. The wind that had bullied them across the lake did not enter the portage except as a high sound in the spruce tops. Down on the trail the air was close and damp. Mosquitoes found the sweat under collars and behind ears. Packs came out. Rods were lashed awkwardly. Paddles were collected and redistributed. Mike stood over the gear and assigned loads with the rough confidence of a man moving familiar pieces.

"Double carry," Will said.

"We can single if we don't baby it," Mike said.

"We have two canoes, four packs, food barrel, loose crap, rods, paddles, and your antique map collection."

"We can single."

"We can injure someone single."

"The trail's not that long."

Will stared at him. "Based on?"

"Based on me looking at the map."

"That's not a trail report."

"It is literally a map."

"Maps are documents of optimism."

"You're a document of something."

Alex lifted the food barrel an inch, set it down, and made a face. "This barrel has been eating at night."

"You're carrying paddles," Mike said.

"I feel seen."

Joel looked at the load and then at the trail. The canoes had to go. The packs had to go. The only question was how many times the same strip of mud and root would receive them. He had no desire to single carry badly for the pride of it. He also had no desire to spend an hour ferrying loads while daylight thinned. The day had already begun to lean away from them.

"We do the canoes and packs first," Joel said. "Come back for barrel and loose stuff if it sucks."

Mike glanced at him. Joel had entered the route argument, which was not his usual place.

"It won't suck," Mike said.

"Then the second trip will be scenic."

Alex pointed at Joel. "Madison with the wisdom of compromise."

"Don't reward him," Will said. "He'll form a committee."

Mike looked from the trail to the gear. His jaw worked once. Then he nodded. "Fine. Canoes and main packs. Quick."

This was how they accepted compromise without naming it. They pretended it had been the plan.

Mike took his canoe first. He bent under it, found the yoke, and lifted in one practiced motion that made the hull rise over him like a dull green roof. Will shouldered his pack and gathered rods. Joel took the second canoe. The weight settled across his shoulders, familiar and still unpleasant. A canoe was light until it was not. The first fifty steps always lied. Then the yoke found the same tender place on both shoulders and began teaching.

Alex took a pack and the bundle of paddles. "Look at me," he said. "A support class."

"Less talking," Mike called from under his canoe.

"More supporting."

"Walk."

The trail climbed.

At first Joel could see only the pale mud between his sandals, the black roots shining through it, the swinging tips of Alex's paddles ahead. Then the canoe shifted and lowered his world to a thin green strip at his feet. The underside of the hull held the sound close: his breath, the knock of a thwart against a branch, the creak of pack straps from the men ahead, mosquitoes whining in the trapped air beneath the canoe. The forest became fragments. Root. Stone. Mud. Alex's heel slipping. Will's rod tube tapping his pack. Mike's voice somewhere farther ahead.

"Step left. Rock's loose."

The warning came back down the line. Will repeated it. Alex said, "Left rock bad," which was not the same warning but did enough.

Joel stepped left.

The trail did not improve. It went up because some older route had decided that up was better than swamp, then crossed exposed granite where boots and sandals had polished the lichen slick. Blowdown forced them into the brush twice. The first tree could be stepped over with care. The second lay at an angle across the trail, too high to step over cleanly under a canoe and too low to duck under without scraping the hull.

Mike, ahead, handled it with profanity and a sideways crouch. Will talked himself through it in short technical pieces.

"Angle. Don't put weight on that root. That's not stable. No, no, this is stupid."

"You need help?" Mike said.

"I need civil engineering."

Alex waited on the far side and looked back at Joel. "You good?"

"Not elegantly."

"Brand consistency."

Joel lowered one end of the canoe enough to see. The hull scraped bark. He backed up, shifted right, tried again. The yoke bit into one shoulder. His sandal slid in mud and caught. For a second the canoe wanted to twist him off the trail. Alex stepped in and got one hand on the front thwart.

"Hold up."

"I am."

"No, hold up with intention."

"Say something useful."

"Tilt right."

Joel tilted right. Alex lifted the nose just enough. The canoe cleared the blowdown and came through with a hollow clack against a branch that made something in the brush go still.

Not silent. Still.

Joel stood on the far side, breathing hard. The canoe pressed his shoulders. A fly landed on his cheek and walked there.

From behind him came Will's cough.

Small. Dry. Almost a laugh but not a laugh. The sound he made when a correction had jammed itself in his throat and wanted out.

Joel stopped.

He knew immediately what it was. He also knew where Will was: ahead of him, beyond Alex and the blown-down tree, bent under his pack beside Mike. Joel had seen him there seconds earlier.

The cough had come from behind.

The body did not believe in interpretation. It believed in direction. Joel turned as much as the canoe allowed and saw the trail dropping away behind them through spruce trunks and alder, empty except for wet roots and the place where their sandals had torn the mud. No person. No other canoe. No bright pack. The lake was hidden below the rise, though wind came up from it in pieces.

"What?" Alex said.

He was twenty feet ahead now, one hand still on the paddles, looking not frightened but inconvenienced by Joel's pause.

"Nothing."

"That's a classic horror answer."

"Bug."

"A bug coughed?"

Joel looked at him.

Alex lifted both hands as much as the paddles allowed. "I'm just saying, large bugs out here."

Ahead, Will coughed.

Real. Visible. He had stopped beside Mike and bent his head, one fist near his mouth. The same sound, or close enough that the difference had no place to live. Then Will called, "You guys coming?"

Mike said, "Keep moving."

Alex looked at Joel. "See? The forest has customer service."

Joel started walking.

There were explanations, and because there were explanations Joel used them. Sound bent under a canoe. Sound bounced off rock. Will might have coughed ahead and the hull might have held it and thrown it behind him. A bird might make a dry click almost like a cough. There might be another group on the lake, though they had seen no boats and heard no voices. Fear was a tool that made duplicates. It took one fact and printed another beside it.

He told himself all this while the trail climbed.

The second half of the portage was worse because it looked better. The grade eased, but the tread vanished into wet moss and black mud. Where the trail crossed low places, old sticks had been thrown down by previous travelers, making a narrow corduroy that rolled underfoot. Pack straps creaked. Someone's buckle clicked over and over, regular as a clock. The canoe shifted with each step and made Joel's neck ache. Sweat ran down his spine and cooled when the wind found gaps between the trees.

At a small rise they stopped.

Not because Mike called a rest. Because everyone reached the same private limit at almost the same time and pretended the clearing had requested them. The trees opened enough to show water through black trunks on the other side, another lake or a long widening of the channel, dark under the west wind. The trail continued down through spruce toward it.

Joel lowered the canoe carefully, first to his thighs, then to the ground. The release of weight felt less like relief than proof of damage. His shoulders pulsed where the yoke had been. He stretched his fingers. They had gone numb around nothing.

Will dropped his pack and immediately checked his rod tips.

"If you say one thing about fishing," Mike said, "I will break that rod and make you carry both pieces."

"I wasn't going to say anything."

"You were looking at the water."

"Looking is not fishing."

"For you it is foreplay."

Alex made a wounded sound. "Gentlemen, I'm trying to recover spiritually."

"Recover quieter," Mike said.

Mike had the older map out before he drank water. Not the newer waterproof printout with clean labels and confidence, but the older folded sheet he liked because it had more contour lines, more little marks, more of the suggestion that somebody had once known the interior intimately enough to draw it. It was soft at the folds despite the map case. He put one thumb against their route and another farther inland.

Will leaned over. "We're here?"

"Here." Mike tapped.

"Or here."

"No. Here."

"Those are not as different as your tone implies."

"They are if you know how to read a map."

"Jesus, listen to this guy," Alex said, but tiredly, with less theater in it than usual.

Joel drank from his bottle. The water tasted faintly of plastic and iodine from some old trip, though the bottle had been washed many times since. He watched Mike's thumb move to a tiny square inland beyond the next camp, connected to the route by a thin broken line that might have been trail, old road, boundary, or wish.

"Old maintenance cabin," Alex said. "Is that a cabin cabin or a map lie?"

Mike did not look up. "Map says there's a cabin."

The phrase came out with the flat sturdiness Mike gave to things he wanted settled. Map says. There it was. Ink as witness. The world reduced to a document and then treated as obedient to it.

Will gave his dry half laugh, which became a cough at the end. "Map says there was a cabin when somebody cared enough to print this."

"Still a cabin."

"Could be a square of moss."

"Could be useful if weather comes in."

"Useful how? Does it have a phone? A road? A concierge?"

"It may be on an old maintenance trace."

"May be is doing a lot of unpaid labor."

Mike folded the map back just enough to keep Will from breathing on it. "If the wind stacks up tomorrow, or somebody twists an ankle, we know there is a feature inland."

"A feature," Will said.

"Yes."

"That's different from a plan."

"It is not the plan. It is information."

Joel looked from the printed square to the forest around them. Human marks did not feel permanent here. They felt like requests. A cabin was a promise people made to themselves about shelter, labor, authority, return. Then the roof went soft. The trail vanished. A tree fell through the wall. Moss took the steps. The map kept the square because paper aged more slowly than wood.

Alex crouched beside the packs, elbows on his knees. For once he did not fill the pause. His face had gone slack with fatigue, and the absence of performance made him look older, not old exactly but closer to the age they all were when no one was reflecting them back. He noticed Joel looking and immediately raised his eyebrows.

"You coming or writing a poem?"

"Drinking water."

"Poem about water."

"Very short poem."

"Let's hear it."

"No."

"Needs work."

Mike capped his bottle. "Five minutes."

"That was not five minutes," Will said.

"It will have been."

They laughed a little, because the wording was stupid and because stopping too long would let their bodies understand what had been done to them. Joel checked his phone out of habit and found nothing. No service, no surprise. The battery was still high because airplane mode had made the device into a clock and camera and dead little mirror. He saw his own face darkly in the glass: sweat, stubble, one mosquito smear near his jaw. Behind his reflection, spruce needles and sky.

He put the phone away.

The second trip back for the remaining gear was not scenic.

They left the canoes and two packs at the rise, marked by nothing because there was no one else to take them, and went back down for the food barrel, the smaller bags, the paddles Alex had grown tired of carrying, and the loose indignities of travel. Without the canoe Joel could see too much. The trail looked worse in full view. Every root seemed placed to prove intention was not required for malice. Mud held the shape of their first passage, heel marks filling with black water. At the blowdown, bark showed fresh scratches from the hulls.

Halfway down, something cracked off to the right.

All four men stopped. Not dramatically. Just the same small halt, bodies arriving at caution before conversation.

The crack was followed by a brushy movement low in the spruce and alder, quick enough to be an animal and brief enough to be nothing.

Will whispered, "You guys hear that?"

"Yes," Mike said.

"That's the second one today."

"Second what?"

"Animal in brush."

"We are in a forest."

"I'm aware of the setting."

Alex looked toward the sound, no smile on his face. The joking Alaska part of him had gone quiet. The actual Alaska, or whatever part of him had learned to listen before naming, stood in its place.

"Probably not large," he said.

"Probably," Mike said.

"I don't love that word."

"You don't have to love it. You have to pick up the barrel."

The brush did not move again. Joel watched the place until leaves became only leaves. There were many reasons for brush to move. Hare. Grouse. Squirrel. Wind arriving late at ground level. A branch settling after their passage. The mind wanted an actor. The woods supplied motion and withheld the rest.

They retrieved the gear.

On the climb back, the food barrel took turns punishing each of them. Mike carried it first and made no complaint, which made the load look lighter than it was. Will took it next and immediately began describing improved harness designs no one had asked for. Alex carried it after that and accused the group of packing "emotional ballast." Joel carried it the last stretch, one hand on the barrel strap, the other on a paddle bundle, the weight knocking against his thigh until a bruise began to announce itself.

At the rise, they loaded again. Nobody said lunch. Lunch had happened in the boats, in pieces, hours ago. Nobody said second camp as if naming it would make it closer. The afternoon had moved from warm to heavy. The light under the trees had flattened, and the brightness over the water beyond the trail had taken on the dull pewter look that came before evening.

The descent to the next water was steep in short sections, then muddy where springs crossed the trail. Mike went first with a pack. Will followed with rods and one loose bag held away from his body like it contained evidence. Alex took the paddles and the smaller dry bag. Joel lifted the canoe again.

His shoulders objected before the yoke settled. He made a sound through his nose and stood.

"You good?" Alex asked.

"Yes."

"That was a married yes."

"I'm not married yet."

"Practicing."

Joel adjusted his grip. "Walk."

Alex walked.

The trail narrowed between black spruce. Roots came across it in hard ribs. Once Joel's sandal slid and he had to take three quick steps under the canoe to keep from going down. The hull clipped a sapling and sprang it back. Somewhere ahead Mike called, "Rock on the right."

Will repeated, "Rock on the right."

Alex said, "Rock on the right, but spiritually."

The phrase came down the line and thinned out. Joel stepped left to avoid the rock, then right to avoid a mud hole. He could hear water ahead now: not lake chop, not the wind-crossed slap from the larger water, but a narrow sound, channel water moving through stones or over a lip. Second Camp was supposed to be near a constricted channel or marsh edge. Sparse sites, Mike had said. Good enough sites, Mike had also said, which Joel had learned meant sites that required agreement from people too tired to object.

The trail leveled. Light showed between trunks. Joel smelled mud before he saw water.

Then, behind him, from uptrail, came a soft click.

Not a branch. Not quite.

A buckle, maybe. A pack strap settling. One hard plastic tooth touching another.

Joel turned his head and the canoe turned with him, awkward and slow. The trail behind was empty. It rose between spruce trunks, already dark in the spaces where the afternoon could not get in. He stood still, listening. His breath filled the hull. His heartbeat moved in his ears. There was only wind high in the trees and the small water ahead.

"Joel?" Alex called from below.

He faced forward again. "Coming."

"Or writing a poem?"

The old joke irritated him less this time. Maybe because Alex sounded tired. Maybe because the question, repeated at a distance on a narrow trail, made the space behind Joel feel briefly inhabited by all the ordinary things that could have made that click: the remaining pack, a loose strap, a branch springing back, their own passage settling after them. The woods kept becoming articulate for half a second and then refusing responsibility.

"Neither," Joel said. "I'm suffering plainly."

Alex grinned up at him from the lower trail. "That's the brand."

They reached the water late.

The put-in was a bad one, mud and angled grass with a black channel sliding past. The lake beyond was not much of a lake, more a widening where water gathered itself before squeezing between alder again. Across it, past a ragged spit of spruce, a small break in the trees suggested the campsite. Or suggested a place where a campsite might be forced by need.

Mike stood with one hand on his hip, looking at the sky as if the sky had failed to meet posted expectations. Will set the rods down with exaggerated care. Alex dropped the paddles and sat on a wet log without checking it first. No one mocked him. That was how tired they were.

"We still crossing?" Will asked.

Mike looked at the distance, the wind, the darkening water. He cleared his throat, but this time no plan came out right away.

That pause did more than any admission would have done.

"It's right there," Mike said at last.

"Right there in daylight," Will said.

"We have daylight."

"We have the concept of daylight."

Alex leaned back on his hands. "I vote for any plan that ends with me horizontal before I become a labor issue."

Mike ignored him and looked at Joel. Joel disliked being pulled into the authority of a decision Mike still wanted to own.

"We can make that," Joel said. "If that's the camp."

"It's the camp," Mike said.

Will picked up the map case, then let it hang at his side. He was too tired to open it.

They loaded without ceremony. The crossing was short and ugly. Wind came down the channel in gusts and pushed them sideways. The canoes rode low with damp gear and tired men. Alex paddled hard in bursts, then stopped too long, then corrected when Joel told him to. Mike and Will led across, their boat dark against the dull water. The ragged spit of spruce slid past on their left. A raven called once from somewhere inland, harsh and unimpressed.

Halfway across, a hollow knock came from behind them.

Joel looked back. Their wake loosened in the wind. The far shore they had left was already a dark line, alder and spruce and mud. No boat. No paddle. Nothing moving except water against grass.

"What?" Alex said, without turning.

"Paddle," Joel said.

Alex paddled.

The knock did not repeat.

They came in at the far side one at a time. Mike and Will first, then Joel and Alex, noses of the canoes sliding into mud under overhanging alder. The landing smelled of wet leaves, old ash, and marsh. Mosquitoes lifted from the grass as if the ground had exhaled them. Back from the water, through spruce trunks and brush, Joel could see a small opening and the gray stones of a fire ring.

Second Camp.

No one said thank God. They were not that kind of men, or not where the others could hear. They pulled the boats higher and set packs in a line above the mud. The day had taken longer than Mike had promised and longer than Joel had privately allowed. It had not broken anything yet. It had only spent them down and placed them here, at a camp with poor sightlines and close brush and water talking quietly behind it.

Mike stood at the edge of the opening, breathing through his nose.

For once, he did not immediately tell them it was fine.

Then the wind moved in the spruce tops, and the mosquitoes found their faces, and work became the only available answer.


# Chapter 7 - Second Camp

Second Camp was not a campsite so much as a place where other people had once lost the argument.

The landing was mud under grass. The first canoe nosed in and stopped as if the ground had taken it by the mouth. Mike stepped out, sank to the top of one sandal, and said, "This works," with no space between the evidence and the verdict.

Will, still kneeling in the front of the boat, looked at the bank. Alder leaned over it in wet green ropes. Black spruce crowded the higher ground. A narrow path went up through flattened grass toward a dim opening where someone had made a fire ring out of dark stones.

"For what?" Will said.

"For sleeping."

"For malaria research."

"Wrong continent," Alex said from the other canoe.

Will pointed at him without turning. "Thank you. I feel corrected and still bitten."

Mosquitoes came up from the grass as if the landing had been saving them. They found wrists, necks, the corners of eyes. Joel slapped one on the back of his hand and left a thin red comma there. His shoulders kept the shape of the portage straps after the pack was gone. His thighs felt used up in a way that belonged less to pain than to debt. The day had spent them and then handed them this.

Behind them, the small channel moved blackly between the alder stems. It was not wide, but it sounded wider than it was. Water slid against grass, then clicked around stones somewhere out of sight. The sound came and went as the wind moved in the tops of the spruce. Now close. Now farther away. Now behind him, though it had not moved.

Joel looked back at the water.

"You're doing it again," Alex said.

"Doing what?"

"The haunted real estate appraisal."

"I'm admiring the lot."

"Needs a detached garage."

"Needs less marsh."

"Marsh is a feature," Mike said. He was pulling his canoe higher by the painter line, heels digging black cuts into the mud.

"Marsh is where features go to die," Will said.

They unloaded because unloading was what came next. That was the mercy of trips like this, before the mercy ended: the body could be given orders. Pull the boat higher. Put packs above the wet line. Take the rods away from where they could be stepped on. Find the stove bag. Find the filter. Find a place to sleep. The work did not require anyone to decide whether the site was bad. It only required hands.

The flat ground above the landing was smaller than it had looked from the water. One tent would fit near the fire ring if its corners were staked into roots and old ash. The second could be forced between two spruce where the ground rose and fell like something breathing. Joel's hammock needed two trees far enough apart and not already claimed by brush. There were jack pines at the back of camp, but their trunks were narrow, and one of them bent when he put his weight into the strap.

He stepped back and watched it.

"What?" Alex said, coming up behind him with a dry bag under one arm.

"I don't love this tree."

Alex put a palm on the trunk and gave it a shake. The pine flexed and returned. "Tree says it has concerns about you too."

"It bends."

"People bend. Trees bend. Men in their forties bend and make sounds they used to mock."

"I am not sleeping under your philosophy."

"It's your funeral hammock."

Joel looked at him.

Alex held up both hands. "Bad phrasing. Withdrawn."

He said it lightly, but he did withdraw it. That was another unfair thing about Alex. He pushed until someone looked at him with actual resistance, and then sometimes he stepped back without needing applause for the decency. Joel looped the strap higher on the trunk and tested it again. The pine bent, but not as much. It would hold or it would not. The day had reduced standards.

Mike set his tent body down near the fire ring. "Will, get the food up before dark."

Will stood with the map case still strapped to the outside of his pack. "I love when you say my name and an unpleasant task in the same breath."

"You want to sleep with the food?"

"Depends what we're calling food."

"Everything Alex won't eat."

"So most of it."

Alex had opened the blue food barrel and was looking down into it with the grave expression of a man reading medical results. "There is a pouch in here called Moroccan vegetable medley."

"That's mine," Will said.

"I assumed. It has the moral loneliness of your cooking."

"It has spices."

"Spices are how suspicious men hide texture."

Mike took the barrel lid from him and snapped it back on. The latch made a small hard click, bright in the close camp. Joel heard it once from Mike's hand and then once again from the brush behind the fire ring.

Not a repeat. Probably not.

A twig, maybe. A strap buckle under a pack. A branch shifting after the wind passed above it.

Joel turned.

The spruce behind camp stood too close for looking. Their lower branches were dead and gray, crossed in layers. Beyond them, alder and shadow. No road, no line of sight, no reason for the sound to have gone there except that sounds in tight places went where they wanted.

"You good?" Will asked.

Joel faced camp again. "Yeah."

"That was a weird yeah."

"Tired yeah."

"There are subcategories."

"Go hang food."

"Authoritarian yeah," Will said, but he shouldered the rope bag.

The food tree problem took fifteen minutes and made all of them worse. The branches near camp were either too low, too dead, too close to trunks, or too snarled with smaller growth to throw a line cleanly. Will selected a spruce with one living arm that reached out over the brush and began performing a system of knots and rocks and whispered correction. The first throw hit the trunk and dropped straight down. The second caught a dead branch and brought down a rain of brown needles. The third sailed over the right limb but carried the rope into alder, where it tangled.

"Technique," Mike said.

"Physics," Will said.

"Technique is applied physics."

"That's the kind of sentence that makes people leave clubs."

Alex sat on a pack and slapped mosquitoes off his calves. "I support Will's right to fail publicly."

"You could help," Mike said.

"I am help. Morale counts."

"Morale weighs nothing."

"Then it portages beautifully."

Joel took the rope from Will without asking and worked it loose from the alder. His fingers had gone clumsy from fatigue. The line was damp and gritty. When he threw, he underthrew, and the rock bag swung back at his shin.

Alex barked a laugh. It came out hard and delighted, then stopped when no one joined.

"Sorry," he said. "That was objectively funny."

"It hit me," Joel said.

"That's part of the objectivity."

Mike took the rope. "Watch."

His first throw cleared the limb. He did not smile. He only handed the free end to Will as if the world had briefly remembered hierarchy.

"Beautiful," Will said. "I hope the branch snaps."

"Tie it."

Will tied it. The barrel went up awkwardly, swinging once into the trunk with a hollow plastic thump. The sound moved through camp and came back softer from the channel. Joel looked toward the water again. The channel held evening in pieces: black water, green alder, a pale strip of sky caught between leaves. Nothing else.

They put the tents where they would fit. Mike and Will took the one near the fire ring after three minutes of argument about slope, roots, and whether condensation was a solvable problem or a moral condition. Alex claimed the lumpy second site with the casual martyrdom of a man who expected witnesses.

"I get the bad ground," he said.

"You volunteered by standing on it," Mike said.

"That's how colonization starts."

"Set up your tent."

"I'm just saying, history is happening."

Will coughed into his sleeve. A dry little cough, half laugh, half tick. "You are one mosquito away from becoming unbearable."

"I passed unbearable at the last portage. This is transcendence."

Joel clipped his hammock suspension and pulled the fabric from its sack. The nylon made its soft sliding sound, intimate and domestic in the darkening trees. He found himself wanting to set up carefully, to do one task well enough that the day could be answered. The first strap was too low. He moved it. The second tree flexed. He moved that too and lost the angle. Mosquitoes landed on his forehead. Sweat and repellent had made a bitter paste at the edge of his lips.

At First Camp, the water had been visible from everywhere. It had made the campsite feel held open. Here the camp was a room with no walls and too many doors. Brush pressed in on three sides. The channel, barely visible through alder, seemed less like an edge than a way in.

He pulled the hammock tight and sat in it. The pine bent, complained without sound, and held.

"Not dead," Alex said from across camp.

"Your concern is moving."

"It's mostly curiosity."

"Also moving."

Mike broke deadfall for a small fire. He used the hatchet sparingly, more for pressure than chopping, setting one boot on the wood and snapping what he could by hand. The hatchet made a flat knock each time it bit. Once, after a knock, another knock came from the channel side. The second was smaller, like a paddle touching a gunwale.

Will, who was kneeling over the stove, stopped with one hand on the fuel canister. "You guys hear that?"

"Hear what?" Mike said.

"Knock."

"Me."

"No. After you."

"Echo."

"Hatchets don't echo off marsh."

"Everything echoes off something if you need it to."

"That's not how that works."

Mike gave him a look. "Please explain acoustics to the forest."

Will opened his mouth, reconsidered, and closed it. The restraint looked painful.

Alex leaned toward Joel. "He's growing."

"He'll relapse," Joel said.

"I'm right here," Will said.

"See?"

The stove took three clicks before the flame caught. Each click sounded too neat under the trees. Blue fire trembled beneath the pot. Joel filled bottles at the channel while Alex held the filter hose and commented on the smell.

"This shoreline smells like a wet dog had a legal dispute."

"That's just marsh," Joel said.

"Then marsh needs counsel."
