Joel balanced one foot on a root and lowered the intake into the water away from the mud he had stirred. The channel looked still until he watched it closely. Then he could see the current pulling bits of grass and foam under the alder. A white moth lifted from the bank and vanished into spruce shade. Mosquitoes whined in his ears. Somewhere downstream a frog made one low sound and stopped.

Alex crouched beside him, quiet for almost half a minute.

"You ever miss it?" Alex said.

"Marsh?"

"No. This." He moved his hand vaguely, taking in water, bugs, twilight, the stupid labor of staying alive in comfort-adjacent ways. "The annual misery project."

Joel looked at him. Alex's face had lost the firelight performance because they were not at the fire. In the last of the open sky his eyes looked tired and ordinary.

"Sometimes while I'm doing it, no," Joel said.

"That's an honest Yelp review."

"Afterward, yes."

"Yeah," Alex said.

The filter gave its small patient ticking as water moved into the bottle. Joel waited for Alex to turn the answer into a joke. He did not.

Then Mike called, "Water?"

Alex raised his voice. "We're discussing brand identity."

"Bring the water."

"Authority is so needy."

The moment closed. Maybe it had never opened much. Joel handed him a full bottle and took the next empty one. The small exchange left him irritated in a different way. He had wanted Alex to keep joking so his irritation could remain clean. Instead Alex had, for a second, stepped out of the role Joel had assigned him. It made Joel feel ungenerous, which was worse than feeling annoyed.

Dinner was rice, chicken packets, Will's vegetable pouch, and the peanut butter noodles Alex made with the solemnity of a private religion. Steam lifted from the pots and held the smell of salt, garlic powder, fuel, and wet wool. They ate sitting on packs, rocks, and the exposed roots around the fire. No one bothered with plates longer than necessary. The sun went behind the trees before it went down, and the camp entered an early, local dark.

Will tried to defend his vegetable pouch.

"It's not bad," he said.

"It's warm compost," Mike said.

"You didn't even try it."

"I saw it."

"Sight is not taste."

"Sight is how taste warns you."

Alex stirred his noodles. "I respect Will's commitment to making dinner feel like a graduate seminar."

"Your dinner is peanut butter and starch."

"Exactly. It knows what it is."

"It knows nothing. It is paste."

"A humble paste."

Joel ate what was handed to him and let their voices move around the fire. He was too tired to contribute much. The old rhythm still worked, even here. That was what made the night feel briefly worse. Their talk did not prove safety. It only proved that men could reproduce themselves in bad conditions. They could assemble the shape of friendship anywhere: around a kitchen island, in a motel room, on a granite shelf, in a dark little camp where the brush stood close enough to take the heat off their backs.

Mike checked his phone once, holding it low as if embarrassed by the gesture. The screen lit his face blue. He turned slightly from the fire and waited.

"Anything?" Will said.

"No."

"Because phones famously love marsh basins."

"I know why."

"I'm just saying."

"Then don't."

Mike put the phone away. Joel noticed that he did not make a joke about it. Earlier, at the take-out, Mike had checked service with annoyance, as if towers were another thing the world owed him for planning correctly. Here he checked like a man touching a pocket where a key should be. The absence was expected and still became information.

"We're fine," Mike said.

No one had asked. That made the sentence heavier than it needed to be.

Will scraped his spoon around his pot. "We're tired. That's different."

"Fine and tired overlap."

"Lots of bad things overlap."

"Jesus, listen to this guy," Alex said, but gently. "One questionable dinner and he's writing a systems paper on doom."

Will pointed the spoon at him. "Doom has inputs."

"Doom has vegetables, apparently."

Joel laughed. It surprised him. Not because the joke deserved it, but because the day had left some last small piece of him available to be moved. Alex caught the laugh and smiled into his noodles without looking up. That was one of his needs too, Joel thought: not only to perform, but to know the performance had landed. The thought came with an unkindness Joel regretted before it was done forming. Why shouldn't a man want proof he had reached the people he had come this far to be with?

Dark thickened. The fire stayed small. The wood was damp enough to smoke but dry enough to burn reluctantly, making little red tunnels in itself. Sparks rose and vanished under the spruce boughs. Beyond the ring of light, the brush took every outline and kept it.

Alex told the thunderstorm story because no one had enough energy to stop him.

"Different trip," he said, pointing his spoon at Mike. "Different river. Same prophet of weather. This guy is standing there in his little hat like he's personally filed paperwork with the atmosphere."

"I said it was moving north," Mike said.

"You said it with force. That's the important part. You said, and I quote, 'That's moving north.'"

"It was moving north."

"It hit us in the face from the west."

"Storm systems rotate."

Will sat forward. "No, no, no, don't give him rotation. He will live there forever."

"Weather has multiple directions," Mike said.

Alex slapped his knee. "Weather had your number. Weather looked at your map and said, 'I don't work for this man.'"

"I got us off the water."

"After the sky turned green."

"Greenish."

"There were children in Nebraska pointing at our cloud."

Will laughed and coughed into his sleeve. Joel laughed too, because he remembered parts of it: Mike shouting over rain, Alex holding a tarp like a doomed sail, Will insisting lightning distance was being calculated incorrectly by everyone but him. Joel had been younger then by only a few years, but the memory had the false distance of another life. The men in it had stronger knees and less money worry in their faces. Or maybe memory always edited the body kindly until grief or fear restored the costs.

"Best part," Alex said, warming now, "is Mike yelling, 'It's just wind,' while a branch comes down right behind him."

"Small branch," Mike said.

"Tree attempted murder."

"Tree missed."

"And we've let you keep navigating."

"Because you enjoy living."

"Debatable."

The fire snapped. Not loud. A resin pocket, maybe. At the same moment something knocked once from the channel.

The talk stopped cleanly enough that the absence of it became its own sound.

Joel looked toward the water. The canoes were pulled high on the bank, their noses angled away from the channel, painter lines tied off around a spruce root. They were not touching. No wave reached them. No paddle lay where water could lift it.

Mosquitoes whined. The stove, cooling, ticked once. Somewhere behind the camp the food barrel creaked faintly on its rope.

"Boat settling," Mike said.

"Boat is on shore," Will said.

"Then shore settling."

"Shore is famously restless."

"A branch," Joel said.

He said it because it was plausible and because plausible things needed saying before other things tried to enter the space. Branch. Animal. Rope. Water. Echo. Camp gear cooling. The world had a large vocabulary of ordinary causes.

Alex looked at him across the fire. "Madison with the emotional support branch."

"Take what you can get."

"I will."

Will picked up his headlamp and angled it toward the landing. The beam showed alder leaves, mud, the side of Joel's canoe, grass flattened by their feet. Nothing moved. Will kept the light there too long.

"Don't burn your battery staring at shrubbery," Mike said.

"Shrubbery made a noise."

"Everything made a noise."

"That's less comforting than you think."

Mike poked the fire with a short stick. "Eat. Clean up. Sleep."

"The old sequence," Alex said. "Civilization in three commands."

They cleaned up slowly. Spoons were licked, then rinsed. Pots were wiped with a scrap of paper towel that disintegrated before anyone wanted it to. Food trash went into the odor bag. Will lowered the barrel just enough to add the night's waste and leftover packets, then hauled it up again with small irritated grunts.

The latch clicked under his hand.

From the brush behind Joel came a second click.

He froze with one hand on the pot.

This time Will heard it too. Joel saw him turn, not toward the sound exactly, but toward Joel turning toward it.

"What?" Mike said.

No one answered fast enough.

Alex filled the gap. "If it's a bear, tell it we have Moroccan vegetable medley."

"Bears have standards," Will said, but his voice had changed.

Mike stood. "Enough."

He took his headlamp and walked to the back of camp, not far, only to the first screen of spruce. He shone the light between trunks, then left, then right. The beam caught old needles, root, a dead branch polished by old weather. It caught two pale moths and made them into white flecks. It caught nothing with eyes.

"It's camp noise," Mike said.

"Camp noise from outside camp," Will said.

"We're in the woods. Outside camp starts everywhere."

Mike came back and sat down harder than he needed to.

Joel listened past the fire. That was the mistake, if it was a mistake. Listening made the dark productive. It brought forward the small brush sounds, the water sounds, the high movement of wind. It made every ordinary noise audition for meaning. He could hear Alex breathing through his nose as he tied off his tent guyline. He could hear Will's spoon scraping one last time, though dinner was finished. He could hear Mike swallow. He could hear his own pulse inside the tender places where the pack straps had ridden.

First Camp had been open enough to let sound leave. Here sound went into the brush and came back handled.

Joel thought of the old maintenance cabin Mike had pointed to on the map, the tiny square inland, the promise that something human had once stood in all this and been useful. A cabin on a map, a fire ring in a bad camp, old foil in ash: marks people made against being swallowed by distance. The marks lasted longer than the reasons for them. Then other people came along and treated the marks as permission.

"You okay?" Alex asked.

Joel realized he had been staring at the fire ring.

"Yeah."

"Another subcategory?"

"Long day yeah."

"That's the premium tier."

"Costs extra."

"Everything does."

The line landed flatter than Alex likely meant it to. Mike looked at the fire. Will looked away. Money was not a clean subject with Mike around, or work around Will. The old friendships were full of doors like that: ordinary sentences that opened onto rooms no one wanted to enter at night.

Alex seemed to notice the drop and disliked it. "Okay," he said, pushing himself up. "I'm taking a piss. Nobody panic."

"Stay close," Mike said.

Alex spread his arms. "See? Panic already."

"Close," Mike said.

"Quit mothering me."

"Headlamp," Joel said.

"I have one."

"On your head?"

Alex touched his forehead, found nothing, and stood there for half a second with his mouth open.

Will made a small delighted sound. "Documented."

"This is harassment," Alex said.

"This is care," Joel said.

"Care is harassment with better marketing."

He came back for the lamp from the top of his pack. The strap had twisted. He untangled it with more force than required and settled it on his forehead. When he clicked it on, the beam jumped across Joel's face, then Will's, then Mike's boots, then the brush.

"Don't piss on anything we're eating," Will said.

"I make no promises."

"Or the food rope."

"Now you're limiting me artistically."

"Alex," Mike said.

"Close. I heard the sermon."

He walked toward the channel side instead of the back brush, stepping through the uneven edge where grass met alder. He did not go far. Ten yards, maybe less. His headlamp marked him in pieces: white on leaves, white on his shoulder, white on the mud at his feet. Then alder broke the beam and made it appear and disappear as he moved.

Joel watched longer than he needed to.

"He's fine," Mike said.

Joel looked at him.

"What?"

"Nothing."

"He's right there."

"I know."

Mike seemed ready to say more, then did not. The restraint had the feel of fatigue rather than tact. He went back to the fire and used the same stick to push the ends of two small logs inward. Sparks rose and died. Will lowered himself onto a pack, then immediately stood again because something under it had shifted. He swore softly and adjusted the straps.

From beyond the alder came the small ordinary sound of a man pissing in the woods. It should have made the camp feel normal. Instead, because they all heard it and pretended not to, it made the boundary of camp more exact. Here were the men by the fire, not looking. There was Alex past the last agreed-upon edge, also pretending he had not gone beyond anything.

"Tomorrow," Will said, because he could not leave silence alone, "we need to get moving early if the wind's still west."

Mike exhaled through his nose. "It'll lay down overnight."

"Maybe."

"It will."

"Weather has multiple directions," Alex called from the dark.

"Shut up and finish," Mike called back.

Alex laughed, not the full bark, but a shorter version made private by distance. The alder caught it and thinned it. For a second, after the laugh ended, Joel thought he heard the shape of it continue beyond Alex, farther down the channel.

Not the laugh itself. The afterimage of it.

He looked toward the water.

"What now?" Will said.

"Nothing."

"That's becoming a genre."

Joel almost told him. Then he did not, because saying it would make it more than it was. A sound could be made larger by description. Fear was bad at scale. It took what fit in the hand and built a room around it.

The fire settled lower. Wind moved in the spruce tops but did not come down to the ground. The camp smelled of smoke, damp nylon, bug spray, old ash, marsh water, sweat cooling in synthetic shirts. Joel rubbed one thumb along the edge of his spoon and felt a nick in the metal he had never noticed before.

Alex's headlamp turned toward the channel.

The motion was small, but Joel caught it because he was already watching the breaks of light through the alder. The beam had been pointed down. Then it lifted and fixed on something farther out, past the place Alex should have needed to look.

"Alex?" Joel said.

His voice came out lower than he intended.

Alex did not answer him. The beam stayed still. Then it moved right, slowly, following.

From the channel edge came a small sound.

Not a knock this time. Not the barrel latch. Not water. A branch touched another branch and stopped. It was the kind of sound a person made when he was trying not to make one, except no person had any business there.

Will stood partway up. "You guys hear that?"

Mike turned his head. "Alex."

The headlamp swung back toward camp for a second. Joel saw the white oval of Alex's face through leaves, amused and irritated, already forming the answer that would bring the moment back into the old shape.

"What?" Alex said.


# Chapter 8 - Nobody Panic

For a second the camp held its shape.

Mike by the fire with the stick still in his hand. Will half risen from the pack, one knee bent, his headlamp strap loose around his neck because he had taken it off to save battery and then forgotten he had done so. Joel standing with his spoon in his hand, the nicked edge pressed into the pad of his thumb. Beyond the alder, Alex's headlamp made a small white wound in the brush.

Alex had just said, "What?"

It had come back to them in his ordinary voice, irritated more than afraid, the voice he used when someone had interrupted a story or questioned a minor competence. The word should have ended the moment. It should have been followed by a joke, a complaint, a report of a raccoon or a branch or his own stupidity. Alex was good at restoring ordinary weather around himself. He could make a bad landing into a bit, a missed throw into evidence of conspiracy, a mosquito bite into a civic failure. The group had relied on that more than Joel wanted to admit.

But the headlamp did not turn fully back toward camp.

It held low and slightly right, aimed into the dark beyond the place where Alex stood. The light touched alder leaves, moved over them, and stopped on nothing Joel could see.

"Alex?" Joel said.

The camp heard his voice and did not answer it.

Then Alex said, "Hold up."

Not loud. Not theatrical. The way he had said it at the beaver dams when mud kept one sandal, the way he had said it on the rough portage when a pack strap caught on blowdown. Practical. Annoyed. Expecting the world to wait because he had found a small problem inside it.

Mike stood all at once. "Come back in."

Alex did not answer.

"Alex," Mike said, sharper.

Will fumbled at his headlamp. The plastic housing clicked against his collarbone. "What is it?"

"Shut up," Mike said.

"I'm asking what--"

"Shut up and get your light."

Joel had not moved. He saw that before he felt it. His body remained in camp while his attention had gone through the alder and stopped beside Alex. The spoon was still in his hand. He put it down on the nearest pack too carefully, as if a louder movement might change what waited out there.

Alex's beam jerked left.

"What the hell," Alex said.

Brush moved.

At first the sound was small enough to stay ordinary. A branch scraping another branch. A foot in wet grass. Something pushing through alder with less care than a person and more care than wind. Then the brush gave way in a heavy rush, not from one point but along a short line of darkness, as if the edge of the camp had been taken in both hands and shaken.

Alex shouted once. Not a word. A raw, insulted burst of air.

Mike grabbed for the bear spray.

The canister had been set upright beside his pack after dinner, part of the normal theater of caution. At First Camp it had seemed like an object that proved they were responsible. Here it looked suddenly small in Mike's fist. He ripped at the safety with his thumb and started toward the channel side.

"Back," he said.

No one went back.

Joel moved because Mike moved. Later he would not know whether he had decided to follow or had been pulled by the sight of another man acting. He took two steps, then stopped and turned for something, anything. His hand closed around the nearest paddle, the bent-shaft one Alex had been using all day because he liked the look of it and because Will said it made no difference for his form.

"Joel," Will said.

"Light," Joel said.

Will had his own lamp on now. The beam shook over the fire, Mike's shoulder, Joel's paddle blade, the wet path to the channel. "Alex!"

"Don't yell," Mike snapped.

"Alex!"

"Will."

The second shout came from beyond the alder.

Alex this time. Or the shape of him under pressure. It started as a word and broke before it reached them. Something hit brush hard. A body hit mud. There was a wet thump, then a tearing sound too brief and too physical for Joel's mind to keep. Alder whipped back and slapped itself. The headlamp beam spun upward, showing leaves from below, then sky, then nothing human at all.

Joel ran.

He struck the first screen of alder shoulder-first. Branches raked his face and arms. His sandal sank past the sole into black muck. The paddle caught across two stems and almost tore from his hand. Mike was ahead of him by two body lengths, broad back hunched, bear spray out, lamp beam jumping. Will came behind making a continuous broken sound, not quite Alex's name anymore, not quite words.

The brush made every distance dishonest. Ten yards became a room with no door. Mike's light opened one narrow piece of it at a time: green leaves turned white, black water between roots, mud polished by movement, a broken stem bright at the fresh split. Joel's own light found Mike's elbow, then lost it. Something crashed to their right. Or behind them. Or across the channel. The sound changed direction in the alder as if the alder had opinions.

"Stop," Mike said.

Joel stopped because the command had found some old trail in him. Will did not. He pushed into Joel's back and nearly knocked him down.

"Where is he?"

"Quiet."

"Where is he, Mike?"

"Quiet."

Something breathed beyond the light.

That was the only word Joel had for it, and even as he thought it he distrusted it. It might have been wind moving through wet leaves. It might have been Alex's breath. It might have been his own breathing made huge by the cup of his hands around the paddle shaft and the blood beating in his ears. It came once, low and close, with a wet catch at the end.

Mike raised the spray.

"Don't," Joel said, too late.

The canister made a hard choking hiss. Orange fog hit the alder and opened into the beam, immediate and chemical, a cloud with weight. It drove back into them as soon as it left the nozzle. Joel inhaled and his throat closed. Fire ran into his eyes. He turned away coughing, blind, spit hanging from his mouth. Will cursed and stumbled into him. Mike kept spraying for another second, then swore hard enough that the word seemed to tear his voice.

"Jesus," Will said. "Jesus, Jesus."

"Move left," Mike said.

"I can't see."

"Then blink."

"That's not--"

"Left."

They moved because standing in one place was worse. Joel wiped at his eyes with the heel of his hand and made the burning worse. His lamp beam became water and color. The alder stems doubled, then slid back into one another. His lungs wanted air and rejected what came.

The smell of spray covered everything for a few seconds. Then another smell came through under it.

Mud. Split green wood. Marsh water. Blood.

Mike stopped so abruptly Joel almost ran into him.

"No," Will said.

He said it once, softly, before he had seen enough. The word had arrived ahead of understanding.

Alex lay ten yards beyond the first screen of alder, in a place that had not looked like a place from camp. There was a narrow shelf of mud between roots, then black water. The alder grew thick around it, trunks no wider than broom handles but multiplied into a wall. Something had cleared a gap through them. Stems were bent flat. Leaves floated in the water with their pale undersides up.

Joel's mind refused Alex at first. It accepted only objects.

One hiking sandal, heel-down in mud. A calf streaked with black muck. A hand open as if asking for something small. The headlamp strap twisted under his cheek, its light pointing sideways into the water. A torn sleeve. Blood on alder leaves, too dark under the lamps. A wet red place where the body had stopped being the private, contained fact of a man.

"No, no, no," Will said.

Now the words had weight. They came out of him as if something was being unloaded too fast.

Mike put out one arm and blocked him.

"Don't touch him."

"He's--"

"Don't touch him."

"We have to--"

"Back up."

Will tried to go around him. Mike caught him by the front of his PFD and shoved him hard enough that Will slipped in the mud and caught himself on a branch.

"What is wrong with you?" Will said.

"Do not touch him."

"He's right there."

"And whatever did it might also be right there."

The sentence entered the brush and made a shape of the dark around them. Joel lifted the paddle with both hands. He did not know what he intended to do with it. Hit a bear. Hit the air. Prove he had hands.

His eyes kept returning to Alex's hand. It had mud in the lines of the palm. One fingernail was torn back. The hand looked separate from the violence because it was still shaped like Alex: broad, careless, capable, the hand that had slapped mosquitoes off his calf and held the filter hose badly and stirred peanut butter noodles with religious seriousness. Joel had spent whole stretches of the day annoyed by the man attached to it. He had measured Alex in performances, in bids for attention, in jokes that asked too much of the room. Now the hand lay open and all of Joel's measurements became obscene.

The woods were very quiet.

Not empty. Quiet. There was water moving under the alder. There were mosquitoes, somehow still alive in the spray and blood, whining thinly around their faces. There were the small internal noises of three men trying to breathe through panic. But the larger woods had withdrawn from them. No frog. No night bird. No branch settling after a disturbance. It was as if every ordinary sound had stepped back to let the scene explain itself.

"Alex," Will said.

Joel almost told him not to say the name. He did not know why. It would have been cruel and useless. He also knew, in some part below reason, that names went farther here than they should.

Mike swept his light past the body and into the brush beyond it. "Hey!"

"Don't," Joel said.

Mike ignored him. "Hey! Get out!"

His voice cracked on the second word. Not fear exactly. Force applied to something that would not take shape.

No bear moved in the beam.

The light showed broken alder, mud gouged into shelves, black water where the channel curled close, a dead spruce branch with old gray lichen on it. It showed one dark smear low on a trunk. It showed something that might have been a print near the waterline and then was only a collapsed place in mud when Joel looked at it longer. It showed nothing with eyes. Nothing retreating. Nothing large enough to have done what had been done.

Then, to their left, brush ticked once.

All three lights went there.

Nothing.

Mike sprayed again, a short burst this time, reckless and furious. The cloud opened and fell. Joel turned his face away before it reached him, but it caught the side of his neck and made the skin burn.

"Stop wasting it," Will said.

Mike rounded on him. "You want to save it?"

"I want to see."

"See what?"

"Anything."

"It's a bear."

The word sounded prepared. Not because Mike had known, but because it was the only box large enough and close enough to put the scene inside. Bear. The route had bears. They had bear spray. They had hung food badly in a bad camp near water and brush. They had known the word before they needed it. A thing named bear could be reported, managed, feared, survived. A bear belonged to the world.

Joel looked again at Alex's body and tried to make the word fit over it.

It fit in places. Violence. Strength. Blood. Mud. The smashed alder. The speed of it. The terrible plain fact that large animals could do more damage to a human body than humans liked to remember.

It did not fit everywhere.

The food barrel was back at camp, still hanging. Alex had been standing. There had been no bawl, no roar, no warning huff, no heavy retreat through brush after Mike came at it with lights. The ground near Alex showed confusion but not the certainty of a large animal leaving. Maybe the mud did not keep prints. Maybe the alder hid movement. Maybe Joel knew too little to know what he was seeing. Fear was not expertise.

"Bear," Mike said again.

Will heard the repetition and flinched from it. "Where is it?"

"Gone."

"How do you know?"

"Because we're standing here."

"That's not how that works."

"Will."

"No, don't Will me. Where did it go? It was just--it was just right here."

Mike lifted the canister toward the dark. "It ran."

"We didn't hear it run."

"We heard everything."

"No. We heard brush. We heard--"

"Stop."

Will's mouth stayed open. His face had gone pale and shiny under the headlamp. The lamp tilted down and lit his own hands, which were shaking hard enough that the beam trembled over the mud at his feet.

Joel stepped closer to Alex.

He did it slowly, not out of courage but because his mind needed one more fact and hated him for needing it. The mud sucked at his sandal. He kept the paddle between himself and the brush as if the shape of defense mattered. Mike made a warning sound but did not stop him.

Alex's face was turned partly into the mud.

Joel saw enough to know he should not try to see more. One eye closed. Cheek streaked. Mouth open as if he were about to object to the inconvenience. The headlamp had slipped under him and lit the water, where blood moved in a thin ribbon and separated into the black current. The sight had an intimacy that felt like trespass. Joel backed up one step.

He remembered Alex at the channel earlier, crouched beside him while the filter ticked into the bottle.

You ever miss it?

This.

The annual misery project.

Joel had given him half an honest answer. Afterward, yes. He had not said what else was true: that he missed the men more reliably when he was away from them, when irritation had time to edit itself into affection, when Alex became a story told at a reasonable volume. He had not said that being with them was sometimes harder than loving them from Madison. He had not said that adulthood had made friendship into a thing that needed planning, mileage, vacation time, weather windows, stored gear, staged keys, and the old deliberate lie that if they got together often enough nothing essential had changed.

Now Alex was ten yards outside camp, and every unsaid thing had become permanent because it had not been useful in time.

"We need to do something," Will said.

Mike rubbed at his eyes with his free wrist. "We are doing something."

"No, we're standing here."

"We're making sure it's gone."

"And then what?"

Mike did not answer.

The question went through Joel with a cold practical edge. Then what. Carry Alex? Where? Through alder, mud, two days of route behind them, harder water ahead, no service, no beacon. Leave him? Stay beside him? Sit in this brush until morning with whatever had done it perhaps close enough to hear them breathing?

Will said, "We can't just--"

"I know," Mike said.

"Do you?"

Mike turned his light on Will, not quite at his face. "I know."

There was no comfort in the words. There was only the beginning of an argument too large for the dark.

Something splashed softly at the waterline.

Joel's body answered before thought. The paddle came up. Mike swung his light. Will made a strangled noise and stepped back into Joel's shoulder.

The beam found water moving around a broken branch. A leaf turned over in the current. A bubble rose and broke.

Nothing else.

"Back to camp," Mike said.

"No," Will said.

"Yes."

"We can't leave him."

"We're not leaving him."

"What do you call this?"

"Surviving the next eight hours."

Will stared at him. "Listen to yourself."

"I am."

"Mike--"

"If that bear is still inside fifty yards, you standing here saying his name is not helping Alex."

The cruelty of it worked because it was practical. Will shut his mouth. His chest rose and fell fast. He looked at Joel as if Joel might have another category available, some third thing between abandonment and suicide.

Joel did not.

"We mark where he is," Joel said. His voice came out thin. He cleared his throat and hated the sound. "We go back. We keep lights out except when we need them. We stay together. At first light we come back."

Will shook his head once, hard. "At first light he's still dead."

No one answered.

Mike crouched without turning his back fully to the brush and picked up Alex's headlamp by the strap. Joel almost told him to leave it. Then he saw the light lying under Alex's cheek, shining stupidly into the water, and understood that Mike needed to do one small task that could be completed. Mike switched it off. Darkness took back the water immediately.

"Don't take his stuff," Will said.

"It's a light."

"It's his."

"Will."

"Don't say my name like that."

Mike held the lamp out to Joel, who took it because someone had to. It was slick. He did not look at what made it slick. He clipped it to the front of his own PFD, then unclipped it again because that felt unbearable, and carried it in his fist.

They backed out of the alder.

No one wanted to turn first. They made an ugly sideways retreat, lights pointing into too many places, feet slipping in mud. Branches caught on straps and sleeves. Once Will stumbled, and Mike grabbed his arm, and Will yanked away as if the help itself accused him. Joel kept the paddle across his body and listened for the sound of something following. Every scrape they made became answerable by another scrape. Every branch they bent sprang back with a small wet tick that sounded, for half a second, deliberate.

When they broke through the alder screen into camp, the fire had settled into red pockets under ash. The tents stood open-mouthed and thin, nylon walls holding the small warmth of men who were no longer inside them. The food barrel hung pale and round in the back of camp, absurd in its correctness. The canoes lay high on the bank. The pots were stacked. The water bottles stood where Joel had left them. Their camp looked abandoned by people who had intended to come back in a minute.

Will stopped at the edge of the firelight and bent over with his hands on his knees. He retched once but nothing came up.

Mike went straight to the packs. "Spray. Where's the second can?"

"Alex had it," Joel said.

The sentence broke something in the air. He had not meant to say had.

Mike looked toward the alder.

"I'm not going back for it," Will said.

"Nobody asked you."

"I'm saying it."

"Stop saying things."

"Don't tell me to--"

"Both of you," Joel said.

It surprised them enough to work. Mike turned. Will straightened halfway. Joel stood with Alex's dead headlamp in one hand and the paddle in the other and felt ridiculous and old and utterly unqualified.

"We need the boats farther up," he said. "Then we sit where we can see the channel and the back brush. No tents."

"No tents?" Will said.

"If something comes through one, I don't want to unzip my way out."

Will almost laughed. It came out as one hard breath.

Mike nodded once. "Canoes."

Work saved them because work did not ask what it meant. They hauled the canoes another few feet from the mud line, too close to camp now but better than the channel. Joel and Mike turned one sideways near the fire ring as a poor barrier, though all of them knew it would stop nothing determined. Will gathered paddles and laid them within reach. Then he moved them again because he did not like the first arrangement. He made a row of them along a log as if order could become defense.

Mike checked the food barrel line. It was still up. The latch was closed. He yanked the rope once and the barrel swung, knocking softly against the trunk.

All three men froze.

The knock came back from the channel side, smaller.

No one spoke.

Joel could not tell whether it was echo, memory, the barrel hitting twice, or something else answering with the exact vocabulary of their camp. His eyes burned from spray. His throat tasted like metal and pepper. He wanted to say branch, or barrel, or nothing. He did not. Words were beginning to feel less like explanations than offerings.

Mike lowered the barrel until it stopped moving. He tied the rope off again with fingers that missed the first turn.

"It's an echo," Will said.

His voice sounded angry at the mercy he had given them.

"Yeah," Mike said.

The agreement was too fast.

They put on every layer within reach. Fleece over damp shirts. Rain jackets zipped to the throat. Hats pulled low. Joel's hands shook when he tried to work the zipper. He looked at the pull and forgot what order fingers used. Will reached over and did it for him without comment, then turned away before the kindness could become visible.

Mike tried his phone.

He did it facing slightly away from them, as if the old embarrassment still mattered. The screen lit his face. He held it up, turned once, stepped onto a root, raised it higher. No bars. No service. The blue light showed the flecks of bear spray on his cheek and the wet shine under his eyes. He put the phone away.

"Nothing," Will said.

Mike did not answer.

"Because of course nothing," Will said. "Because why would we have the one piece of equipment that solves this."

"Will."

"No. Don't. This is exactly when people have the thing."

"We don't."

"I am aware."

"Then save it."

Will laughed once, without humor. "Save what?"

Mike sat on a pack near the fire ring and set the bear spray across his knees. "Your battery."

"Great."

"Your air."

"Good."

"Your whatever keeps you from making this worse."

Will stared at him. For a second Joel thought he would cross the small distance and hit Mike. Instead he crouched by the turned canoe and put both hands over his face. His shoulders moved once. Then again. No sound came out. That made it harder to watch than crying would have been.

Joel looked away and saw Alex's tent.

The second tent, the lumpy ground he had claimed with theatrical martyrdom, stood half zipped. One guyline had pulled loose. The door flap moved when the wind reached the camp, lifting and dropping with a small nylon whisper. Inside would be his sleeping bag, his dry clothes, the pouch of bland food he had guarded from Will's spices, the paperback he had been unlikely to read, his spare socks, maybe the little bag of electrolyte tablets he had insisted were superior to everyone else's. An inventory of a man who had assumed morning.

Joel turned from it.

"We should get his pack," Will said, still crouched.

"No," Mike said.

"Not from out there. From the tent."

Mike looked at the tent. "In the morning."

"It's right there."

"In the morning."

"You think the bear cares about his socks?"

"I think if we start sorting a dead man's gear right now, you are going to come apart."

Will lifted his head. His face was wet and furious. "Don't diagnose me."

"Then stop acting diagnosable."

"Jesus Christ, Mike."

"Enough." Joel heard his own voice and recognized the thinness in it. "Enough for tonight."

They sat under the tarp because it was the only structure that let them see out. The tarp had been pitched low against possible rain and now made them hunch. Water from old condensation or fog gathered along one seam and fell at intervals onto the dirt. Tick. Tick. Then a longer pause. Then tick again. The sound made time without moving it forward.

They killed the fire after a short argument. Mike wanted light. Joel wanted not to sit in a lit room for whatever stood outside it. Will said both options were bad in different, specific ways until Mike told him to pick one or be quiet. Will picked quiet and made it look like injury.

When the last flame died, the world became depthless.

Headlamps stayed off except for brief checks. In darkness the camp rebuilt itself out of sound. The channel spoke first: water under alder, small and black. Then the food barrel rope, tightening and easing as wind moved above. Then nylon from Alex's tent. Then someone's swallow. Then Mike's thumb, once, on the bear spray safety. The click was tiny and enormous.

Joel sat with the paddle across his lap. He had washed his hands twice from a water bottle and could still smell blood where the cracked plastic of Alex's lamp had marked his palm. Maybe it was not there. Maybe the smell had entered the whole camp. He kept rubbing his thumb against the seam of his pants.

Will whispered, "What if he was still--"

"No," Mike said.

"You don't know what I'm going to say."

"Yes, I do."

"What if he was still alive when we--"

"No."

"You don't know."

"Will."

"Don't say my name."

"He was gone."

The words sat between them. Joel wished Mike had not said them. He wished someone else would say they were true. He wished truth had a sound you could identify in the dark.

Will's breathing changed. "You can't know that."

Joel looked toward the place where Will was only a darker shape beside the canoe. "He was gone."

It cost him less than it cost Will to hear it. That made it worse.

For a while no one spoke.

Joel tried not to listen. The effort made him listen harder. His mind sorted sound into columns without permission: water, rope, nylon, breath, branch. Ordinary. Ordinary. Ordinary. Maybe ordinary. He thought of the motel wall, the way he had lain awake and matched each noise to a source until he could sleep. Bathroom fan. Door latch. Alex laughing through plaster. Mike's cough or Will's. The exercise had seemed like a way to settle the world. Now it felt like practice for a test that had changed its rules.

A branch cracked beyond the channel.

Will inhaled.

Mike lifted the spray.

Joel held up one hand, though neither man could see the gesture well in the dark. He waited. The crack did not repeat. Wind moved in the spruce tops and arrived at camp a few seconds later in pieces. A dead twig dropped somewhere behind them.

"Branch," Joel whispered.

He hated himself for needing the word and hated the word for being useful.

Later, maybe minutes later or an hour, something moved near Alex's tent. Nylon touched nylon. Joel turned his lamp on for one second. The beam showed the loose flap settling, the guyline slack, no animal, no man. He turned it off before the light could travel farther and find the alder.

"Wind," Mike said.

"I know," Joel said.

Will said nothing.

The night became a series of decisions not to move. Do not check the body. Do not check the sound by the channel. Do not retrieve Alex's spray. Do not open his tent. Do not ask whether anyone else heard the small almost-voice that might have been water under a root. Do not say his name more than necessary. Do not let the dark make an argument and win it.

Joel's legs cramped. His back tightened. The cold worked upward from the ground through the soles of his sandals and into his knees. Mosquitoes found the gap between his cuff and glove. The bear spray burn faded from flame to raw skin. He pressed his tongue against the roof of his mouth until the taste of pepper changed to blood from a bitten place there.

Will began talking again near what might have been midnight.

Not loudly. Not even to them at first. He listed facts under his breath, the way he might list gear before a portage.

"Bad food hang. Marsh camp. Dusk movement. He stepped away. We heard brush. Spray cloud. Body ten yards out. Bear country. This is bear country."

Mike said, "Stop."

"I'm just saying what happened."

"You're making yourself worse."

"I am trying to keep sequence."

"Keep it in your head."

"My head is currently not a trusted environment."

Joel almost laughed. The sound rose in him and stopped at his throat, not because it was funny but because it was Will, impossibly Will, still able to make a sentence that was both precise and unbearable. He looked toward the shape of him in the dark and felt an affection so sudden it hurt.

"Sequence doesn't save us tonight," Joel said.

Will was quiet for a few breaths. "It might tomorrow."

"Tomorrow," Mike said, with the hard relief of a man taking possession of a word. "At first light we assess. We mark the site. We pack. We leave."

"Leave which way?" Will said.

"Out."

"Thank you. Very detailed route plan."

"Not now."

"It's not a small question."

"I said not now."

Will let out one dry, broken cough. The sound went into the dark above them and seemed to come back from the brush behind the fire ring, thinner and later.

Joel turned his head.

Nothing.

He looked at Will. Will was still beside the canoe, one hand over his mouth as if ashamed of the cough. Mike had not moved. Maybe neither of them had heard the return. Maybe there had been no return. Maybe Joel's ears, burned and strained and full of blood, had made a double out of one sound.

He said nothing.

The silence after that felt less like restraint than debt.

Near dawn the cold deepened. The black in the trees thinned by degrees without becoming light. The channel showed itself first as a dull strip. Then the turned canoe. Then the hanging food barrel. Then the three packs they had pulled close and never opened. Shapes came back into the world reluctantly, each one bringing its own accusation.

No one had slept. They had leaned, shifted, jerked awake from half-dozes, and returned to listening.

Mike stood when there was enough gray to see his hands. His face had changed in the night. It had not softened. It had set around something. He picked up the bear spray, tested the weight of it, and looked toward the alder screen.

Will remained seated. "Don't."

Mike said, "We have to."

Joel stood too. His knees made small private sounds. He took the hatchet this time instead of the paddle, then looked at it and understood there was no correct object. A hatchet was only a smaller tool in a night that had made tools ridiculous, but it had weight and an edge, and his hands accepted it.

Will looked at both of them as if they had betrayed him by becoming able to move.

"At first light," Mike said.

Joel wanted to tell him this was not light. It was only the end of dark. But the distinction belonged to a world that still cared about precision. He clipped his lamp to his head and did not turn it on.

Will picked up the map case before he followed them. No one asked why. Joel understood anyway. A map was another thing with lines on it. Another thing that promised the world could be reduced to marks and distances if a man held it hard enough.

Together, three men walked toward the place where the fourth had become a fact they could not yet name.


# Chapter 9 - Bear Work

Morning did not make Alex easier to see. It only made the details insist on themselves.

For a while there was no sunrise, only the dark thinning into usable gray. Fog sat low in the channel and did not move like weather. It lay over the black water in a torn sheet, caught in the alder and the spruce roots, and the far bank came and went inside it. The campsite smelled of wet ash, bear spray, blood, mud, crushed alder, and the sour human fear that had come out of them in the night and dried cold on their shirts.

Nobody had slept. They had leaned under the tarp, then straightened, then jerked out of half dreams when water dripped from the seam or a spruce needle ticked against nylon. The cold had got into the joints of their hands. Their headlamp straps had left marks in their hair. At some point Will had begun shaking without admitting he was cold. At some point Mike had stopped saying anything at all.

When there was enough light to make the world accountable, Mike stood.

"We have to look," he said.

Will did not move. He was still sitting with his back against the turned canoe, knees up, the map case clamped between his hands. He had held it most of the night. The plastic was bent where his thumbs had worried the edge.

"No," he said.

Mike looked toward the alder. His jaw worked once. "Yes."

"You can say that in your little trip-leader voice all you want."

"It isn't a vote."

"Everything is a vote after somebody dies."

Joel stood before Mike could answer. His knees hurt. The night had put ten years into them. He picked up the hatchet from where it lay beside the packs, then looked at it in his hand. It was a camp tool with a nicked orange handle and black pitch dried below the head. It had split kindling two nights ago while Alex complained that nobody appreciated the wood arts. In Joel's grip it became both more serious and more useless.

Will watched him. "You too?"

"We have to know what we can know," Joel said.

The sentence sounded like something from an office, a meeting with a whiteboard and bad coffee, a way to reduce damage to categories. He hated it as soon as he said it. But the other choices were worse. Stand here. Sit here. Listen. Let the brush keep Alex.

Mike took the bear spray. There was less in it now. Joel could tell by the way Mike weighed it without meaning to, the little drop of his wrist. Mike saw Joel see it and looked away.

"Stay close," Mike said.

"Really?" Will said.

Mike's head turned a fraction.

Will's mouth stayed open, but no more came out. He got up slowly, as if every part of him had to be persuaded separately. He still held the map case.

"Leave that," Mike said.

Will looked down at it as if surprised to find it there. "No."

"It won't help."

"Neither will your can of spicy air, apparently."

Mike took one step toward him. Joel said, "Enough."

The word was small, but it landed because none of them had strength for the old shapes yet. Mike stopped. Will tucked the map case under one arm and followed.

They went through the same gap in the alder they had made at night. In daylight, the distance was obscene. Ten yards. Less than the length of two canoes. A man could throw a mug from the fire ring and hit the place where Alex had died. Joel had spent the night making the distance larger because terror needed space to justify itself. Morning took that away.

The alder held the marks of them and of whatever else had moved there. Branches were snapped at shoulder height and knee height. Leaves hung torn and silver-backed. Mud was smeared on stems. The black ground underfoot had been cut by sandals, knees, roots, something broader, something dragged. Bear spray had left orange dust on low leaves. It looked artificial in the gray, like paint from a child's toy.

Joel tried not to step in blood and found that there was no clean path.

Mike stopped first. Will stopped behind Joel with a hard intake of breath.

Alex lay where they had left him.

That fact had its own violence. Joel had known it would be true, but some childish part of him had expected morning to have corrected the scene, to have moved Alex into a form that could be approached. Morning had no such mercy. The body was still half turned toward the water, one arm bent under him, one hand open in the mud as if he had been interrupted while reaching. His shirt was torn and dark. His hiking sandal was gone from one foot. His other sandal was still strapped on, absurdly intact, heel packed with black muck.

Joel kept his eyes on the hand.

It was less terrible than the rest and therefore worse. Mud had dried in the creases of the palm. One fingernail was broken. The fingers were slightly curled. It was recognizably Alex's hand, not a fact from a report, not a casualty, not a body part in the abstract. It was the hand that had snapped a mosquito dead against his calf and then held it up for inspection. The hand that had stirred noodles like a man conducting. The hand that had reached back without being asked to steady Joel's boat over a beaver dam.

"No, no," Will said.

Not the panic from last night. Smaller. A correction addressed to the world.

Mike cleared his throat. The sound was raw from pepper spray. "We need to check the site."

Will stared at him. "The site."

"Will."

"No, say it again. I want to hear how professional you can make it."

"If it was a bear, we need to know if it's still close."

"If?"

Mike's face tightened. "Because you're going to make a federal case out of the word."

"I am looking at our friend dead in the mud and trying to be accurate."

"Accuracy won't move us."

"Inaccuracy got us here."

The sentence hit harder than Will seemed to expect. Mike's eyes changed. For a moment the whole camp, the route, the two vehicles, the old map, the missing emergency device, all of it stood between them with Alex lying at its center.

Joel stepped between the next words before they could happen.

"Tracks first," he said.

Mike looked at him as if he had forgotten Joel was there.

"Tracks," Joel said again. "Then his spray. Then we cover him. Then we decide."

"We decided," Mike said.

"No, we didn't."

Will's head turned toward Joel.

Joel did not look at him. If he did, the relief in Will's face might ask for more than Joel could give.

They began with the ground because the ground at least pretended to be evidence.

The mud made no simple testimony. It took some marks too well and refused others. Where the water had seeped up through root holes, impressions slumped almost as soon as they formed. Where alder roots crossed under the surface, footprints split and slid. Their own night movements were everywhere: Mike's deeper heel, Will's narrow sandal tread, Joel's own scraped sideways step where he had coughed blind through the spray. Alex's tracks came from camp, paused near the edge, turned slightly as if he had shifted his weight, then disappeared into churn.

Beyond that was damage.

Something had crushed a low tunnel through the alder from the channel side. Or from the brush behind the channel. Or from the place where the channel widened and doubled back. Joel could not hold the map of it in his head. He crouched, one hand on a wet root, and studied a broad mark in the mud near Alex's shoulder.

It could have been a paw.

That was the worst of it. It could have been. Wide. Deep at one edge. A dragged smear where claws or roots or a slipping heel had cut through the top layer. He knew too little about bear tracks to argue with the possibility. He knew too much about pattern-making to trust his desire against it.

"There," Mike said.

He pointed with the bear spray, not touching anything.

Will leaned in. "Maybe."

"It's a print."

"Maybe."

"Don't maybe this."

"I'm not maybeing it." Will's voice shook with cold or anger. "I'm saying maybe because that's the correct word when you don't know."

"It is a bear print."

"How many toes?"

Mike stared at him.

"No, answer. How many toes do you see? Because I see mud and panic."

"Will."

"There should be a track line. Something that heavy doesn't do one magic print and levitate out."

"It's all churned up."

"By us. By him. By something. Fine. But look." Will crouched lower, careful not to touch the mud. His hand hovered over a place near the waterline where the ground should have held anything large leaving. "This is nothing."

"Water washed it."

"In six hours?"

"Current."

"Current goes that way."

"Jesus Christ."

"No, this is exactly the conversation."

Mike stepped back, then turned his lightless headlamp toward the brush as if habit could still make a beam. His throat worked. "A bear came into camp. Alex stepped out. It hit him. It left through the channel. That is what happened."

"You left out the part where he said 'What.'"

No one moved.

Fog pulled through the alder behind Alex, thinned, and caught again.

Joel felt the word from last night reopen in him. What. Alex's voice pitched irritated and curious, speaking to something just beyond his light. Not afraid yet. Not performing. Answering.

Mike said, "He heard brush."

"He answered somebody," Will said.

"No."

"You don't get to no that."

"He answered a sound."

"A sound that sounded like somebody."

Mike's face went hard and blank. "You don't know that."

"Neither do you."

Joel looked at the mud. "We heard him say it. That's all."

Will laughed once, a dry little break. "Great. Court-admissible Joel, everybody."

"I mean it," Joel said.

"I know you mean it. That's why it's annoying."

Mike lowered the bear spray. "The more we stand here talking, the less daylight we have."

Daylight. The word had seemed generous from under the tarp. Here it was already being spent. The fog blurred the channel, but above the trees Joel could see the low ceiling of cloud moving faster than he liked. Wind had not reached the ground yet. It worked in the spruce tops first, a high rubbing sound. Later it would find open water.

They found Alex's bear spray six feet from his body, half under leaves, safety still in place.

Mike stared at it for too long.

"He didn't get it off," Will said.

"I can see that."

"I'm saying--"

"I can see it."

Joel picked it up before either of them could use it in the argument. The canister was cold and wet. Mud filled the groove around the safety clip. Alex had carried it clipped to the front of his PFD because Mike had told him twice to stop putting it in the side pocket of his pack.

Quit mothering me, Alex had said the first time, at Will's house, grinning while he changed nothing.

Then, on the first lake, when Mike pointed at the canister with his paddle and told him again, Alex had clipped it where it belonged with great ceremony and said, Look at me growing as a person.

Joel slid the canister into his own jacket pocket. The pocket sagged.

"Don't take his stuff," Will said.

"It's spray," Joel said.

"I know what it is."

"Then don't make me say why."

Will looked away.

There were other objects. Alex's missing sandal lay upside down in the water weeds, where the current worried at the strap. One of his gloves had been mashed into mud. A torn corner of rain jacket hung on a broken alder stem. The cracked headlamp, which Mike had taken the night before, was back at camp in the cookpot where Joel had put it because he could not stand holding it and could not put it with anyone's personal gear. That small inventory became an accusation. Things could be recovered. A man could not.

They did what they could for Alex.

No one called it burial. The ground was root and water and cold stone under a skin of mud. They had no shovel. They had no right tool and no safe time and no strength for a task that would not protect him from anything. Mike brought the spare groundsheet from Alex's tent. Will objected because it was Alex's, then stopped because every object here was Alex's or no one's now.

They unfolded the groundsheet beside him. It snapped softly in the damp air.

"Careful," Mike said.

Will's eyes flashed. "I was planning to be casual."

"I'm saying--"

"Don't."

Joel knelt in the mud. The wet came through the knee of his pants immediately. He took the corner of the sheet. Mike took another. Will stood useless for two breaths, then crouched and took the third, his hand shaking so badly the plastic trembled.

Moving Alex even a little was impossible and necessary.

Joel closed his mind around the work. Corner here. Lift enough to pull cloth. Don't look at the torn place. Don't let the sheet catch under the arm. Don't put weight on the open hand. Mike said something under his breath that might have been a curse or a prayer. Will made a sound and bit it off. The groundsheet slid under part of Alex and bunched under another part and would not do what they needed. They had to try twice.

At last they covered him as well as they could.

The sheet made a poor shape over the body, too low in one place, too high in another. Rainwater already beaded on it. They gathered stones from the channel edge to hold the corners. The stones were cold and slick. Joel chose each one as if choosing correctly mattered. A flat one for the shoulder corner. A heavier round one near the feet. A granite chunk with lichen on it for the side where the wind might get under.

Will set his stone down, then moved it an inch, then moved it back.

"This is insane," he said.

Mike wiped mud from his hand onto his pants. "It's what we can do."

"That sentence should be illegal."

"You have a better one?"

Will looked at the groundsheet. "No."

The admission emptied him. He stepped back and put both hands on the top of his head, fingers locked in his hair. His map case hung from the crook of his elbow. Joel remembered Will at twelve in the church basement, doing the same thing over a lost youth-group scavenger hunt clue, furious that the world would not yield to close reading. Joel had not thought of that room in years: folding chairs, watered-down lemonade, the smell of dust and floor wax, someone's mother telling them to use indoor voices. Will had wanted the clue to be fair. Joel had already suspected that fairness was something adults said when they had run out of patience.

Now Will stood in the alder beside a covered body and still wanted the clue to be fair.

Joel turned away from him before the memory could make a speech out of itself.

Mike took the map case from Will without asking and opened it on a root-wide patch of ground. The plastic crackled. He pointed to their approximate position with a muddy finger.

"We go back," Will said at once.

Mike did not look up. "We don't know that yet."

"We know it."

"Back means the rough portage, the beaver mess, the wind lake from yesterday, all of it against us with three men and two boats."

"Forward means unknown."

"Forward means shorter to the Highlander."

"On paper."

"On the map."

"Those are the same only when the map isn't lying."

Mike's eyes lifted. "The map is not lying."

"Fine. The map is politely omitting."

Joel could see both routes as bodily pain. Back meant passing the beaver dams again, the rough portage, the narrow channel where each ordinary sound was now contaminated by what had followed them into camp. Forward meant wind they had not tested, camps they had not seen, the old cabin mark that might be nothing, Mike's confidence moving ahead of evidence. Both directions required leaving Alex under a groundsheet in the alder.

"We can't carry him," Mike said.

Will's jaw set. "You don't know that."

"I do."

"He's not gear."

"That's why I'm saying it out loud before someone breaks himself trying to prove he loved him enough."

Will stepped toward him. Mike did not step back.

Joel got between them too late to prevent the words but early enough to prevent hands.

"If we try to carry him," Joel said, "we don't get out today. Maybe not tomorrow. We need both boats moving. We need three people able to portage. We need--"

"Stop," Will said.

Joel stopped.

Will's face had gone rigid. "Don't turn him into logistics."

Joel looked at the groundsheet. The stones. The dark water licking at alder roots. "I don't know how else to get us out."

That was the first fully honest thing anyone had said that morning.

No one answered it.

They marked the site. Mike tied a strip torn from Alex's orange stuff sack to an alder above the body, high enough to see from camp and low enough not to look like a flag. Will hated that too. Joel hated that they needed it. He made a note in Will's little waterproof notebook because his own was packed too deep: Second Camp. Body ten yards east/channel side of camp. Covered with gray groundsheet. Orange marker on alder. Date. Approximate time.

His handwriting looked calm. That was another kind of lie.

Will watched the pen move. "Write bear."

Mike looked at him.

"Go ahead," Will said. "If we're committing."

Joel kept the pen above the paper. "Observed possible large animal sign."

Will laughed without humor. "You are the worst kind of useful."

"I know."

"Put no clear tracks."

Mike said, "Don't."

Joel wrote: no clear track line.

Mike breathed through his nose. The sound was almost a snort, almost the beginning of an argument, but it did not become one.

From the brush on the far side of the channel came a low laugh.

Not loud. Not theatrical. It did not roll through the fog like a ghost story. It was a single bark of amusement, cut short in the old familiar way, the sound Alex made when a joke pleased him before he decided whether to make it bigger. A hard little laugh with his chest in it.

Will stopped breathing.

Mike's face emptied.

Joel's hand closed around the pen until the plastic bent.

The fog held still over the water. A cedar waxwing clicked somewhere upstream, small and real. The channel moved under the alder with its ordinary black patience.

"Bird," Mike said.

No one asked which bird.

The laugh did not repeat.

Joel waited for his mind to reject it. Grief, exhaustion, memory, a raven, a fox, water hitting a hollow root, Will making some involuntary sound and Joel assigning it the wrong owner. There were explanations if he reached for them. They lay around the moment like tools laid out on a tarp.

He did not pick one up.

Will said, very softly, "That was him."

"No," Mike said.

"Mike."

"No."

"You heard it."

"I heard an animal."

"You did not."

Mike turned so fast the map case slid off the root. "Then what? Say it. Say the thing you want to say."

Will opened his mouth.

Joel grabbed his sleeve. Not hard. Enough.

Will looked at the hand on him. His eyes were red, the skin below them gray. He understood, or nearly understood, before Joel did. Whatever came next would be heard. Whatever word they gave the morning would enter the woods and belong partly to the woods.

Will shut his mouth.

They stood that way for several seconds: Mike with the bear spray half raised toward fog, Will caught between speech and silence, Joel holding a pen and another man's sleeve beside a covered body.

Then, from closer, from the alder behind the groundsheet or from the channel bend or from nowhere Joel could put on a map, Alex's voice said, "Quit mothering me."

It was soft. Almost fond. Almost annoyed.

The phrase came out with the exact little drop on mothering, the way Alex had said it when warnings bored him but attention pleased him. It was not a scream from beyond death. It was not even a call. It was conversational, as if he were just out of sight adjusting a strap, as if he expected somebody to roll their eyes and let him continue.

Will made a sound like his body had been struck.

Mike sprayed into the alder.

The hiss tore the morning open. Orange fog burst against wet leaves and came back in pieces. Joel pulled Will backward by the sleeve. The spray drifted low and chemical, cutting through mud and blood and spruce. Mike swept the canister left to right, too fast, wasting what little remained, his arm rigid.

"Stop," Joel said.

Mike did not.

"Mike, stop."

The canister coughed and weakened. Mike lowered it. His mouth was open. His eyes streamed, though the spray had gone mostly away from him this time.

Nothing moved in the alder.

No body shifted under the groundsheet. No shape crossed the channel. No bear crashed away. No Alex stood up laughing at the cruelty of it. The woods gave them wet leaves, fog, black water, the cedar waxwing's last small tick before it flew.

Will bent and vomited into the mud. There was little in him. Coffee acid, water, a string of bile. He stayed folded over afterward, one hand braced on a root, the other still holding the map case against his ribs as if it were an organ.

Mike wiped his eyes with the back of his wrist. "We leave now."

His voice was hoarse and flat.

Will spat. "You don't get to say bear anymore."

"I didn't."

"You were about to."

"I said we leave."

"Because what, the bear learned comedy?"

Mike took two steps toward him and stopped with the spray canister hanging useless at his side. His control had not returned. Only the outline of it. "Because staying beside him is worse."

The words were simple enough to stand.

Joel looked at the groundsheet. Rain or fog had gathered on the plastic and begun to run in narrow lines toward the mud. One bead hung at the edge, swelled, fell onto a leaf. The leaf dipped and came back up.

He wanted to say Alex's name. Not to the brush, not as answer, not as lure. Just once, properly, to the covered shape on the ground. He did not. The wanting itself frightened him. It felt like stepping toward a voice.

They went back to camp without turning their backs until the alder forced them to.

The camp in daylight looked worse than it had in darkness because daylight made its inadequacy visible. The tarp sagged with condensation. The fire ring was a black scrape full of wet ash. Alex's tent stood half open, one guyline slack, the fly torn along the lower edge where some branch or body had caught it in the confusion. The turned canoe lay between the fire ring and the channel like a child's idea of a wall. Paddles were lined along a log where Will had placed them during the night, a neat row of useless tools for a crossing no one wanted.

Mike went straight to the food barrel.

"Don't," Will said.

Mike stopped with his hand on the rope. "We need to know."

"We know enough."

"No. We know one thing we can't use."

He lowered the barrel carefully. The rope rasped over the branch. The sound made Joel's shoulders tighten. The barrel descended in little jerks, pale and round, until Mike caught it against his chest and set it on the ground.

The latch was not open, exactly. One tongue had not seated fully into its keeper. The red strap crossed the lid, but the metal tab sat proud by half an inch, enough to make a small clean shadow.

Joel knew Mike had checked it in the night. He had watched him. Mike had lifted the latch, pressed it down, tied the rope, yanked the line once. The barrel had knocked against the trunk, and all three men had listened to the knock come back from the channel side. But he also knew hands failed under pressure. He knew a latch could look closed when it was not. He knew memory edited itself to reduce blame or increase it.

Mike crouched and touched the tab.

"Don't contaminate the evidence," Will said.

Mike gave him a look.

"Sorry," Will said. "That was the wrong genre."

Mike pressed the latch. It clicked home.

All three men looked toward the alder.

Nothing answered.

The relief was not relief. It was only one expected bad thing declining to happen.

Mike opened the barrel and checked the contents. Oatmeal, coffee, noodles, peanut butter, rice packets, Will's spices in doubled bags, Alex's plain rice meals with his name written on duct tape in thick marker because he had claimed otherwise the communists would steal them. Everything was there. Nothing torn. No claw marks in the plastic. No food dragged through camp.

"Food didn't bring it," Will said.

Mike closed the barrel. "Food could have attracted it without being eaten."

"You hear yourself?"

"Yes."

"Do you?"

"Enough."

Joel expected Will to answer. He did not. He was looking at Alex's labeled rice meals through the translucent side of the barrel. His face had lost the argument and found something worse.

Packing was another form of lying. It required belief in later. Later they would need dry socks. Later they would need the stove. Later a map would matter. Later a pot should be nested correctly and fuel capped and straps tightened. The body wanted only to sit down, or run, or go back into the alder and remove the groundsheet and undo what had happened by force of wanting.

They packed anyway.

They did it badly.

Joel poured too much water into the coffee grounds because his hand shook when he meant to stop. Brown liquid climbed the filter cone and spilled over the rim onto the dirt. He stood watching it run past a spruce root until he heard Alex in memory, not from the brush but from yesterday evening by the stove: This is less coffee than a wet opinion. Joel had rolled his eyes then. Now the line arrived whole and useless, and he gripped the mug until heat hurt his fingers.

Will took down Alex's tent.

No one had asked him to. Maybe that was why he did it. He unzipped the door, then stopped with one hand on the pull. The zipper made a small dry line through the morning.

Mike turned sharply.

"It's me," Will said.

The phrase hung there. It should have been ordinary. It had become impossible to say without meaning too much.

Will ducked into the tent and began handing things out: sleeping bag in its stuff sack, a shirt, socks, a paperback swollen slightly from damp, a spare bland-food pouch from the tent pocket, a little bag of electrolyte tablets, a headnet, spare batteries. Each object came out with an afterlife already attached. Joel put them into Alex's pack because no other category existed.

At the bottom of the tent, Will found a small plastic bottle of hot sauce wrapped in a sock. He held it up and made a sound that was almost a laugh.

"He lied," Will said.

Mike looked over.

"Bland food purity was a lie," Will said.

No one answered.

Will screwed the bottle tighter and put it in the pack with unnecessary care.

Joel took the cracked headlamp from the cookpot. Dried blood darkened one side of the strap. Mud had got into the hinge. He could not decide whether to pack it with Alex's gear or keep it out as evidence or throw it into the channel because looking at it made the morning tilt. He wrapped it in a spare bandanna and put it in the top pocket of Alex's pack.

"We take his pack?" Will said.

"Some of it," Mike said.

"What does that mean?"

"It means we can't carry everything."

"I can carry his pack."

"No."

"You don't know what I can carry."

"I know what you can carry over one portage while crying and not sleeping. I know what you can carry over six."

Will's face twisted. "Go to hell."

"Later."

It was the kind of Mike line that would once have worked. Dark, quick, irritatingly timed. No one laughed. Mike seemed to realize this half a second after he said it. His mouth tightened, but apology would have been a different language and none of them had that language ready.

They divided Alex into necessities and evidence and things too personal to abandon.

Necessities: bear spray, fuel, batteries, first-aid odds and ends, one fleece layer, food. Evidence: headlamp, the safety-on spray, a note about where the body was, the orange marker, photographs if they could take them. Things too personal to abandon: wallet sealed in a dry bag, phone with no service, a small notebook with only mileage estimates and grocery lists in it, the hot sauce because Will put it in his own pocket and dared anyone to speak.

Mike took photographs of the site from the camp edge and from inside the alder. He used his phone, then held it up afterward and checked service again though he knew. No bars. No emergency call. The blankness of the screen had become another voice saying the same thing.

"Battery," Joel said.

Mike lowered the phone. "Yeah."

He turned it off.

Will watched him. "We could climb."

"What?"

"Tree. Ridge. Anything. Signal sometimes--"

"There is no ridge."

"You don't know."

"Look around."

"Trees are not a topographic argument."

"We are not climbing wet spruce for one imaginary bar."

"People get emergency calls out with no displayed service."

"People also fall out of trees."

Joel said, "Wind's coming up."

That redirected them because it was outside and therefore harder to accuse.

The spruce tops were moving now. The fog on the channel had begun to tear into strips. Beyond the narrow water, through the screen of alder, the next lake showed only as a pale hard brightness. The surface was not visible from camp, but they could hear it beginning to work: small waves touching rock, then not touching, then touching again. A steady increase. A clock with no face.

Mike went to the map and spread it on the turned canoe. He put stones on two corners. Will came despite himself. Joel stood back, close enough to see but not close enough to become responsible for the route.

"Back to First Camp," Will said.

"Forward to the larger lake, then the marked site if we can make it," Mike said.

"If we can make it is not a plan."

"It is when every plan starts with if."

"The rough portage is behind us. We know it."

"And it took the four of us half the afternoon fresh."

"We were not fresh."

"We were fresher than this."

"Forward gets us to the cabin mark eventually," Will said, jabbing the map with one finger.

Mike looked at him sharply.

"What? That's your thing. Map says there's a cabin."

"Not today."

"Then why forward?"

"Because the take-out is forward."

"And the body is behind us," Will said.

There it was. The real geography.

Mike folded the edge of the map, then flattened it. His hands were steadier with paper than they had been with the spray. "We leave a note at camp. We mark the site. We travel. We get to the Highlander or to service or to people. We bring them back."

"Bring them back to what?"

Mike did not answer.

"No, say that one too," Will said. "Since we're saying practical things."

Joel looked toward the alder. The orange strip was just visible from camp, a small wrong color in the brush.

"We need to move while we still can," Joel said.

Will turned on him. "You keep saying we."

"Because it's still true."

"Is it?"

The question came without heat. That made it harder.

Joel had no answer that would not sound like a poster in a ranger station. Stay together. Keep moving. Don't panic. All the good advice in the world became thin when spoken beside a dead friend.

Will looked down first.

They finished packing. Mike loaded the canoes as if load order could repair reality. He put the heaviest packs low, centered the food barrel, tied the loose painter with too much force. Will repacked the map case twice. Joel checked the stove cap, then checked it again because he could not remember doing it. They left Alex's tent fly folded under a rock with a note in a freezer bag: ALEX B. BODY MARKED CHANNEL SIDE. GEAR PARTIAL. BEAR ATTACK SUSPECTED. NO SERVICE. GOING FOR HELP.

Joel wrote bear attack suspected because leaving the word out felt like cowardice and putting it in felt like fraud. Suspected was the hinge he could live with for the next hour.

Before they pushed off, Will walked to the edge of the alder gap.

Mike said, "Don't."

Will stopped.

The word had worked because it was not an order this time. It was a plea with bark on it.

Will stood with his back to them. "I'm not going in."

No one moved.

"I just--" He cleared his throat, and the small rough sound went into the trees. Joel flinched before he could stop himself. Will heard the flinch. His shoulders rose, then dropped. "I just need a second."

Mike opened his mouth and closed it.

Joel looked at the channel, at the fog taking itself apart, at the water brightening under wind. He understood that leaving would not be a clean action. It would be repeated all day. Every paddle stroke would leave Alex again. Every portage would leave him. Every practical decision that kept the living alive would commit the betrayal one more time.

Will did not say Alex's name. That was what Joel noticed. He stood at the edge of the brush and gave the silence nothing it could use.

Then, from somewhere near the covered body, very soft, came the click of a food barrel latch.

The actual food barrel sat in Mike's canoe, twenty feet behind them, strapped under the middle bar.

Mike turned. Will turned. Joel did not, for one second. He looked at the barrel in the canoe first. Red strap. Metal latch seated. Rope tied. Still.

The click did not repeat.

Will whispered, "No."

Joel said, "Don't answer it."

The words left him before he could weigh them. They were too loud in the morning and still not loud at all.

Mike lifted the mostly spent spray toward the alder, then lowered it. Something in his face had gone older and smaller.

"Boats," he said.

They got the canoes into the water. The hulls scraped mud and then floated free with a soft, living shift. Three men, two boats. The imbalance showed immediately. Mike would take one canoe alone with the food barrel and heavier gear. Joel and Will would take the other. No one said that Alex's absence had a seating arrangement.

Joel stepped into the back of the canoe. Will got in front with the map case between his knees. His paddle lay across his lap for a moment before he picked it up. Mike shoved off first, too hard, then corrected with two quick strokes.

For a few seconds they drifted in the narrow channel beside Second Camp.

The tarp sagged. The fire ring was black. Alex's tent site had become a flat wet patch under spruce. The orange strip in the alder moved once in the wind and then stilled. The groundsheet was hidden from the waterline, which was good and not good enough.

Joel put his paddle in.

The blade entered without splash. The water took the shape of it, closed around it, and let it go.

Behind them, from camp or brush or the channel's crooked mouth, Alex gave one low bark of laughter.

Will's shoulders seized.

Mike said, without turning, "Keep paddling."

No one said bear.


# Chapter 10 - Windbound

They did not leave.

For ten minutes they believed they had. That was the cruel part. The canoes had come loose from the mud. The narrow channel had taken them in its black strip and carried them away from Second Camp one boat length at a time.
