Behind them the tarp, the dead fire, Alex's flattened tent site, and the orange shred in the alder had begun to arrange themselves into memory, which was not mercy but distance. Mike paddled ahead alone, the food barrel low in the middle of his canoe, his shoulders set too high. Will sat in front of Joel with the map case under one knee and his paddle moving in stiff, angry strokes.

No one turned around when Alex laughed from camp.

No one said anything for the first bend. Joel made himself look at the back of Will's rain jacket, at the wet seam running down the hood, at the small pale place on his knuckle where he gripped the paddle too hard. He counted strokes without meaning to. Twelve to the bend. Eight through the slower water. Six where the channel widened and the current hesitated. Counting gave his mind a shape that was not the shape under the gray groundsheet.

The lake showed itself all at once.

It was not large on the map. It had been a neat blue widening between lines, a thing they would cross before choosing the next water, the next portage, the next answer. In front of them it was white at the tips and darker in the troughs, a blunt field of water shouldering east under wind. Gusts came down the length of it without obstruction. They flattened the near grass and tore spray off the farther chop. The opposite shore was visible, then blurred, then visible again, low and black under cloud.

Mike's canoe lifted, dropped, and swung sideways before he corrected.

"Left shore," he called over his shoulder.

The wind took the words and scuffed them flat. Joel heard them anyway. He also heard the little catch under the certainty, the fraction of effort Mike had to spend to make the sentence sound like a plan.

Will did not turn. "No."

Mike dug on his right and angled toward the near point. "We can hug it."

"No."

"Don't start."

Joel held the canoe steady with a backstroke and felt the wind get under the empty front of Mike's boat. That was the problem made visible: three men, two canoes, one of them suddenly wrong. A tandem with one man in it did not become a solo canoe because the map required it. Loaded low, it could be managed on calm water. In this wind it was a long argument with physics.

"Mike," Joel said.

Mike looked back. His face was wet from fog or spray or both. The lake behind him kept lifting.

"Not with the boats like this," Joel said.

"If we stay close to shore--"

"That shore has blowdown all along it."

"So we don't land."

Will laughed once without humor. "Outstanding. Lake too windy to cross, shore too bad to land, plan is don't need either."

"Shut up and paddle."

"That's not a plan either."

A gust hit then, clean and broad. It caught Mike's canoe on the quarter and pushed the bow away from the shore. Mike drove his paddle down hard, corrected, overcorrected, and for one second the hull lay almost broadside to the chop. The canoe did not tip. It only showed them the idea.

Joel's hands tightened on his paddle.

Mike saw it too. He swore and let the boat fall back toward the shelter of the channel mouth.

No one called the decision. The lake called it. They backed out in pieces, Mike first because he had no choice, Joel and Will following because there was no better courage than refusing stupidity. Wind pushed at their shoulders all the way into the channel. The black water there looked protected only because the lake was worse.

Second Camp reappeared around the bend as if it had been waiting.

The tarp sagged where they had left it. The turned ground under spruce was darker than the surrounding needles. The orange strip in the alder moved once and then stopped. Joel did not look for the groundsheet. From the waterline it was hidden. That was good. That was unbearable.

They landed badly. Mike drove his canoe into the mud and stepped out too fast, one foot sinking to the ankle. The boat slid back. He grabbed the painter and hauled until the bow rasped onto roots. Will got out before Joel told him to wait and nearly went down when his sandal caught under a submerged branch.

"Careful," Joel said.

Will rounded on him. "Fantastic note."

Joel let it go.

They dragged the canoes higher than before. Wind had a way of making men believe water wanted things personally. Mike tied his boat to a spruce and then tied it again to a different root, not trusting the first knot once it was made. Joel ran the painter of his and Will's canoe around a low cedar twice. His hands had lost some fine feeling from wet and cold. The rope felt both slick and abrasive.

"We wait an hour," Mike said.

Will stared at the lake mouth. "An hour."

"Wind can lay down."

"Wind can also not do that."

"Thank you, meteorology."

"No, no, no." Will pulled his hood back from his face as if it had insulted him. His hair stood wet against his forehead. "You don't get to do the Mike thing where the bad version is somehow not part of the plan."

Mike stopped coiling rope. "The Mike thing."

"Yes."

"Good. Great. We're naming things now."

"Somebody should."

Joel set his paddle under the canoe seat and straightened. The movement made his back flare. "We wait because we can't cross. That's the whole plan for the next hour."

Will looked at him. The anger in his face had no object sturdy enough to hold it. It moved from Mike to Joel to the channel to the alder and back again.

"And if it doesn't lay down?" he said.

Joel looked at the lake. A line of whitecaps moved from west to east like something being combed hard. "Then we make the next decision."

"That is such a software answer."

"It's a weather answer."

"It's an answer that gets paid by the sprint."

Under other circumstances Joel might have laughed. Will had meant it to wound and to remain a joke in case wounding was too much. That was one of his talents. He could leave himself an exit in the sentence. Joel found he missed the part of him that would have taken the exit.

Mike lifted the map case from the canoe and wiped water from the plastic with his sleeve. "Backtracking is worse."

"You keep saying that like the word worse has one setting," Will said.

"Back is the beaver mess, the rough portage, the lake from yesterday against this same wind, and then another day at least to the truck. With one less person. With him behind us."

Him. Not Alex. The pronoun hung there and failed at kindness.

"Forward is unknown water with a solo boat in wind," Joel said.

Mike looked at him sharply.

Joel did not soften it. "Forward may still be right. It's not right right now."

The practical sentence steadied the air for half a breath. Then Will said, "We could wait for someone."

No one answered too quickly. The thought had entered all three of them and found no food.

"Who?" Mike said at last.

"Another group. Rangers. A plane. I don't know."

"This route doesn't have scheduled patrol."

"You don't know everything this route has."

"I know enough."

Will made the same dry broken sound he had made that morning. "That might be the problem."

Mike turned on him. "You think I wanted this?"

"I think you wanted to be right."

There was no wind under the tarp because the tarp was not up anymore. The sentence arrived bare.

Mike took one step toward him and then stopped. His fists were not closed, but his hands looked ready to become fists. Joel watched the old geometry reassemble: Will verbal and needling because silence hurt, Mike controlling because uncertainty humiliated him, Joel between them because the alternative was worse. Alex would have made a joke by now. Not a good one. Something too loud, something that made everyone turn away from the edge.

The thought of him as useful came too late.

"Phones," Joel said.

Mike blinked once.

"Check them," Joel said. "Here, lake mouth, maybe there is something in the wind. It costs us five minutes."

"There won't be," Mike said.

"Then it costs five minutes to know."

Will was already pulling his phone from the doubled dry bag in his pack. The motion was clumsy. His fingers fumbled the fold twice. He had put the phone away after taking pictures in the morning, and Joel could see he hated touching it now because it had become a device for proving the world was absent.

Mike checked his too. Joel did not have his out. His was sealed deeper, off, saved for later in the way people saved things that could not save them.

The screens lit their wet faces weakly.

No service.

Mike lifted his phone higher, then turned in a slow half circle. The gesture was so common it was almost comic, a man asking a towerless sky for a bar. He stepped onto a root, held the phone above his head, frowned at the blankness, and stepped down.

"Emergency call?" Will said.

"No."

"It can sometimes--"

"It says no service."

"That doesn't mean no emergency network. That's a separate--"

"Will."

"I'm just saying."

"I know."

That stopped Will more effectively than anger would have. He looked down at his own screen. His thumb moved once. Maybe he was checking the time. Maybe the weather cached from days ago. Maybe an old text that had no business in this place. Joel saw his face shift and close.

"Turn them off," Joel said.

Mike did. Will did not, at first.

"Will."

"I know," Will said, and powered it down.

They put the phones away with more care than the objects deserved. The absence of service did not become more dramatic for being confirmed. It simply moved from assumption to fact. No beacon. No satellite messenger. No one listening except whatever the route itself had been listening with.

They re-made the camp they had meant to abandon.

That was the worst work of the day because it was work done against hope. They put the tarp back up over the same miserable center between the fire ring and the canoes. The first line went badly; Mike chose a spruce limb that sagged under tension, and when Joel pointed it out Mike said it was fine, then saw the sag and moved it without admitting anything. Will stood under the empty space where the tarp would be and held two stakes in one hand like he had forgotten their purpose.

"Corner," Joel said.

Will looked at him.

"That corner. Pull it toward the cedar."

Will obeyed. The ordinary task gave him a body again. He took three steps, wrapped the line, unwrapped it because he had crossed it wrong, wrapped it again, then tied a knot too elaborate for the job.

Mike watched the knot. "You building a suspension bridge?"

"Yes, Mike. For the tiny rescue vehicles."

Mike almost smiled. It died before reaching his mouth.

The tarp lifted at last. Wind snapped one loose edge until Joel tied it lower. It would not be comfortable. It would keep the worst rain off the stove and packs if the rain came from the right direction. It made a roof over their refusal to leave.

The body remained beyond the alder.

Joel had been trying not to frame the camp around that fact and failing. Every position was measured by it. The stove was eight yards from the gap. The boats were twenty. The food barrel, re-strapped after the morning's check, sat under the canoe for the moment and would need to be hung again if they stayed. The orange marker was visible only from one angle near the shore. It moved when the wind touched it, then vanished into leaves. The covered place itself could not be seen. Not seeing it did not remove it. It made the mind draw it.

Will kept looking at the gap.

"Don't," Mike said.

Will's eyes stayed there. "I didn't do anything."

"Don't start doing it."

"What, looking?"

"Standing there like you're about to go in."

"I'm not."

"Good."

"Don't good me."

Mike pressed the heels of his hands into his eyes. When he took them away, he seemed smaller and angrier for having shown tiredness. "We can't spend all day at the edge."

"We can spend it in the center?" Will said. "Is the center good? Did the center put in an application?"

Joel crouched by the stove and checked the fuel bottle. He had already checked it. He checked it again because the motion delayed speech. The bottle was capped. The pump was clean enough. They had not eaten since coffee and the failed oats. Hunger had become part of the fear, a flat weak place under the ribs. He could make rice. He could make noodles. Every food category had been touched by Alex now. His labeled bland meals in the barrel. His hot sauce in Will's pocket. His jokes stored in their heads where the thing could reach them if they let them out.

"We should eat," Joel said.

"You're hungry?" Will said.

"No."

"Then why--"

"Because we need to function."

Will opened his mouth, closed it, and looked away. Mike got the barrel without comment.

The latch made its small metal click when Mike opened it.

All three men froze.

It was the real latch. Under Mike's hand. At their feet. Joel saw the tab lift. He saw the red strap slacken. He saw cause and sound joined cleanly. His body did not care at first. It heard the click and threw him back to the alder, to the impossible click by the covered body, to the food barrel sitting in the canoe while another latch spoke from the brush.

Mike saw it in both of them and hated them for making him see himself.

"That was me," he said.

"We know," Joel said.

"Do you?"

Joel met his eyes. "I saw it."

That mattered. He did not know why yet. But the sentence had weight: I saw it. The ordinary world was still possible when sight and sound agreed.

Will lowered himself onto the turned canoe. "Great. New rule. Applaud every verified noise."

"Could do worse," Joel said.

Will looked at him, surprised. For one second the corner of his mouth moved. Then the wind hit the tarp and the moment went.

They ate because bodies required betrayal after betrayal. Mike boiled water. Joel divided rice and peanut butter and a torn packet of tuna. Will refused at first, then took the mug and held it until steam fogged the lower half of his glasses. He had not cleaned them since the night before. A smear of mud crossed one lens. Joel wanted to tell him and did not. The small caretaking sentences were all suspect now. Quit mothering me. Careful. Eat. Sleep. Stay close. The language that kept people alive could also be used to move them.

Will ate three bites, then put the mug down. "No, no, no."

Mike's head came up.

Will was not looking at the brush. He was looking at the food. "I can't."

"You can," Joel said.

"Don't."

Joel stopped.

Will pushed the mug away and stood too fast. The tarp line brushed his hood. He flinched as if touched by a hand.

"I need to walk," he said.

"You need to sit down," Mike said.

"I am going to walk six feet in a circle like a zoo animal. Is that inside the approved grief perimeter?"

"Everything you say gives it more," Mike said.

The sentence silenced them more completely than a shout.

Mike seemed to hear what he had said only after it was out. His face changed, not into apology, but into guarded calculation. He had named something. Not fully. Not in words they could examine. But he had stepped off the bear track and onto another trail.

Will stared at him. "Gives what?"

Mike looked toward the lake. "Nothing."

"No. No, no, no. You don't get to say that and then nothing it."

"I meant sound carries."

"Bullshit."

"I meant we're all worked up and repeating ourselves and making it worse."

"Making what worse?"

Mike grabbed the mug Will had abandoned and set it harder than necessary near the stove. "The situation."

"You are physically allergic to nouns."

Joel said, "Wind first."

Will turned on him. "What?"

"Wind first," Joel said. "Whatever else is true, the wind is why we're here."

"That is not the whole reason we're here."

"No. It's the reason we can do something about."

Will laughed under his breath. "Can we?"

Joel did not answer. He went to the shore because he needed to see the lake again. The others followed, Mike because he would not let Joel be the one to assess it, Will because remaining under the tarp alone was worse.

At the channel mouth the wind had strengthened. The low cloud had dropped until it seemed snagged in the spruce tops on the far shore. The lake surface had a hard metallic look now, dark gray under white streaks, water moving in separate orders: the long push of wind down the length, the sideways chop rebounding from rock, the confused slap where both met near the point. A canoe could cross it. Men crossed worse water every year and told stories afterward. That was the dangerous truth. Not impossible. Only stupid for them, now, with one solo boat, shock in their hands, and no spare margin.

Mike stood with his arms crossed. "It may drop near evening."

"And then what?" Will said. "We cross at dusk?"

"If it's calm enough."

"If it's calm enough at dusk after no sleep and half a meal, with a dead man ten yards behind us and something doing community theater in the brush."

Mike's jaw tightened.

Joel kept his eyes on the water. "We don't cross in the dark."

"Nobody said dark," Mike said.

"Dusk becomes dark faster when you're wet."

"I know how light works."

Will coughed once, a small dry bark.

The same cough answered from behind them.

Not an echo. Not delayed by the rocks in any natural way. It came from the camp side, left of the tarp, near the food barrel and the turned canoe. Will was standing on Joel's right at the channel mouth, bent slightly into the wind, one hand still at his throat from the first cough. The second cough had the same tight scrape in it, the same little embarrassed finish, as if the body that made it wanted to explain itself and could not.

Joel turned.

The camp was empty.

Rain had not started yet, but the air had thickened. The tarp moved. The barrel sat under the canoe. The packs lay where they had left them. No shape stood by the spruce. No branch moved except with wind. There was enough distance between Will and the camp for the fact to become large.

"What?" Will said.

Joel looked back at him.

Will's face had already understood from Joel's expression. "What?"

Mike said, "Sound off the water."

Joel did not answer.

"It was my cough," Will said.

"You coughed," Mike said.

"Twice?"

"Echo."

"From behind us?"

"Wind does weird things."

"That's not how that works."

The phrase struck the air and then seemed to remain there, available.

Joel saw Will realize it. His mouth closed hard.

Mike looked away first. "Back to camp."

"No," Will said. "No, wait."

"Back to camp."

"You heard it."

"I heard a cough."

"My cough."

"You are not the only mammal with a throat."

"Find me the other middle-aged anxious ex-LED-quality-control mammal in the spruce and I will apologize."

Mike's face flashed with anger and fear together. "Do you want to stand here and feed it?"

Again the word, almost spoken around. It.

Joel said, "Back. Eyes on camp."

They walked the short distance with too much care. No one turned his back fully until the brush forced it. At the tarp, Mike picked up the bear spray from where he had set it beside the stove. The canister was almost spent. Its weight had become symbolic before it had become useless, and now it was both.

Will stood in the center of camp and looked at the place the cough had come from.

"Say something," he said.

Mike stared at him. "Are you out of your mind?"

"Not to it. To me. Say what you think."

"I think we are exhausted and cold and hearing things."

"No, you don't."

"I think we are going to sit tight until the wind drops."

"That is not an answer."

"It is the only answer you get."

Will turned to Joel.

Joel hated him for it a little. He hated the request in his face, the old assumption that Joel would be fair, would be precise, would not bully the evidence into shape. They had been boys together in rooms where adults told them truth was the same as goodness if you chased it hard enough. They had grown up and learned that truth could arrive too late to help, or too ugly to use, or without instructions.

"I heard it from camp," Joel said.

Will's face loosened with relief and then tightened with fresh fear.

Mike said nothing.

"I saw you," Joel said to Will. "You were here."

"So."

"So we know that much."

"That's it?"

"That's it."

Will laughed, almost silently. "You're killing me with the scientific method."

"I'm trying not to get us killed without it."

That ended the conversation because it had too much of the truth in it.

The hour passed. Then another. The wind did not lay down. It rose, dropped, rose again harder, as if reconsidering and becoming more committed. Low cloud came in from the west and erased the far shore for minutes at a time. The air smelled like cold metal and spruce pitch and old water. Mosquitoes vanished into whatever cracks mosquitoes used when weather turned. The quiet without them was not peace.

They sorted gear because inaction became unbearable.

Mike repacked his canoe twice on shore, moving weight forward, then back, then forward again. He tried kneeling behind the center thwart and rocking the hull under his hands, testing balance that would not matter until water told the truth. Joel watched without comment. The old Mike would have taken advice as insult and silence as judgment. This Mike took both badly and continued working.

Will opened the map case on the canoe seat. The plastic had fogged inside. He wiped it with the sleeve of his fleece and left streaks. Joel could see the route under his hand: the blue lake they could not cross, the portage line beyond it, the old dotted trace farther along that might or might not lead toward the cabin. The map made a clean argument. It had no wind on it. No body. No copied cough. No shortage of men.

"If we backtrack," Will said, not looking up, "we know every ugly piece."

"We know some of it," Joel said.

"We know more than forward."

Mike tightened a strap on his pack. "We also know it takes us past where he--"

He stopped.

Will looked up. "Past Alex."

Mike's mouth moved once.

"Say his name," Will said.

"Don't."

"No, say it. If we're going to use him as route data, say his name."

Mike threw the strap end down. "Alex is dead in the alder. There. Happy?"

The name changed the camp. Not because the woods needed it. Because they did. Joel felt the syllables enter the air and become part of what could come back. He wanted to snatch them away, absurdly, like a pot sliding off the stove.

Will looked stricken, though he had asked for it. "Don't say it like that."

"You told me to say it."

"I told you to stop making him an obstacle."

"He is an obstacle if our choices get us killed."

"He is our friend."

"He's both!"

The shout went into the spruce and came back as nothing.

Mike's breathing was loud. Will's face had gone white except for the raw red around his eyes. Joel waited. The woods waited too, or seemed to. That was the trap in it. Every silence now looked intentional if a man stared long enough.

Nothing answered in Alex's voice.

That was almost worse. The camp held the name and did not spend it.

Mike turned away first. He cleared his throat, caught himself doing it, and pressed his lips together.

Joel saw that too. Habit becoming suspect in the living. The body no longer allowed even its small noises to be innocent.

By midafternoon the first rain arrived. It came as a mist, so fine at first that Joel could see it only where it gathered on the black blade of his paddle. Then the drops thickened and began to tick through the spruce. The lake darkened under it. The whitecaps remained, lower but steadier, the whole surface working with a cold patience that did not care about men, maps, or names.

"We need to decide before dark," Mike said.

"We decided," Will said. "We're stuck."

"Stuck is not a decision."

"It sure has decision-like consequences."

Joel pulled his hood up. Water had begun to run from his hair down the back of his neck. "We don't move in this."

Mike looked at the lake again, and for a moment Joel thought he would argue from pride alone. Instead he nodded once, almost invisibly.

"Fine," Mike said.

The word did not mean fine. It meant the line had been reached.

They secured the camp for weather with the bitter efficiency of men who had lost the argument with the day. Boats higher. Packs under tarp. Wet clothes separated from less wet clothes though nothing was dry. Food barrel closed, latched, strapped, and hung again from the same bad tree because there was no better tree within the shrinking perimeter they were willing to use. This time Joel watched the latch close. Mike saw him watching and said nothing.

Will helped until helping ran out. Then he stood under the tarp and looked toward the alder gap again.

"No," Mike said.

Will did not move.

"I'm not going in," he said.

"Good."

"I was just thinking."

"Try less."

Will turned on him, but the fight was gone from his face. What remained was worse. "What if he isn't covered right?"

Mike shut his eyes.

Joel felt the question pass through him like cold water. The groundsheet. The stones. Rain gathering on plastic. Wind getting under one loose edge. Animals. The thing. The indignity of weather. The impossibility of fixing all of it.

"He is," Joel said.

Will looked at him. "You don't know."

"I know we covered him as well as we could."

"That sentence again."

"It's the only one I have."

Rain strengthened. It hit the tarp in separate taps that became one continuous sound. The lake disappeared behind the wet screen of alder and low cloud. The camp pulled inward until it was stove, packs, knees, rope, wet sleeves, the stink of bear spray still caught in fabric, and three men trying not to listen too hard.

Mike sat with his back against a canoe and the spent bear spray across his lap. Will sat opposite him, arms around his knees, the map case under one hand. Joel stayed near the tarp edge because he could see both the channel and the food barrel from there. That was how he justified the position. The other reason was that sitting deeper under the tarp made him feel trapped inside the sound of their own breathing.

"We're fine," Mike said.

He said it quietly, to the rain or to himself. The old phrase had lost its use and kept its shape. No one mocked it. No one accepted it.

Joel looked at the three of them under the tarp and knew the sentence was now dangerous in two directions. Dangerous because it was false. Dangerous because it had been said again, in Mike's exact voice, with the tired downward pressure at the end. Another piece given away.

Will's mouth opened.

Joel shook his head once.

Will closed it.

For several minutes they listened to the rain. Some of it was only rain. Some of it was spruce releasing water from higher branches. Some was lake chop reaching through the channel and touching mud. Once, a strap ticked against a pack buckle until Joel reached over and stopped it with his thumb. The absence after the ticking stopped was immediate and large.

Near the food barrel, from the wrong side of the tarp, Will coughed once.

Will sat in front of Joel with both hands over his mouth.

Mike lifted his head.

Joel did not turn quickly. He turned with care, as if the speed of looking could become an answer. The barrel hung pale and wet from the branch, red strap darkened by rain, latch seated where he had seen it seat. There was no one beneath it. No figure behind the spruce. No movement except water running down bark.

The cough did not repeat.

Will lowered his hands. He had not made a sound. His eyes were wide and furious, as if the thing had not frightened him so much as plagiarized him badly.

Joel put one finger to his lips.

This time Will obeyed without argument.

Rain came down harder. The camp they had tried to leave became shelter again, and shelter was only a shape that kept weather off while sound found its way in.


# Chapter 11 - The Second Night After

The second night after was worse because they knew what they were listening for and still did not know what it was.

Rain closed the camp. It came down without force and without mercy, a cold steady rain that made everything close. It put a roof over the smallest sounds and flattened the rest. The tarp became an instrument. Each drop struck, joined another, became one continuous hand on nylon. Water gathered along the low edge and released in ropes. The spruce above them held its own store of rain and spent it later, heavier, as if another weather had been waiting in the branches.

The men sat close without comfort.

Mike had the bear spray across his knees. The canister looked smaller in the dark, its orange cap wet and dull, its weight wrong for what they wanted from it. Will had the map case open though there was no light to read by. He kept one hand on the plastic as if the route might move under him if he let go. Joel had his notebook in the dry pocket of his jacket and had not taken it out yet.

They had agreed on watches. Then they had all stayed awake.

The agreement itself had been brief and useless. Mike had said first watch, because saying first watch sounded like command. Will had said no, no, no, we need actual rotations, and then had been unable to produce one that mattered. Joel had proposed two-hour blocks, not because two hours was sensible but because any number was better than the three of them staring into rain until morning. They named the blocks and immediately broke them. Mike would not close his eyes. Will closed his twice and opened them both times before a minute passed. Joel sat near the tarp edge, his hood up, one knee braced against a pack, watching the food barrel hang in the bad spruce beyond the gray line of rain.

The barrel was still where they had put it before dark. Closed, latched, strapped, hauled into the same inadequate tree because moving farther from camp would mean moving nearer the alder gap, and none of them had said that aloud. The rope ran black against the trunk. The barrel turned a little when the wind found it, not enough to swing, only enough to remind Joel that it was there.

Beyond it, somewhere behind brush and weather and the orange strip Mike had tied that morning, Alex lay under the groundsheet.

No one had checked him before full dark.

Will had asked once. Not directly. He had said, "The wind could get under plastic," while staring at the gap, and Mike had said, "It won't," too quickly. Joel had watched Will's mouth move around the next sentence and fail. That was how the old friendship worked under strain: the worst thing approached, became visible, and then they all looked at the practical object nearest to hand. Knot. stove. map. rope. The object was mercy because it did not require an answer.

Now there were no objects left to fix.

"If it is a bear," Will said, too softly, "it may still be nearby."

Mike's head shifted. "It is nearby because we're still here."

"That's not a reason."

"It's the only reason that matters."

"No. No, it's not."

Joel said, "Save it."

Both men looked at him. He rarely used that tone. He did not like how easily it came, how quickly fatigue found a harder voice in him and put it on without asking. The rain filled the space after it.

Will looked back at the map. "I'm not trying to be an ass."

"You don't always have to try," Mike said.

Will gave him a thin look. "Good. Nice. Productive."

"Then stop."

"Stop what? Thinking about the large animal that may or may not be a large animal? Because I have made several attempts."

Mike drew a breath, held it, let it out through his nose. Joel saw his throat move. He did not clear it. The withheld sound was visible and more intimate than the habit itself.

"We treat it like a bear until there is a better thing to do," Mike said.

"There isn't a better thing to do," Will said. "That is not the same as it being a bear."

"Will."

"There is a difference."

The phrase entered the tarp space and sat there with them. Joel thought of the first camp, Will saying it over some minor correction, a lure knot or a map distinction or one of the thousand ways he made the world bearable by cutting it into categories. There is a difference. There was. There should have been. Between bear and not bear. Between echo and answer. Between friend and the sound of a friend.

Joel said, "Not tonight."

Will looked at him.

"The difference doesn't help tonight," Joel said.

Will's face tightened. He wanted to argue. It was not only habit now. It was survival to him, or the nearest thing he had. If he could say it correctly enough, arrange the pieces, distinguish cause from coincidence and animal from voice, then the world might become answerable again. Joel had known him as a boy in church basements where adults used language the same way. Prayer requests. Testimonies. Concerns. They could turn disaster into a form if they named it in the right tone. Cancer became a season. Divorce became a burden. A dead child became a homegoing. Joel had hated that language before he knew he hated it, the soft hand it laid over anything sharp.

Will had not hated it then. He had simply been bored and restless and overbright, whispering wrong jokes during youth group and coughing into his sleeve when he tried not to laugh. Joel could see him at twelve, fluorescent-lit and too thin, making Joel look down at his Bible so the adults would not see his face. They had learned early that some rooms punished the direct sentence. They had learned to say almost, to imply, to talk beside the worst thing until it passed.

The woods did not punish the direct sentence. It kept it.

A drop struck Joel's wrist and ran under his cuff. He pulled his hand back and tucked it inside his sleeve.

"What are you thinking?" Will said.

"Nothing useful."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the one I've got."

Mike shifted the bear spray on his knees. "We need less talking."

Will laughed once. No humor in it. "You first."

Mike turned his face toward him. In the dark under the tarp his eyes had no color. "I mean it."

"So do I."

Joel lifted a hand. Not high. Just enough. Both of them saw it and both of them stopped, which surprised him. He kept his hand up another second, then let it fall. He listened until the rain separated again into layers: tarp above, spruce beyond, water dripping from a paddle blade somewhere near the boats, the lake shouldering itself against the channel mouth.

Then the food barrel latch clicked.

It was a small sound. Ordinary. One of the first trip sounds. Plastic, metal, pressure released. The kind of sound a body could identify before thought arrived. Joel's skin tightened before his mind placed it.

The barrel hung outside the tarp, pale in the rain, ten yards away and too high for any man to touch without the rope.

No one moved.

The click had not come from the barrel. Or the barrel had made a sound without moving. Or something below it had made the barrel's exact sound. Joel watched the hanging shape. The strap remained tight. The lid did not lift. The rain ran down its curved side. Nothing stood beneath it.

Mike's hand closed over the bear spray.

"Don't," Joel said.

"I didn't."

"I know."

"Then don't tell me don't."

Will whispered, "It was the latch."

"I heard it," Mike said.

"The latch is up there."

"I can see that."

"Then how--"

"I can see that."

The second time was quieter and worse. Mike was not arguing the fact. He was refusing its use.

Joel leaned forward until his rain jacket pulled tight across his shoulders. He kept his eyes on the barrel and tried to make his body understand that sight and sound no longer belonged to one another by right. A thing could be seen shut and heard open. A man could stand beside him and cough from camp. The ordinary world had not vanished. It had come apart at the joint.

Something moved at the edge of the tarp.

Not outside. On the tarp itself. A bead of water traveled along the seam above Mike's shoulder, gathered, fattened, and dropped onto his sleeve. Mike flinched, then hated that he had.

Will covered his mouth with both hands. It might have been to stop a laugh. It might have been to stop speech. His fingers were pale where he pressed them into his face.

"Barrel can make noises," Mike said.

Joel looked at him.

"Wind twists it," Mike said. "Strap settles. Plastic flexes. Temperature drops."

"Sure," Joel said.

Mike heard the flatness. "What?"

"I said sure."

"You mean bullshit."

"I mean possible."

"Possible is not agreement."

"No."

Will lowered his hands. "There is a difference."

Mike's head snapped toward him.

Will's eyes widened a little, as if he had heard the phrase only after saying it. He pressed his mouth closed.

They sat in the rain, three living men under a piece of nylon, each trying not to give the night an obvious shape to take.

The tent zipper came from the dark behind them.

It was not loud. It did not need to be. A small tooth-by-tooth rasp, rising, then stopping. A pause. Then the downward rasp, slower. Joel knew the sound from every camp he had ever slept in, knew the slight catch where wet fabric pulled and the slider dragged over grit. He also knew where both tents were. One stood collapsed low and damp between two spruce roots where Mike had refused to move it because moving it meant handling Alex's half-packed things. The other sat closer to the tarp, fly sagging, door facing them, zipper plainly shut. Joel's hammock and bug net hung dark between cedars, straps slackened by rain but visible. No one was inside any of them.

The zipper moved again.

Up. Down.

Patient. Demonstrating.

Will said, "Both tents are right here."

"I know," Joel said.

"No," Will said. "No, I mean right here."

"I know."

Mike stood too fast and hit his shoulder on the tarp line. Water shook down over all of them. He raised the bear spray toward the sound, though the sound had already ended.

"Show yourself," he said.

"Don't," Joel said.

Mike's jaw worked. "It's an animal."

"Then it doesn't understand English."

Mike froze with his mouth half open.

That, more than anything, made Joel afraid. Not the zipper. Not the latch. Mike had wanted to answer. The thing, or the night, or whatever shape the fear had found, had made a situation with only one human motion in it: challenge it, name it, push back. Mike's whole adult life had trained itself toward that motion. A bad deal, a wrong map, a man arguing from the front of a canoe, a bear in camp, a route going ugly. Stand up. Make noise. Take command. But sound had become the trap, and command was only another sound.

Mike sat back down slowly. Rain dripped from the brim of his hood onto his hands.

"I'm not going to sit here while something walks around camp," he said.

"You don't know it's walking," Joel said.

"Oh, that's comforting."

"You don't."

"So what is it doing?"

Joel kept his eyes on the tents. "Making sounds."

"Christ."

Will made a soft, broken sound. "That's the first true thing anyone's said in two hours."

Mike turned on him. "Do not start."

"I am deeply past starting."

Joel put one hand on Will's sleeve. Not gripping. Just contact. Will looked down at it, then at Joel.

"What?" he said.

Joel shook his head once.

Will swallowed the next sentence. It cost him. Joel could see it cost him in the muscles around his mouth, in the way his body needed to push language out and had nowhere to send it. He looked suddenly young and old at the same time, the boy from church and the unemployed man under a wilderness tarp, both of them silenced by rooms that had too many rules.

Out in the rain, Alex laughed once.

Will bent forward as if struck in the stomach. Mike did not move. Joel's hand tightened on Will's sleeve without his meaning to.

The laugh came from beyond the barrel, near the alder but not exactly at it. Alex's bark of laughter, the hard bright start, the little scrape at the end when he had been drinking coffee or trying to get everyone to turn toward him. It did not sound far away. It did not sound close. Rain made a room of the camp and put the voice in every corner.

"Stop," Will said.

Joel's grip tightened.

The laugh came again, farther off this time, or shaped to sound farther off. Thinner. Less body in it.

"Stop," Will said, louder.

"Will."

"Stop it."

Joel grabbed his sleeve with both hands. Will tried to pull free. For one bad second Joel thought he would go, not because he believed Alex was alive but because grief does not need belief to move the body. Then Will stopped fighting. His breath came in small hard pulls, almost coughs, and he held them back until his shoulders shook.

Mike said, "Maybe somebody's out there."

Nobody believed him fully, but they all needed the sentence. A poacher. Another group. A ranger. Some human cruelty so improbable it became attractive. Men could be punished. Men could be found. Men could explain why they had a dead friend's laugh in their mouths.

"Then why Alex?" Will said.

Mike did not answer.

The rain thickened. It drummed hard enough to make talk private and impossible at once. Joel heard the lake beyond the channel as a low shove, felt it more than heard it. The water had kept them here. That was an ordinary fact. Wind and rain and one bad boat. No monster was needed for that. The wilderness was fully capable of killing them without intention. It had been killing people that way forever, through weather, cold, mistake, distance, pride. The awful part was not that nature had become malicious. The awful part was that something intimate had entered all that indifference and learned to use it.

Mike lifted the bear spray, looked at it, and set it back across his knees.

"If we hear it again," he said, "we don't answer."

Will laughed into his hands. "Now you arrive."

"I said don't answer."

"I heard you."

"Then act like it."

Will's head came up. "What do you think I'm doing?"

"Talking."

"So are you."

"Because you keep--"

Joel said, "Enough."

It came out quieter than before. It worked better. Mike looked away. Will looked at the wet ground between his sandals. Joel could see his toes, pale and wrinkled in the straps, dirt caught under one nail. Everyone had been wet too long.

"We need rules," Joel said.

Mike's mouth tightened. "Rules."

"For tonight."

"Don't answer voices. Don't leave camp. Don't split up. There's your rules."

"Visual confirmation," Joel said.

Will looked at him.

"If one of us hears one of us," Joel said, "we look first. We confirm by sight. We don't move toward sound unless we can see the person."

Mike stared into the rain. "This is insane."

"Yes."

Will gave a short, surprised breath that almost became a laugh and did not.

Joel took the notebook from his jacket pocket. The cover had softened at the corners despite the plastic bag. He opened it under his coat, shielding the page with his body, and clicked his pen once. The sound was small but all three of them noticed it.

"Sorry," he said.

"Don't apologize to the pen," Will said.

For a second Joel almost smiled.

He drew four columns because columns were better than prayer.

Alex.

Mike.

Will.

Camp.

The pen shook once before he steadied it. His hand was cold and clumsy; the page took the ink badly where damp had reached the paper. Under Alex he wrote laugh. mothering. hold up. He paused before the last one. Had they heard hold up after Alex died? Not cleanly. Not yet. They had heard him say it before. They had heard him use it on the route, in camp, at the edge before the attack. A seed, not a return. Joel crossed nothing out. Instead he made a small mark beside it. Later.

Under Will he wrote cough. no no no. He hesitated, then added difference.

Under Mike he wrote we're fine. opens up. throat clear. The words looked foolish on the page, too ordinary to justify fear. They looked like notes from a bad meeting, evidence for an argument no one would believe.

Under Camp he wrote latch. zipper. paddle knock. rope tap. stove click.

Mike leaned over. "What is that?"

"What we've heard," Joel said.

"You're making a ghost spreadsheet?"

"I'm making a list."

"Same thing, Madison."

The old nickname landed almost normally. Joel had been Madison since he moved there, as if relocation were a defect and the city itself had replaced whatever name they used before. Alex had made it louder. Will had used it when he wanted to make Joel provincial in a different direction. Mike used it when Joel was being, in Mike's view, too careful, too Midwestern in the wrong way, too cleanly reasonable.

For half a second Joel almost laughed. The almost hurt.

"Put that down," Will said.

Joel looked at him.

"Madison," Will said. "It heard that. Put it down."

Mike stared at him. "Jesus, Will."

"No, I'm just saying."

"Stop just saying."

Will flinched, then pressed his lips together.

Joel wrote Madison under Joel, in a fifth column he had not meant to make. He did not like seeing his own column there. He wrote wait beneath it, then stopped. The entity had heard him say it. It had heard all of them. Maybe from the landing. Maybe from the first knock at First Camp. Maybe only from Second Camp. The timeline mattered. The timeline might save them later. Tonight it only widened the dark.

"What about if it makes a sound that isn't a voice?" Mike said.

Joel capped the pen halfway, then uncapped it. "Same rule."

"How do you visually confirm a paddle knock?"

"You don't move toward it."

"What if it's the canoe?"

"Then it will still be the canoe in daylight."

Mike looked at him, angry because the answer was good enough to obey and not good enough to relieve him.

Will said, "What if it wants us not to move? What if this is the whole point? It pins us. It makes camp unsafe. It waits."

"Then we wait better than it does," Mike said.

Will blinked.

Mike seemed almost embarrassed by the sentence. "For tonight," he said.

That was the closest thing to courage anyone had said since the rain began.

Joel wrote: night rule - no sound without sight.

The words were too neat. He made himself underline sight once.

Something knocked from the shoreline.

Hollow wood on stone. One clean knock.

No one laughed. No one breathed loudly enough for the sound to hide inside it.

The knock came from near the canoes, or near the water beyond them, or from a place the rain had invented between. Joel could see the two canoe hulls pulled high and turned partly on their sides, dark and shining. Paddles were stacked beneath one boat, blades inward, exactly where he had left them. Painter ropes lay tight. No hull moved.

Another knock.

Not louder. Not closer. The same. The kind of repeat a person might make if the first had not been answered.

Will's hand went to the map case. His fingers pressed the plastic until it crackled.

Mike whispered, "Bear won't do that."

Will turned his head slowly toward him.

Mike did not look back. He had given up the word by accident and knew it.

The knock came a third time, but wrong. Softer at the start, with a scrape after it, like a paddle shaft sliding on granite. Joel saw in memory the first portage, a paddle knocked from under a thwart, Alex cursing with too much theater, Will saying that is why you don't put them there, Mike telling him to load faster and lecture less. The sound in the rain had no memory. It only had the shape.

"It's learned gear," Joel said.

He had not meant to say it aloud.

Both men looked at him.

The rain made the sentence small enough that the camp might not keep it. That was what he told himself. A lie, probably. Or not a lie. He did not know how close a sound had to be to matter. He did not know whether rain protected them or delivered them in pieces.

"What does that mean?" Will said.

Joel looked at the list. "It doesn't need our voices."

Will's face changed. Until that second, even after the cough, even after Alex's laugh, the danger had arranged itself around speech. Do not answer. Do not call. Do not give it phrases. But camp itself had been talking since the first day. Buckles. zippers. latches. ropes. paddles. stove. nylon. Every practical action had made a vocabulary. They had brought a language of objects into the woods and used it freely, believing objects could not betray them because objects did not mean.

The shoreline knocked once more.

Mike lifted the bear spray and lowered it again.

"We should bring the paddles in," he said.

"No," Joel said.

"They're right there."

"No."

"If something takes a paddle--"

"Then we deal with that when we can see."

Mike stared at the black shapes under the canoe. "We may need them fast."

"We're not getting on the water tonight."

"You don't know what happens tonight."

"I know we don't chase a paddle knock in the dark."

Will whispered, "I hate that he's right."

Mike shut his eyes. His face folded inward for a second. He was exhausted. They all were, but Mike wore exhaustion as a private insult. He opened his eyes and kept his body still.

The knock did not come again.

They sat until the rain changed pitch. The hard drumming softened into a finer hiss. Water ran from the tarp edges steadily. Somewhere to Joel's left a pack strap ticked against a buckle. He reached over and stopped it with his thumb. The absence after it stopped was immediate and large.

"Good catch," Will whispered.

Joel shook his head.

Will nodded once. No praise. No more words.

Time lost its edges. Joel checked his watch under his sleeve and saw that only twenty-three minutes had passed since he last looked. The night had the malicious generosity of giving them too much of itself. Mike sat with his chin tucked, eyes open. Will shifted, winced, shifted again. His knees must have hurt. Joel's own lower back had become a hot band beneath cold clothing. His feet were numb at the toes and raw where the sandal straps rubbed. He wanted to take the sandals off, dry his feet, change socks, do any of the small maintenance that kept a body from becoming stupid. He did none of it. Bare feet in the dark felt like surrender.

"You can sleep," Joel said, barely audible.

Will shook his head.

"Close your eyes for ten."

"That's how every terrible camping story begins."

"No talking."

Will made a face but closed his eyes.

For almost a minute he stayed that way. Then, from beyond the tarp and to Joel's right, Will said, "You guys hear that?"

Will's eyes opened.

He had not spoken.

The copied voice was quiet and exact enough in its parts to be wrong as a whole. It had Will's dry edge, the little lift at the end, the old invitation to make everyone else listen with him. But it carried no breath before it, no body after. It was a sentence removed from a man and set in the rain.

Mike's face went slack. Only for a second. Then anger covered it.

"No," Will whispered.

Joel put one finger to his lips.

Will's eyes filled, not with tears at first but with rage. His own phrase, his own small anxious hook, had been placed outside him and used without permission. Joel understood the rage better than the fear. Fear said something was dangerous. Rage said something had crossed into ownership.

The voice came again. "You guys hear that?"

Same phrase. Same direction. Too even this time. It did not correct for the fact that no one had answered. It did not become impatient. It did not understand impatience, maybe. It had only the sound of invitation.

Mike turned his head toward Joel slowly, asking without asking.

Joel pointed two fingers at his own eyes, then toward Will sitting under the tarp. Visual confirmation. Will was here. Therefore Will was not there. The conclusion was crude and lifesaving.

Will mouthed something.

Joel shook his head.

Will's mouth closed.

The copied voice did not speak again.

After a while, Mike leaned close enough that Joel could smell stale coffee and rain on his breath. "What did you write?"

Joel showed him the page.

Mike took it in with a strained, impatient glance. The columns. The small fifth column with Joel's name. The underlined rule.

"Add that," Mike whispered.

Joel wrote under Will: you guys hear that.

The pen moved too loudly. He stopped between words and listened. Nothing answered the scratch of ink on damp paper. Of course nothing did. It could not read. It could not see the columns. It could only hear what they gave it. The thought steadied him more than it should have. A limit, however small, was a kind of shore.

He wrote more carefully: writing safe? Then put a question mark beside it. He did not know. He only knew the page had made no sound except his pen, and the pen had not yet become bait.

Will watched him write. His eyes moved over the page but he did not speak. Joel saw the question in him, bright and hungry. Can it learn from this? Does it understand? Is it a recording? Is it animal? Is it us? The questions crowded his face. Joel put the notebook back in the plastic bag before Will could borrow it and turn questions into air.

Mike saw the motion and nodded once.

It was the first time all night the three of them had agreed on anything without saying it.

The rain eased near midnight, or what Joel thought was midnight. The woods grew louder when the rain softened. Water dropped from needles. The lake resumed its individual slaps against the channel bank. Somewhere far off a loon called once and then did not call again. The sound was so ordinary Joel almost hated it. A real animal in a real night, carrying on with its life in the same dark that held Alex's voice.

Will's head dipped.

Joel watched him. The dip became a nod, the nod became a jerk, and Will woke with a sharp inhale that he managed to cut off before it became a cough. He looked ashamed.

Joel touched two fingers to his own chest, then pointed at Will, then curled his hand under his cheek: I watch, you sleep.

Will shook his head.

Joel pointed harder.

Will looked at Mike. Mike, after a moment, jerked his chin once in agreement. That decided it because Mike still had enough authority to make surrender feel like compliance.

Will lay down on his side under the tarp, not in the tent, not in the hammock, just on the thin pad they had dragged near the packs. He did not take off his rain jacket. He put the map case against his chest like a child with a book. His glasses stayed on. Within minutes his breathing grew uneven, not sleep exactly but a border state his body took without permission.

Mike remained upright.

"You too," Joel whispered.

"No."

"Ten minutes."

"I said no."

Joel did not push. Mike needed to refuse something. If sleep was the thing, fine.

They listened together.

Without Will talking, Mike seemed less angry and more frighteningly alone. The rain had carved the force out of him. He sat with the spent canister, a man built for decisions in a place where every decision fed the wrong mouth. Joel could see him wanting daylight with a physical hunger. Daylight would not solve it, but it would return cause to shape. It would let them count objects. Canoes. paddles. packs. barrel. body. It would let them put grief back into work.

"Tomorrow," Mike whispered, "we move."

Joel nodded.

"I don't care if it's ugly."

Joel did not nod to that.

Mike saw the distinction and almost smiled. Not with humor. Recognition, maybe. "Fine. We assess and move."

Joel nodded once.

Mike looked toward the hanging food barrel. "That thing makes the latch again, I am not going out there."

"Good."

"Don't good me."

Joel looked at him. Mike looked back. For a second they were simply two wet men being petty in the dark, and the pettiness was human enough to hurt.

Then Mike cleared his throat.

He stopped halfway through the sound.

Joel saw his face change.

The unfinished throat clear hung in the tarp space, small and living. Mike's own habit had caught him by surprise. He put one hand over his mouth, as if he could take it back. No answer came from the woods. No copied throat clear, no route instruction, no we're fine in Mike's voice from beyond the barrel. The absence was not relief. It was a held breath.

Mike lowered his hand.

"I hate this," he whispered.

Joel nodded.

There was nothing useful to say to that, and because there was nothing useful, he said nothing.

Will slept for maybe twelve minutes. Then his body, treacherous with exhaustion, coughed.

Not loud. A dry catch in the throat, the familiar small bark. He woke as it left him and clapped both hands over his mouth, eyes wide behind rain-spotted lenses.

All three men listened.

The answer came from near the food barrel.

The same cough. Will's cough. Wet night around it. A little embarrassed finish, as if the body that made it wanted to apologize.

Will began to shake.

Mike raised the bear spray and kept it aimed at the dark below the barrel. Joel put his hand on Will's shoulder, hard enough to hurt if hurt would keep him here.

The cough did not repeat.

Joel leaned close to Will's ear. He did not speak. He pressed two fingers against Will's shoulder, then pointed at Will, then at the ground: you are here. Will stared at him, not understanding at first. Joel did it again.

You are here.

Will nodded. Once. Then again.

The gesture steadied Joel too. That was the terrible simplicity. Not what is it. Not why Alex. Not bear or not bear. The rule for the night was smaller and more exact: the man you can see is the man. The sound is only sound until sight gives it a body.

Something brushed the outside of the tarp.

All three froze.

The brush came again, low along the edge behind Mike. Fabric dented inward an inch and released. Mike turned with the bear spray. Joel's mind supplied branch, wind, water weight, a rope end shaken loose. The tarp edge moved again, harder this time, then stopped.

No voice followed.

No laugh. No latch. No zipper. No knock.

That was worse in its own way. The silence after a touch was nearly a shape.

Mike crawled one knee forward, slow, keeping under the tarp. Joel caught his sleeve.

Mike looked back.

Joel shook his head.

Mike pointed at the tarp edge with two fingers, then at his own eyes.

Visual confirmation.

It was almost funny that Mike had learned the rule well enough to use it against him.

Joel hesitated. The tarp edge was three feet away. Not ten yards. Not the alder. Three feet. They could look without leaving shelter. They could also give the night exactly what it wanted: a body leaning out, a face at the edge, a man responding to a sound because not knowing had become intolerable.

Will reached for the notebook.

Joel let him.

Will opened to a blank lower corner and wrote with slow block letters, the pen nearly tearing the damp page.

WAIT.

Then, after a moment, he added:

DAYLIGHT.

He pushed the notebook toward Mike.

Mike read it. His face moved through anger, insult, calculation, and finally exhaustion. He sat back.

The tarp edge did not move again.

Joel took the notebook and wrote below Will's words:

NOT SOUND. SIGHT.

Will read it and nodded.

Mike read it and looked away.

The night went on.

It was not dramatic after that. That was part of its cruelty. They did not spend the whole night in a sequence of voices and signs. Much of it was only wet discomfort and waiting. The body made its demands without respect for terror. Joel had to piss and did not. Will's leg cramped and he worked it out silently, teeth bared. Mike's hands grew stiff around the bear spray until Joel pointed at them and Mike flexed his fingers with visible irritation. Rainwater found a path down Joel's neck. The tarp sagged, and at one point all three men had to push upward with their palms to dump a cold pool off the far edge. The water hit the ground outside with a heavy slap. Everyone listened afterward to see if the slap would be returned.

It was not.

Once, near the hour before dawn, Alex said, very softly, "Quit mothering me."

Not from the alder. Not from the body site. From the camp center, or the shore, or the rain's false room. The phrase came with less of him in it than the laugh had. The shape of Alex without Alex's heat. The old joke emptied of the face that made it survivable.

Will did not move. Mike did not lift the bear spray. Joel closed his eyes for one second and saw Alex at the motel, Alex at the first muddy pullout, Alex holding up the hot sauce like a sacrament, Alex alive enough to be irritating. Joel had resented that life when it filled the air. Now the absence used the same air and asked for an answer.

No one gave it one.

The phrase did not repeat.

Gray began slowly, not as light but as a thinning of black. The trees appeared first as darker decisions inside dark. Then the hanging food barrel took shape, wet and pale, latch closed. The canoes emerged from the shore gloom, still there. The tents, both of them. Joel's hammock straps. Packs. stove. map case. Mike's hand around the bear spray. Will's face, unshaven and slack with the beginning of shock beyond fear.

The world looked almost accountable again.

No one said morning. No one said fine.

Joel opened the notebook one last time before they began the work of leaving. The page had buckled from damp. The columns were ugly, crowded, childish in their certainty and still the best thing they had.

Alex: laugh. mothering.

Will: cough. you guys hear that. no no no. difference.

Mike: we're fine. opens up. throat clear.

Camp: latch. zipper. paddle knock. rope tap. tarp touch.

Joel: Madison. wait.

At the bottom, in Will's block letters:

WAIT.

DAYLIGHT.

And beneath it, Joel's own:

NOT SOUND. SIGHT.

Mike leaned over, read the last line, and said nothing. Will read it too. His eyes stayed on the words for a long time.

Joel understood only this: something had learned the sound of their camp. It had learned the parts of them that carried without bodies. It had learned latch and zipper and paddle, cough and laugh and the little phrases men used because silence felt too large. It had not learned the page. Not yet. Maybe not ever.

That was not hope.

It was only a fact with a hard edge, and by morning a hard edge was enough to hold.


# Chapter 12 - Bad Crossing

Morning gave them a smaller wind and the same fear.

The camp looked almost innocent in gray light. That was the first cruelty of it. The barrel hung shut. The canoes were still on the stones. The tents sagged and steamed a little where the rain had stopped. Joel's hammock straps made dark bands around wet trunks. Nothing in the daylight confessed to what it had done in the night.

Alex's groundsheet showed through the alder as a flat wrong color.

No one looked at it for long.

Mike stood with both hands on his hips, staring at the water as if the lake were a man refusing an order. His hood was down. His hair had dried in hard cowlicks during whatever few minutes of sleep his body had taken without permission. The bear spray hung from his PFD by its strap, orange and dented and nearly empty. He had not put it away. He had not offered to pack it in any dry bag.

Will sat on a rock with the map case across his knees. The plastic had clouded inside from condensation. He kept wiping it with the heel of his hand and making it worse. His glasses were spotted from the night. His face had the pinched, elderly look of a man who had spent too long cold and upright.

Joel stood at the shore and watched the lake measure itself in short black lines.

It was not calm. Calm would have been a different world, and they were not in it. But the high white tearing of the day before had flattened. Wind still came from the west in uneven pushes, but there were spaces between them now. The near shore was protected by the point, and beyond the point the main water showed dark, ribbed, manageable if they were clean and quick and lucky.

Those were too many conditions.

Mike took them as permission.

"We move now," he said.

Will did not look up from the map. "That's still ugly."

"Ugly is not closed."

"That's not a category."

"It's the only category we have."

Joel wanted to object with something better than instinct. He wanted a sentence with weight in it, a fact that could stand against Mike's need to be gone. The map showed the nearest exit forward. Backtracking meant Alex, the beaver dams, the rough portage, First Camp, the first water, the long unspooling of everything they had already survived badly. Staying meant another day beside the covered body and another night under nylon, listening until they stopped being men and became only ears.

He looked at the notebook page again. The pencil and ink had buckled the damp paper. Will's block letters still sat near the bottom.

WAIT.

DAYLIGHT.

Joel's own line beneath it:

NOT SOUND. SIGHT.

It had worked for the night because the night had been made of waiting. Morning was work. Morning required deciding which sounds to trust enough to move a boat through them.

Will tapped the map case. "If we miss this landing, we're committed to the north shore."

"Then don't miss it," Mike said.

"I don't steer your boat."

"No, you just narrate it afterward."

Will made a small sound, almost a laugh and almost a cough, and strangled it. All three of them heard the restraint in it. The habit stopped in his throat before the woods could take it.

Mike saw it too. His mouth tightened, then loosened. "Sorry."

Will looked up at him. Neither of them knew what to do with that.

Joel folded the notebook and put it back in the plastic bag. "We can cross the protected part and reassess from the lee of the island."

"There isn't an island," Mike said.

"Rock hump. Whatever."

Will lifted the map. "Technically there is a named island."

Mike looked at him.

Will said, "Not helpful. Understood."

For a second the old rhythm tried to return. It came weakly, like a fish flashing once under dark water, and was gone.

They broke camp without calling tasks across the site. That alone changed everything. On every other morning of the trip, leaving had been a noise: Mike telling someone to get the barrel down, Will asking where his filter cap went, Alex laughing from the wrong side of a job, Joel answering from a pack or a stove. Now they pointed, nodded, touched objects, made small hand motions like men working beside a sleeping child.

The silence did not make the work graceful. It made it stupid and slow.

Mike lowered the food barrel while Joel watched the brush. The rope hissed over the branch, a normal sound so exact it made Joel's teeth touch. The barrel thudded against the trunk. Mike flinched and then angrily did not look toward the alder. Will packed the tarp with fingers that kept missing the fold. Twice he started to ask something and stopped himself with his mouth open. Joel handled the stove, the wet mugs, the fuel bottle, the small hard things that could be counted and made to fit.

Alex's gear had already been divided the day before, but his absence kept being reissued by objects. His spare socks in a compression sack. His hot sauce wrapped in a bandanna. The cracked headlamp in Joel's side pocket. A red carabiner Alex had used for nothing important and had still somehow needed to bring.

Will found the carabiner clipped to the outside of a pack and held it up.

Nobody claimed it.

Will put it in the pack anyway and shut the buckle with great care, as if roughness would be heard as disrespect.

They did not go back to Alex's body. Not fully. Mike walked to the edge of the alder and checked the orange tape from ten feet away. Joel went with him because letting Mike go alone would break their only rule before breakfast. Will came halfway and stopped behind them, arms wrapped around himself, map case under one elbow.

The groundsheet remained under its stones. Rain had slicked the plastic down so it showed the shape beneath too clearly in some places and not enough in others. The stones looked small. Every stone anyone had ever placed on a grave looked small once it was there.

Mike stood with his jaw working.

"We come back with people," he said.

It was the first future he had allowed that included anyone outside the three of them.

Joel nodded.

Will said, "We write that."

Mike turned. "Write what?"

"That we left. Direction. Time. Names again. If someone comes in from behind us."

"No one's coming in from behind us."

"Mike."

The name was not argument. It was exhaustion shaped into one syllable.

Mike looked away. "Fine."

Joel tore a page from the back of the notebook and wrote beneath the overhang of his jacket while rainwater fell from the spruce in slow after-weather drops. He kept it factual. Three survivors leaving Second Camp morning Day 4. Suspected fatal animal attack. Body covered and marked. Attempting north crossing toward route exit. Names. Date as best he could track it. He did not write voices. He did not write mimic. He did not write the woods uses us.

Will watched his hand. "Put weather."

Joel added rain overnight, wind lowered but still strong.

Mike read it once, folded it into a plastic bag, and wedged it under a stone near the first note they had left for Alex. His hands were careful. He did not say anything over the body. None of them did. Silence there felt like the last decent thing they could give him and also the safest.

When they turned back, from somewhere near the waterline came a soft click.

Food barrel latch.

All three men looked at the barrel. It sat strapped in Mike's canoe, closed, visible, ten yards away in open light.

The click did not repeat.

Mike breathed through his nose. "Daylight," he said, as if daylight were a rule the thing had violated.

Will opened his mouth, then shut it.

Joel pointed at the barrel, then at his eyes, then made a short downward motion with his palm: seen. Done.

They went to the boats.

Three men in two canoes made every old habit wrong. They had known this since Alex died, but knowing it on shore was different from loading it into moving water. One boat wanted a second body in front. The other wanted Alex's uneven strength, his complaints, his big shoulders blocking the low spray. Instead there were packs where men should have been and men where the dead had made space.

Mike took his canoe alone. He loaded the food barrel and the heaviest packs forward to keep the front down, then shoved one blue dry bag farther ahead with his foot until it wedged under the seat. It was too much weight in the wrong place and still not enough. The empty back half made the canoe look sly and unbalanced, a boat pretending to be ready.

"You need ballast behind you too," Will said.

"I need the front down."

"You need trim."

"I know what trim is."

"I'm just saying--"

Will stopped. His own phrase had become a bad tool in his hand.

Mike softened by one degree. "I know. Hand me the tent."

Will handed it over.

Joel and Will took the other canoe. Will in front because that was the shape the boat still knew, because Joel steered, because changing one more thing seemed impossible. Joel tucked the notebook into his jacket pocket instead of the thwart bag. He wanted it on his body. He did not examine why.

They pushed off without a launch joke. No one said here we go. No one said nobody panic.

The first twenty yards were almost easy.

The camp slid behind them in pieces: wet tarp line, black spruce, the pale flash of the barrel rope left looped around a branch, the alder mouth. Joel kept his head forward. He did not turn for the orange tape. He could feel Will wanting to turn. The canoe carried the want through the slight hesitation in the front stroke.

"Eyes front," Joel said.

Will's shoulders rose. He paddled.

Mike moved to their right and a little behind, correcting too often. The solo canoe yawed with every wind push. He would take four hard strokes on one side, switch, dig, overcorrect, swear under his breath, then begin again. He was good enough to keep moving and tired enough to make it ugly.

Joel kept their bow angled toward the first black shoulder of rock across the water. It looked close when the wind paused and far when it rose. The lake had the color of old tea under a pewter sky. Low cloud pressed the far trees into a single dark line. Each paddle stroke lifted cold drops that blew back against Joel's hands.

No one spoke for a while.

That should have helped. It did not. Silence left too much room for water.

Sound carried differently in the open. Under trees, the night had made false rooms. Here the lake took every noise, flattened it, and slid it sideways. Mike's paddle slap came from the right, then seemed to come from behind Joel's ear. Will's blade knocked the gunwale once and the small wood sound ran ahead of them over the surface. A gull or raven called somewhere high and unseen. The call arrived without a body.

Joel watched Will's back. He had learned to read the front paddler by muscle and angle: Will's left shoulder dropping too fast when he was rushing, his wrist turning out when he was tired, the small pause before he switched sides. Now Joel watched for something else too. Confirmation. The living Will was in the front. The living Mike was behind and right. Alex was not anywhere the water could put him.

The first gust struck them past the lee of the point.

It did not hit like a wall. It leaned. The canoe slid sideways, then the bow rose over a short dark wave and slapped down. Spray blew across Will's chest. He gasped and kept paddling. Joel dug hard on the right to bring them back. The packs shifted behind Will with a wet rubber squeak.

"Keep the left working," Joel said.

Will nodded without looking back.

Mike fell farther behind.

"Mike," Will said.

Joel made a sharp sound with his tongue before he could stop himself.

Will flinched. "Sorry."

Joel looked back. Mike was visible, head down, shoulders pumping, his canoe angled wrong but not in immediate trouble. He lifted his paddle once, not a wave exactly. Alive. Seen. Joel returned the gesture and faced forward.

They crossed another hundred yards.

Halfway to the rock hump, from somewhere ahead and left, Mike's voice called, "It opens up after this."

The sentence came clean across the water.

It had Mike's working voice, not his argument voice. The practical tone he used at the mouth of a channel, at the start of a portage, when the route looked worse than he wanted to admit and he needed everyone to believe the next bend would justify the last one. It was not loud. It did not echo. It simply arrived in front of them.

Mike was behind them and to the right, head down, paddling.

Will stopped.

The canoe lost its rhythm at once. The bow swung left. A wave slapped the front quarter and threw water over Will's knees.

"Paddle," Joel said.

Will did not move. "That was--"

"Paddle."

"Mike said--"

"Mike is behind us."

Will turned halfway, too fast. His paddle blade lifted and caught the wind. The canoe rolled under them.

"Low," Joel snapped.

Will dropped his center of weight and grabbed the gunwales. For one second they were not crossing a lake but holding a table with the legs cut short, everything sliding toward the low edge. Joel braced, corrected, felt the hull resist and then come back.

"Paddle," he said again, quieter because quiet worked better now.

Will paddled. His strokes were too fast and shallow. The canoe yawed. Joel had to work against him, then with him, then against the water. His forearms burned in a narrow band. Cold water spread around his heels.

Behind them, the real Mike shouted something.

Joel did not answer. He looked over his shoulder long enough to see Mike still there, still in his own bad boat, one arm raised in a question. Joel pointed forward. Mike dropped his arm and dug in.

From the far trees came Will's own voice: "You guys hear that?"

Will made a noise low in his throat.

"Look at me," Joel said.

Will did not turn. His head had lowered between his shoulders.

"Will."

"That was me."

"No."

"That was me."

"The living you is in the boat. Paddle."

The sentence cut through. Joel saw it in the way Will's shoulders changed. Not comfort. Instruction. That was all they had. Will put the blade in and pulled.

The rock hump did not protect them. It was too low, more of a black back just under the skin of the lake than an island. Wind broke around it and came at them in short confused slaps. Mike tried to come up on their right and lost ground. Joel saw his canoe turn broadside for two bad seconds. Mike threw a hard draw stroke, cursed, and straightened out.

They were not going to make the planned landing.

Joel understood it before he let himself say it. The intended landing lay farther right, a notch where the map showed a short portage into the next chain. From shore it would have been obvious, maybe. From water it vanished into the repeating black teeth of spruce and rock. Every possible landing looked like a bruise. Every gap closed as they approached it.

"Right?" Will said.

"No."

"The portage is right."

"We won't make it."

"Then what are we doing?"

"Nearest gravel."

"That loses the route."

"So does swimming."

Will laughed once, not because it was funny. The laugh came out broken and stopped short. Joel heard the woods receive it. He hated that thought and had it anyway.

The next gust pushed them toward a low, steep shore where spruce roots gripped rock like black fingers. No landing there. Joel bore left, saw nothing, then saw a shallow fan of pale stones below a dead cedar leaning over the water. It was not a campsite. It was barely a place. It was enough to stop moving.

He pointed with the paddle.

Will saw it and nodded too many times.

"Don't jump early," Joel said.

Will nodded again.

They came in too fast.

The first stone struck the hull with a flat bang. Will threw his paddle across the gunwales and grabbed at a root. The root broke in his hand. The canoe slewed. Joel jammed his paddle down to pry them straight and felt the blade catch between stones. The shaft torqued hard. Pain ran up his wrist. For one stupid second he held on because losing a paddle felt impossible. Then the next wave lifted the stern and drove the canoe sideways.

"Let it go," Will shouted.

Joel let go.

The paddle popped free, spun once in the shallows, and drifted along the hull toward deeper water.

Will lunged for it.

"Don't," Joel said.

Will lunged anyway, belly over the front thwart, fingers scraping the blade. The canoe tipped toward him. Joel threw his weight back and grabbed Will's PFD with one hand.

"Will."

Will caught the shaft with two fingers, lost it, caught it again by the throat. He dragged it in and sat back hard, breathing through his mouth.

The blade had split near the edge. Not broken through, but cracked, a pale line in the dark wood.

"Nice," Joel said, because anger needed somewhere small to go.

Will stared at him. Then, absurdly, he said, "That was an expensive paddle."

"I'll invoice the lake."

The joke was poor. It still worked for half a second. Will's face twisted as if he might laugh, cry, or vomit. He did none of them.

Joel stepped out into thigh-deep water. Cold took the bones out of his legs. He grabbed the painter rope and hauled. Stones rolled under his sandals. The canoe resisted, then came up grinding. Will climbed out on the shore side and nearly went down, one hand still holding the cracked paddle like proof of something.

Mike came in badly.

He had seen their landing too late and tried to correct from too far downwind. His canoe ran in at an angle, front heavy with the barrel and packs. The bow struck a submerged rock with a sound that was not the hollow knock from the night but close enough to make Joel's stomach close. The boat stopped. Mike did not. He pitched forward onto one knee, caught himself on the center thwart, and the thwart cracked with a sharp wooden pop.

"Damn it," Mike said.

The word was too small for the day.

His canoe swung broadside in the wash. Joel splashed over and grabbed the front. Will got the back rope. Together they dragged it high enough that the next wave only slapped the stern. Mike stayed kneeling for one second, head lowered, both hands on the cracked thwart.

"You hurt?" Joel asked.

Mike shook his head.

"Look at me."
