Mike looked up, furious. Not at Joel. Not exactly. At needing to be checked like cargo.

"You hurt?"

"No."

Joel nodded. Sight before statement. Even for that.

They unloaded in pieces because pieces were all their hands could manage. Packs first. Barrel. Tent. Map case. Spare clothes. The blue dry bag from the front of Mike's boat had a dark wet seam along one side. Mike saw it and swore again, softer.

"It wasn't clipped," Will said.

"It was clipped."

"The roll wasn't."

"I rolled it."

"Not enough."

Mike rounded on him. "Do you want to do this now?"

Will's face changed. "Do what? Point out reality?"

"Reality is we landed."

"Reality is something used your voice while you were twenty yards behind us."

The wind moved through the spruce above them. Nobody answered that, which was a kind of answer.

Joel pulled their canoe above the stones and turned it sideways against the wind. His hands were numb. His mind felt too clear and not clear enough. He could see everything in front of him as separate facts. Two canoes. One cracked thwart. One cracked paddle. One wet dry bag. Three men. No Alex. No proper landing. No known camp. No one coming.

"Inventory," he said.

Mike looked at him as if the word belonged to some other trip.

"Gear first," Joel said. "Then map. Then camp."

"We should find the portage."

"With what dry hands?"

"It's north of here."

"Everything is north of something."

Will made a weak gesture with the cracked paddle. "The portage was east of the notch. We are not at the notch."

Mike rubbed both hands over his face. "I know where we are."

Will's eyes sharpened behind his wet glasses. He did not say it.

Joel said, "Maybe. We still make camp."

"It's morning."

"Emergency camp. Dry what we can. Fix what we can. Then decide."

Mike looked toward the tree line. The shore rose quickly into spruce and jack pine with no inviting space between them. Moss covered the first shelf of rock in dark soaked mats. Blowdown made a low fence ten yards inland. It was a bad place for camp, but bad had become a relative word.

From somewhere up the shore, Alex said, "Hold up."

The phrase was soft. Not a shout. Not even exactly a call. It had Alex's old impatience in it, the half-laugh he used when someone got ahead of the story he wanted to be part of. It came from beyond a screen of cedar and deadfall, the direction a person might walk if he had landed first and found something.

Will closed his eyes.

Mike turned his head toward the sound.

Joel caught his sleeve.

Mike looked down at Joel's hand. His face went hot and ugly. "I know."

Joel did not let go.

"I said I know."

Joel let go slowly.

The shore held still. No brush moved. No one said hold up again.

Will sat down on a wet stone as if his legs had quit. "It's using commands now."

"It's using what it has," Joel said.

Mike laughed under his breath. "Oh, great. That's better."

"It matters."

"Why?"

Joel looked at the broken cedar, the bad stones, the canoes pulled high and wrong. "Because it isn't making new things."

Will opened his eyes. "You don't know that."

"I know what we've heard."

"That's not the same."

"No."

Will swallowed. "There is a difference."

He said it without irony, and no one mocked him for it.

They sorted the wet bag under the cedar. It held Will's sleeping bag, a fleece, one pair of socks, and the spare fuel canister. The canister was fine. The fleece was wet along one arm. The socks were damp but salvageable. The sleeping bag had taken water along one side and into the footbox, not soaked through but bad enough that cold would own it by night if they did nothing.

Will knelt over it with both hands spread on the fabric. His face went blank in a practical way that was almost worse than panic.

"It's synthetic," he said.

"Good," Joel said.

"That is the entire good news."

"We'll wring it and hang it."

"In what sun?"

Joel looked at the sky. Low cloud. Wind. The occasional brightening that might become nothing.

"In what we've got."

Mike inspected the canoe. The front thwart had cracked on one side near the screw, not split free. It flexed when he pushed it.

"Usable," he said.

Will looked up. "For what?"

"For a canoe."

"That's an aspiration."

Mike's mouth opened, but the fight did not come. It died somewhere between his chest and teeth. He looked old for a second, older than forty, older than anyone should look while kneeling in wet sandals on a shore with a boat he had trusted.

"We can lash it," he said.

Joel nodded. "Paracord?"

"Repair kit."

Mike dug it out himself. Small roll of cord, tape, zip ties, a multi-tool, a packet of screws that suddenly seemed both ridiculous and precious. Joel held the thwart in place while Mike wrapped cord around the cracked joint and through the seat frame. His hands were clumsy from cold, but the work steadied him. Over-under. Pull. Cinch. Knot. Test. Practical motion returned his face to him by degrees.

Will propped the cracked paddle against a rock and examined the blade. "This can be taped."

"Then tape it," Mike said.

"I'm going to."

"Good."

"I said I'm going to."

Mike closed his eyes. "I heard you."

They worked in that strained quiet. The wind dried nothing and stole heat from everything. Joel's wet shorts clung to his thighs. His sandals had trapped grit under the straps, and each step ground it into skin. His hands shook when he tried to thread cord through a narrow gap. Hunger came and went without resolving into appetite.

At noon, or what felt like noon, they ate handfuls of trail mix because cooking meant unpacking too much and making too many sounds. Will chewed with his eyes on the trees. Mike stood while eating, unable to sit because sitting would admit they were stopped. Joel drank cold lake water through the filter and passed it without speaking.

The filter pump squeaked on the third stroke.

All three men froze.

Then Will, who held it, lifted the pump into view and squeezed it once more deliberately. Squeak. His face was white with anger at the object.

"Sight," he said.

Mike gave one short nod.

That became the method of the afternoon. When a buckle clicked, someone showed the buckle. When the taped paddle shifted against stone, Will lifted it. When the repaired thwart creaked under Mike's test, Mike pointed to it and did not pretend not to be afraid. Each confirmed sound narrowed the world a little. Each unconfirmed sound widened it again.

They found no real campsite. They made one.

Twenty yards inland, above the first wet shelf, the trees opened around a patch of thin duff and exposed root. It was just large enough for the tarp if they ran one line low between two black spruce and tied the other to a dead cedar that might hold through evening. The ground sloped. Water would find them if it rained again. There was no proper place for the tents, but one could go crooked behind a jack pine and the other could be half-pitched as gear shelter. Joel's hammock could hang if he accepted a bad angle and a trunk that leaned like it had regrets.

They carried gear up one load at a time. Nobody went alone. That rule established itself without discussion. Two men down, two men up, always one in sight of the other. It made every task take longer. It also kept them alive, or gave them the feeling of doing so.

Once, while Mike and Joel carried the food barrel, Will called from shore, "Wait."

Both men stopped.

Will stood below them beside the boats, one hand raised, fully visible.

"Real me," he said.

Mike almost smiled. "Unfortunately."

Will pointed to the far canoe. A loose painter rope had slipped and was tapping the hull in the wind. Tap. Tap. Tap. A small hollow knock. Ordinary. Accounted for.

Joel walked down and tied it tight anyway.

Across the lake, too far to be certain of any single tree or stone, the old camp lay hidden behind the point. Alex lay there. The distance was not large enough. It would never be large enough. Water moved between them without judgment, carrying light, carrying sound, carrying the shape of a man's voice if something asked it to.

Late in the afternoon the cloud thinned but did not break. They hung Will's sleeping bag from a line and watched it lift and sag in the wind like a tired flag. The wet side darkened the fabric. Drops gathered at the lowest seam and fell one by one into the moss.

Mike unfolded the map on a dry bag under the tarp. The three of them crouched around it. Nobody put a finger down until everyone could see the hand that did it.

"We're here," Mike said.

His finger touched a point west of the intended portage notch.

Will leaned close. "Maybe."

"We're here."

"Mike."

"What?"

"Say maybe."

Mike stared at the map. His throat moved. He did not clear it.

"Maybe we're here," he said.

The concession cost him more than shouting would have. Joel watched Will recognize that and choose not to press.

If Mike was right, the portage was less than a mile east along the shore. Less than a mile on a map. In wind, with damaged gear, after a bad landing, with the light already leaning away, less than a mile was not a distance. It was a trap wearing a number.

"We can walk shore and find it," Mike said.

"Today?" Will asked.

"Before dark."

Joel looked at the trees. Shore walking here meant slick rock, roots, blowdown, little coves that forced them up and around, no line of sight to the boats, and constant sound from water on stone. It meant one man wanting to scout ahead, one man telling him not to, one man hearing something that might be another man.

"Tomorrow," Joel said.

Mike's eyes moved to him.

"We have enough bad decisions for one day," Joel said.

Will gave a weak snort.

Mike did not. "If weather comes back, we're stuck."

"We're already stuck."

"We're one lake farther."

"And no safer."

That landed. Not as victory. There was no victory left in being right.

The wind pushed through the emergency camp and made the tarp lines tick. Joel turned his head at the sound. He saw the line, saw the knot, saw the small metal hook tapping the dead cedar. He reached up and wrapped the loose tail around the line until it stopped.

Mike watched him do it.

"Camp," Mike said at last.

Will lowered his head, either in relief or defeat.

They made the place as livable as a bad place could be. The repaired canoe stayed down by the stones, flipped and wedged with packs against the wind. The better canoe sat above it. Paddles came inland this time and lay under the tarp, cracked one taped and marked with a strip of orange cord so no one trusted it too much. The barrel went up only shoulder-high between two trees because there was no good branch and no one wanted to walk farther into the spruce. Mike hated the compromise. He made it anyway.

Joel collected small dead twigs from under cedar skirts, but everything was damp. They did not build a fire. Smoke would have felt human, and that temptation alone made him distrust it.

They ate without cooking again. Tortillas stiff from cold. Peanut butter squeezed from a pouch. Hard cheese sweating in its wrapper. Alex would have complained about all of it and then eaten more than anyone. Joel cut the cheese into three portions and almost made a fourth. His hand paused over the knife until Will looked away.

Evening came early under the trees.

Not dark yet. Worse. The hour when every object still had shape but no detail, when the mind began improving what the eye could not finish. The lake below them showed between trunks in strips of dull metal. The bad crossing lay behind those strips, completed but not survived in any way that felt final.

Will took out the notebook.

Joel let him.

Will turned to the page with the columns and added in small block letters beneath Mike:

IT OPENS UP AFTER THIS - WATER - NOT MIKE

Under Will he wrote:

YOU GUYS HEAR THAT - FAR SHORE - NOT ME

He hesitated, then under Alex:

HOLD UP - SHORE - NOT ALEX

The names made the denials look childish and necessary. Not Mike. Not me. Not Alex. The page was beginning to fill with refusals.

Mike leaned in and read them. "Add the latch this morning."

Joel glanced at him.

Mike looked annoyed by the glance. "What? It happened."

Will wrote:

LATCH - DAYLIGHT - BARREL VISIBLE

Then he stopped, pen hovering. "Should we write what it wants?"

"No," Joel said.

Will looked at him.

"We don't know," Joel said.

"It wants us to move wrong."

"Maybe."

"It wants us to answer."

"Maybe."

Mike said, "It wants us dead."

Joel looked toward the lake. "Everything out here can do that without wanting."

No one liked the sentence. Joel did not either. But it felt closer to the truth than Mike's version, or more useful. Want gave the thing a human center. Want made it something you could bargain with, enrage, outthink in a clean way. What they had was worse and smaller: repetition, opportunity, hunger maybe, a shape of attention without a mind they could trust.

Will wrote at the bottom of the page:

REPETITION, NOT INTENTION?

He put a question mark after it and underlined nothing.

For a while they sat with that.

The first knock came from the lake after full dusk.

One hollow tap. Wood on hull, or paddle on stone, or the memory of either. The boats were visible below as two pale undersides in the last light. The paddles lay under the tarp at Joel's feet.

Mike turned his head.

Will looked at the paddles.

Joel put one hand on the cracked blade. Sight.

The knock came again, farther back across the water, or farther down the shore, or from no distance the ear could defend.

No one moved.

It was not a cliff edge. It was not a new revelation. It was only the day returning in miniature: a sound they knew, placed where it had no right to be, asking them to make the world whole by walking toward it.

They did not.

After a time the lake quieted. The tarp ticked once, and Joel found the line with his eyes. Will closed the notebook and slid it back into the plastic bag. Mike sat with the nearly empty bear spray beside his knee, not in his hand.

They had crossed one bad piece of water and lost the comfort of believing motion meant escape.

By dark, that was the chapter of the day: one lake farther, wet, damaged, accounted for, and no safer than before.


# Chapter 13 - Emergency Camp

Emergency Camp had no fire ring and no mercy.

It was not a camp until they had made enough mistakes around it to call it one. Before that it was only a steep poor shore, a wet shelf of root and stone, two canoes dragged up out of reach of the chop, and three men trying not to look toward the places where sound gathered.

The afternoon thinned without becoming warmer. Wind came over the lake in hard panels and broke against the spruce, shaking water loose from the branches long after the rain had ended. Every gust made the tarp snap unless Joel kept the line in his hand. The line had been too short for a proper pitch, then too long after he retied it, then the wrong angle once Mike moved the gear under it. Nothing wanted to fit. The place accepted their work the way rock accepted water: by letting it run over and away.

They set the tents where roots rose highest from the mud. One tent went crooked between a jack pine and a spruce with half its stakes buried sideways under stones. The other would not pitch at all because the ground fell away under one corner, so they made it into a gear shelter, fly pulled low and patched where the fabric had abraded against a pack buckle during the landing. The silver repair tape shone in the dim light like a wound dressed by someone who had never believed in medicine.

"This is ridiculous," Will said.

He said it flatly, without heat. That made it worse.

"It's shelter," Mike said.

"It is fabric losing an argument with gravity."

"Then argue quieter."

Will looked at him, and for a second Joel thought the old exchange would build itself out of habit. Will would explain load angles or water runoff. Mike would tell him to stop narrating weather. Joel would say something small enough to pass as help. Alex would make it a performance, would step back from the sagging fly and judge it like bad theater, would call them pioneers in the dumbest possible voice.

The space where Alex would have stood remained open.

Joel tightened the tarp line again. The hook at the dead cedar ticked once against bark. He saw it, wrapped the loose tail around the line, and stopped the sound.

Sight.

That was the word under everything now. Not faith. Not reason. Not even trust. Sight was smaller and meaner and more useful. Sight was the barrel visible when the latch clicked. Sight was Mike behind them when Mike's voice had come from ahead. Sight was Will in the front of the canoe while Will's own question moved out of the trees. Sight was what the world had been reduced to after sound became a bad witness.

They had written that in the notebook and still kept forgetting, because men had lived by answering one another for forty years and rules made in a morning did not cut deep enough by evening.

The wet sleeping bag hung from a line between two spruce trunks, lifted and dropped by the wind. It was Will's. The footbox had taken water when the dry bag failed, and the dark wet strip along one side did not shrink no matter how many times they wrung it. Will stood under it with both hands in his armpits, watching it as if attention might dry fabric.

"Synthetic," he said again.

No one answered. They had used the good news already.

Mike knelt beside his canoe under the low tarp, redoing the repair on the cracked thwart. The first lashing from the shoreline had held, but he did not trust it. Trust had become one more thing they did not have enough of. He had taken the repair sleeve from the kit and cut two thin strips of aluminum with the multi-tool, working the metal back and forth until it gave. Now he set the strips along the split wood, braced them with a tent stake, and wrapped cord through the seat frame with furious care.

It was the best thing about him and the worst. Problems became objects. Objects could be held, splinted, forced back toward function. If the damage had an edge, a screw, a wet seam, a cracked place, Mike could give himself to it completely and become almost gentle.

His fingers shook from cold. He pretended they did not.

Will watched too closely.

"It's a field repair," Mike said without looking up.

"I didn't say anything."

"You were breathing critically."

"There is a difference between skepticism and breathing."

Joel almost smiled. The almost hurt.

He wrote the line down later because he knew the thing outside might have heard it. He hated that this was now what affection did: took a living man's small joke and filed it with the rest of the ammunition. He hated himself for thinking of Will as material while Will was still alive, cold, standing ten feet away with wet glasses and hands that could not stop finding tasks.

The repair held when Mike leaned on it. It creaked, but the creak came from the canoe in front of them, visible, accounted for. Mike leaned again, harder than necessary.

"Don't prove a point by breaking it," Joel said.

Mike glanced up. "That's what field repairs are for."

"For breaking?"

"For finding out before you're in the middle of the lake."

Will's mouth opened. Joel could see the correction rise, technical and helpless. Then Will shut it again. The effort showed in his jaw.

Mike saw it too. "Say it or don't."

"I was not going to say anything."

"You were absolutely going to say something."

"Fine. I was going to say testing to failure is not the same as checking."

"Congratulations. You said it."

Will looked toward the trees as if ashamed of the sound of his own voice.

That was new, and because it was new, Joel noticed it more sharply than the argument. Will had always filled air the way some men fed a stove. He put words into space and warmed himself by them. Now each sentence left him looking after it, frightened of where it might go.

The wind pushed through the tarp and brought lake smell with it: cold tannin water, bruised cedar, mud opened by their sandals. Joel's socks were damp inside his camp shoes. His wet shorts had dried only at the edges and remained cold at the waistband. Hunger came over him in shallow waves and broke before it became appetite.

They cooked because they had to warm something. It took too long. The stove sputtered in the wind, blue flame flattening under the pot, then flaring when Mike moved his pack as a shield. The click of the igniter had made all three of them freeze before flame took. Joel saw the stove. Mike saw the stove. Will pointed at it with two fingers anyway, a small bitter ceremony.

"Sight," Will said.

"Yeah," Joel said.

"We are going to become insane in a very organized way."

"We're ahead of schedule."

Will gave one breath that might have become a laugh if it had more strength. He held it in. The held-in thing seemed to remain between them.

They ate rice from the pot because bowls were packed somewhere stupid. Mike wanted to keep food simple, no smells, no waste, no mess. Will wanted more salt and did not ask. Joel scraped the bottom with a spoon and divided the browned, stuck layer into three equal pieces. He almost made a fourth. His hand moved before thought did.

No one mentioned it.

Alex had complained about food constantly and eaten constantly. He had treated every bland meal as a personal betrayal and then gone back for more, making noises over tortillas and cheese like a man forced to survive a hostage situation. Joel had wanted to tell him to shut up at least twice a day. Now the quiet around the food was so complete it felt staged.

Mike sealed the pot in the kitchen bag and reached for the barrel. The latch snapped under his thumb.

Will flinched.

Mike lifted the barrel toward him. "Me."

"I know."

"I'm right here."

"I said I know."

Mike set the barrel down too hard. "Don't make me responsible for every noise."

Will's face went white in a way that had nothing to do with cold. "I am trying to keep track of reality."

"So am I."

"No, you're trying to pin it down and yell at it until it becomes a route problem."

Mike stood. The tarp was too low, and his shoulder struck it, shaking cold water onto all three of them.

Joel put a hand up. Not between them, exactly. Just up.

The gesture worked because everyone wanted it to. Mike turned away first. Will sat back on his heels and pushed both hands through his hair. The rainwater from the tarp had spotted his glasses again. He took them off and wiped them on a shirt already too damp to help.

From somewhere behind the camp, in the spruce above the root shelf, Alex said, "Hold up."

Will turned before Joel could stop him.

It was not dramatic. That was the horror of it. No shout. No scream. Just Will's body obeying an old arrangement: a friend calls, you turn. His shoulders twisted toward the dark. His head lifted. For less than a second he looked not afraid but ready, almost relieved, as if the world had made a mistake and was about to correct it by sending Alex down through the trees, wet and loud and too pleased with himself.

Mike grabbed Will's shoulder.

"No."

Will stopped so sharply his glasses fell from his hand and landed in the duff.

The voice came again, softer. "Hold up."

It stood behind them in the trees with Alex's impatience in it. The little lift at the end, the half-command, half-request. The exact sound of him at a beaver dam, at a landing, catching a painter rope, making everybody wait because he had found a better way or a worse joke.

Will whispered, "Alex."

Mike's fingers tightened on his shoulder. "No. It isn't."

Those words changed the camp more than the voice had.

They had all known it in pieces. Joel had written denials in the notebook. Will had said repetition and maybe recombination. Mike had conceded uncertainty, maybe, with the map in front of him and the water behind him. But Mike had not given up the world out loud. He had kept bear as long as he could. He had kept weather, shock, echo, grief, mishearing, any hard word with a normal body behind it.

Now he stood with one hand on Will's shoulder and said that their dead friend's voice was not their dead friend.

Will bent slowly and picked up his glasses. He did not put them on right away.

Nothing moved in the spruce.

Joel took one step sideways until he could see both men and the black line of trees beyond them. He wanted to write. The need came so strongly it embarrassed him. His hand went to the notebook in his jacket as if paper were a weapon. It was not a weapon. It was a place to put what sound had ruined.

Mike let go of Will.

"You okay?" he said.

Will looked at the ground. "That's a stupid question."

"I know."

"Then why ask it?"

"Because I don't have a better one."

Will put on his glasses. One lens was smeared dark. "No."

Mike nodded once. "Okay."

That was all the comfort they managed.

Joel opened the notebook under the tarp with his body blocking what little wind came through. The pages had swollen at the edges. Pencil still worked better than pen. He turned past the existing columns, then back again because the columns mattered. Alex. Mike. Will. Camp. Joel had added his own name last night and wished he had not, as if writing it had made an opening.

Under Alex he wrote:

HOLD UP - BEHIND CAMP - NOT ALEX

He hesitated and added:

TWO TIMES

Will crouched beside him. "It used the same phrase at the shore."

Joel nodded and wrote:

SHORE BEFORE CAMP

"Same voice?"

Joel did not answer. He looked at Will.

Will closed his eyes. "Right. Sorry."

Mike came over and knelt on the other side. "Put no movement."

Joel looked up.

"No brush. No steps." Mike's face was hard with the effort of being accurate. "If we're writing evidence, write evidence."

Joel wrote:

NO VISIBLE MOVEMENT

Will leaned closer. "That's not evidence of absence. That's only absence of observed movement."

Mike stared at him.

Will held up both hands. "I'm not being cute."

"No one thought you were being cute."

"I'm saying we should be precise."

"Write a peer-reviewed article after we get out."

Will's face tightened. "Precision is not the enemy here."

"Talking might be."

The sentence landed harder than Mike seemed to intend. It was too close to true and too blunt to survive as an ordinary insult.

Will looked away first.

Joel wrote:

LESS TALK.

Then, because the phrase looked like command and because commands had become suspect, he crossed it out and wrote beneath:

TALK ONLY WHEN NEEDED.

The page did not make that easier. It only made it visible.

For a while the emergency camp held itself around them. Wind. Tarp. The lake below speaking in its low side-on way against the stones. Will's sleeping bag lifting and dropping. The repaired thwart creaking once as the canoe settled under its own bad fix. Each sound required a glance. Each glance used energy. Fear had become clerical.

Will took the pencil from Joel and wrote in the margin:

IT REPEATS.

Then he stopped, pencil tip pressed hard enough to darken the paper.

"Maybe not only repeats," he said.

Joel looked at him.

Will swallowed. The words wanted out of him. You could see them backing up behind his teeth, crowding him. He was a man built out of distinctions, and the distinction had weight.

"Maybe it can recombine," he said, barely above breath. "Not invent. Not like us. But if it has enough sound. Phonemes. Bits. Tone. It might be able to assemble something close to new. Like speech synthesis, only not, not technically, because that implies a model and training data and I know that sounds insane but listen, if it can take Alex's voice and place a phrase where Alex never stood, then maybe the phrase is fixed but the use is not. It could be building from pieces."

"Will," Joel said.

"No, that's important."

"I know."

"Then let me finish."

"Don't give it more."

Will's mouth stayed open.

The warning had not been sharp. Joel wished it had been. Sharpness would have given Will something to argue with. Instead it came out tired and almost kind, and Will received it like a hand against his chest.

Mike said, "He's right."

Will turned on him. "You don't even know what I'm saying."

"I know you're feeding the tape recorder."

"It is not a tape recorder."

"Great. It's a magic tape recorder."

"That's exactly the kind of sloppy thinking that gets people killed."

"People are already killed."

Silence took the sentence at once. Mike looked as if he would take it back if the woods had not already heard it.

Will stood and walked two steps out from under the tarp, then stopped because two steps was as far as the rule allowed without making everyone follow. His shoulders shook once. Not sobbing, or not quite. Cold, rage, grief, all of it using the same muscles.

Joel wanted to say Alex's name. Not to answer the thing. Not to call him. Only to put the name where it belonged, with them, in the camp, among the living who had known him. But the name felt like food left out.

So he wrote it instead at the top of the column, already there, already insufficient.

Alex.

The pencil mark did not sound like him.

Dusk lost the trees by degrees. First the separate needles went away. Then the branches became large black hands. Then the spaces between trunks filled until the woods were one thing standing above camp. The lake kept a little light longer, holding it in dull strips between the lower trunks. The emergency camp seemed to shrink as the light left it, tarp and tents and men drawn tighter against the dark.

They brought the paddles fully under the tarp. Joel's cracked paddle had been taped and marked with orange cord, a warning to the hand. Will checked the tape twice and then stopped because checking became another kind of fear. Mike tied both painter ropes off again down by the canoes and climbed back up wet to the knees.

"Boats are high," he said.

Will nodded.

"Ropes tied."

Joel nodded.

"Barrel's as good as it's getting."

The barrel hung shoulder-high between two trees, a humiliating compromise. No clean branch. No good distance from camp. No one willing to go deeper into the spruce at night to perform proper food storage for an animal they no longer believed was the main problem. Mike had tied it anyway, because not tying it would have meant surrendering one of the last ordinary rules.

Will glanced toward it. "If a bear comes, it gets a nice convenient snack."

"If a bear comes, we can almost call that refreshing," Mike said.

No one laughed. The line deserved a laugh from some earlier version of them.

The tent zipper sounded from the gear shelter.

All three men turned.

The shelter sat five yards away, half pitched low, its patched fly silver at one corner. The zipper pull hung at the door where Will had left it. It hung still.

Then the zipper moved again.

Not the whole length. Not a theatrical opening. Just three careful teeth separating, the small dry rasp of fabric and metal. The door gap widened by an inch, maybe less, as if an invisible finger had found the pull and tested whether they would look.

They looked.

Looking was allowed.

Will made a small sound through his nose.

Joel raised one hand, palm down. Stay.

Mike's face had gone empty in the way it did when he was very angry. He took one step toward the shelter.

Joel caught his sleeve.

Mike did not turn. His arm hardened.

Joel shook his head.

The zipper did not move. The shelter showed no shape inside but bags, a coil of rope, the corner of a blue dry bag, the pale round lid of a pot. All visible. All ordinary. All arranged into the kind of scene a man could ruin by stepping into it too quickly.

"Wind," Mike said, without conviction.

Will whispered, "No."

Mike looked at him.

Will pointed. His hand shook. "Pull tab is tucked under the flap. Wind can't get that angle."

"Don't," Joel said.

Will's eyes flashed. "I'm identifying a mechanical constraint."

"Quietly."

Will closed his mouth.

Mike stepped back under the tarp. That was harder for him than going forward would have been. Joel could see the effort in his neck, the humiliation of retreat from a three-inch zipper sound. But he did it.

The gear shelter remained half open. No one fixed it.

After full dark, the paddle knock began below them.

One hollow tap from the shore.

Joel's hand went at once to the paddles at his feet. The cracked one. The spare. Will's. Mike's. He touched each shaft as if counting heads. All there.

Tap.

The sound came again from lower down, near the canoes or beyond them. Wood on wood, maybe. Paddle against gunwale. Hull settling on stone. The lake putting a small thing in motion. There were enough possible causes to build a reasonable world from, and none of them had the shape of the sound in the dark.

Mike reached for the bear spray and stopped with his hand beside it.

"Boats are high," Joel said.

Mike looked at him.

"Ropes tied. Paddles here. Sight."

Tap.

Will leaned forward, elbows on knees, hands over his ears without fully covering them. "It's using camp."

Joel nodded.

"Not us. Camp."

"It heard camp too."

"Jesus."

Mike said, "Maybe it wants us to check the boats."

"Maybe it wants us to answer," Will said.

Joel looked at the dark below the trees. Want was a dangerous word. It put a person inside the sound. It gave the dark a center, a plan, a face it had not earned. Everything out here could kill without wanting. Cold did not want. Wind did not want. Water did not want. A bear could want only in the simple body sense of hunger or fear, and even that was not malice. Want made the thing too human.

He took the pencil and wrote:

DO NOT ASSUME WANT.

Will read it and gave a short, bitter nod. "Fine. Function."

Joel wrote:

FUNCTION: MAKE US MOVE / ANSWER / SEPARATE?

The question mark mattered. He pressed it into the paper.

Mike crouched closer, rain jacket whispering. "Add old cabin."

Will looked at him sharply. "What?"

"Tomorrow."

"No."

"We can't sit here."

"And we can't just chase a square on an antique map because the lake scared you."

Mike's throat worked. He did not clear it. Joel noticed that. The swallowed habit, the authority denied its own sound.

"It isn't because I'm scared of the lake," Mike said.

Will gave him a look so exhausted it had almost no anger left. "Mike."

"The canoe is damaged. The paddle is damaged. Your bag is wet. Weather's not stable. We're off the route and we missed the portage landing. If we keep hugging shore looking for it, we spend half a day in the wind beside water where voices carry. If we cross again wrong, we lose a boat or one of us goes in. The old map puts a maintenance cabin inland from the next portage. Maybe there's a trace. Maybe there's a road."

"Maybe there's a fairy goddamn godmother."

"Map says there's a cabin."

The phrase came out smaller than when he had said it before. Not declaration. Not certainty. A man offering up the last solid object he had.

Joel felt Will beside him gather for the correction. Map says there was a cabin. Map says someone once paid a printer. Map says a lot of things. Joel could hear all of it, and because he could hear it he knew the woods might too.

"Tomorrow," Joel said.

Both men looked at him.

He wished he had a better reason. He wished he believed in the cabin. What he believed was narrower: they could not move at night; they could not stay for another full day without becoming colder and more stupid; the water had nearly killed them in daylight; the woods were no safer but at least a trace would put solid ground under their feet. The cabin did not have to be real. It only had to be the next testable human mark.

"We look in daylight," Joel said. "All three. No scouting alone. If it is nothing, we come back."

"Come back to what?" Will asked.

Joel did not answer.

The paddle knock stopped.

That made the silence after it feel selected.

Mike rubbed both hands over his face. "We need sleep."

Will looked at the hanging bag. "That's optimistic."

"Rest, then."

"Also optimistic."

"Horizontal misery," Joel said.

Will stared at him for a second, then gave a laugh so small it barely had air in it. The laugh frightened him as soon as it left. He looked toward the trees.

No answer came.

They arranged the night by sight and exhaustion. Nobody called it a watch schedule because schedules belonged to people who expected the future to obey an order. Mike took the first sitting position under the tarp with the bear spray beside him but not in his hand. Joel would stay in the hammock at the bad angle and try to rest without closing himself away from the others. Will would take the one pitched tent, because his sleeping bag needed body heat and because arguing about fairness would only produce more sound.

Will objected anyway.

"You take it," he told Joel.

"No."

"Your hammock is garbage."

"Your bag is wet."

"That's not a moral category."

"It's a heat category."

Will looked too tired to appreciate his own kind of language coming back at him. He ducked into the tent and stopped before closing the zipper. The pull was between his fingers. All three of them watched it.

"This is me," he said.

"Seen," Joel said.

Will drew the zipper slowly, then stopped halfway and left it there. "I don't want to make the whole sound."

No one told him that half a door was stupid. Fear had its own engineering now.

Joel climbed into the hammock. The hang was wrong, shoulder higher than hip, fabric pinching behind his knees. Cold came through the underquilt where it had been compressed against his wet clothes. Above him the tarp sagged inches from his face and shivered with each gust. He could see Mike's boots, the orange can of spray, the notebook in its plastic bag beside the stove. He could see the half-open gear shelter. He could see Will's tent wall, pale in the dark under the tarp edge, moving softly when Will shifted inside.

He could not see Alex.

That remained the basic fact. Not from camp. Not under the tarp. Not on the shore. Not behind the spruce. The living did not become more available because the dead could be sounded out of the dark.

Joel took the notebook from the bag and wrote by headlamp under his jacket so the light stayed small.

Alex:
HOLD UP - SHORE / CAMP - NOT ALEX

Mike:
IT OPENS UP AFTER THIS - WATER - NOT MIKE

Will:
YOU GUYS HEAR THAT - FAR SHORE - NOT WILL

Camp:
LATCH - DAYLIGHT - BARREL VISIBLE
ZIPPER - GEAR SHELTER - NOT WIND?
PADDLE KNOCK - PADDLES UNDER TARP

He paused over the last column.

Joel:

The blank after his name seemed louder than the lake. He had given it less, maybe. Not nothing. No one living among others gave nothing. Wait. Just wait. Eyes front. Paddle. Small jokes. His voice had moved over the same water, under the same trees. If sound could be kept by whatever listened, then parts of him were already out there, stored without permission.

He did not write that.

On a clean page he wrote in block letters:

DO NOT ANSWER.

He looked at the sentence. It was too simple and not simple enough. A dead friend could ask. A living friend could call from out of sight. A boat could knock. A zipper could open. A bear could actually come. The rule was not a rule for survival so much as a rule for choosing which danger to prefer.

He added beneath it:

UNLESS SEEN.

Then he crossed that out.

Too late for that. The living men could be seen and still have their voices stolen elsewhere. The rule was not answer because of sound. Answer only to the body in front of you, to gesture, to what eyes could hold and hands could touch.

He wrote:

CONFIRM WITH SIGHT. THEN MOVE.

Mike leaned over from his sitting place and read it upside down. His face in the headlamp was all planes and grime, eyes sunk deep.

"That's the plan?" he said.

"For tonight."

Mike looked toward Will's tent, then the trees, then the lake. "Great plan."

Joel waited.

Mike rubbed at the bridge of his nose. "I don't have a better one."

From inside the tent, Will said, "That's going in the minutes."

His voice was muffled by nylon, ordinary and exhausted and alive. Joel saw the shape of him through the tent wall, one knee raised, one elbow moving as he fought the wet sleeping bag around himself. Sight gave the voice back to him for one second.

Then, beyond the camp, low among the spruce, Alex laughed once.

Not loud. Not the full bark. A small breath of it, as if he had heard Will and could not help himself.

Will went still inside the tent.

Mike's hand closed around the bear spray.

Joel put the notebook page where both men could see it, black letters in the narrow light.

DO NOT ANSWER.

No one did.

The laugh did not repeat. The lake worked below them. The tarp ticked once, and Joel found the line with his eyes. The food barrel hung where they had left it, too low and visible between two trunks. The gear shelter's zipper remained half open. The canoes down by the shore were pale shapes turned away from the dark water. Nothing moved that could be trusted. Nothing spoke that had to be believed.

They held their silence until it became less like courage and more like another kind of labor.

By the time Mike's head began to lower against his chest and Will's shivering settled into uneven breaths, Joel had understood the cabin for what it was. Not rescue. Not safety. A square on an old map, a human mark in a place that was steadily taking human things apart: voices, routes, jokes, certainty, the clean usefulness of saying a name.

In the morning they would put stones on the map corners and pretend paper still knew how to tell the world what to be.


# Chapter 14 - The Old Map

In the morning Mike spread the old map on a wet rock and put stones on the corners.

The rock was not flat enough. Nothing there was. The lake had dropped in the night or seemed to have, leaving a black line on the stones where water had held and withdrawn. The canoes lay higher than the mark, pale and cold under spruce shadow. The tarp sagged over the root shelf. Will's sleeping bag hung like an animal skin no one had finished cleaning. Every object they owned looked borrowed from a worse version of the same trip.

Joel stood with his arms folded against his chest and watched water gather in the creases of the map case. The plastic was clouded from old scratches. Under it the printed lakes and portages held their clean colors, blue and green and brown arranged with confidence. Little broken lines crossed land as if land had agreed to be crossed. A square sat inland from the portage they had missed the day before, small enough to be nothing, exact enough to become hope.

Maintenance cabin, the old legend said.

The print was thin at the edges. A previous fold ran directly through the word cabin, making the letters step over a white break.

Mike had found the map before coffee. He had not slept, or had slept only in dropped pieces. His eyes were dark under his cap. The stubble along his jaw had come in gray and uneven. He held himself with the hard attention of a man who had woken before doubt could get up with him.

"Here," he said.

Will did not bend closer. He stood above the rock with both hands tucked under his armpits, one palm bandaged from a small cut the night before where he had caught himself against granite. The bandage had already loosened at one edge. Damp made every repair temporary.

Joel could see the old route as Mike saw it. Emergency Camp below them at the wrong shore. The missed portage north and west along the lake, reachable if the wind stayed down and the repaired canoe behaved and no sound pulled them apart at the landing. The cabin inland from that portage, connected by a faint black line to something marked as an old fire road. Past that, perhaps a road a truck could use. Perhaps a ditch. Perhaps a memory of a road. Perhaps nothing.

Paper was good at perhaps. It held all possibilities in the same ink.

"We leave the boats cached," Mike said. "Take what we can carry for two days. Repair kit, first aid, filter, stove, one pot, food. Sleeping bags. Tarp. Map. Compass. If the cabin has anything useful, we use it. If there is a road, we follow it. If there isn't, we come back."

"That's a lot of if," Will said.

"Everything is if."

"No." Will leaned down then, because he could not resist the map even when he hated it. "Some things are distances. Some things are contours. Some things are false hope printed in black by a guy in an office thirty years ago."

Mike's throat moved. He did not clear it.

Joel noticed the restraint because the habit had become visible by its absence. A day earlier he might not have known what Mike was stopping. Now the unsounded thing seemed to stand between them with the rest of the sounds they were trying not to give away.

"You want to paddle big water with a cracked boat and voices?" Mike asked.

Will looked toward the lake.

The lake had eased in the night but not forgiven them. Small wind marks ran over it from the middle out, darkening and smoothing by turns. The far shore had no scale. Trees lined up there in black ranks, all of them looking close until Joel imagined crossing and understood again how distance hid itself on water. The voice last night had not needed a body to cross that distance. Sound carried where men could not.

"No," Will said.

Mike waited.

"I don't want to walk into wet brush because of a square," Will said. "Those are different categories of stupid."

"Great. Pick a category."

Will's mouth tightened. He had slept in the least wet bag and looked worse for it, as if warmth had only given his body more room to shake. "The map says there was a cabin."

"Map says there's a cabin."

"You hear the difference?"

"I hear you making every sentence longer."

Joel crouched beside the rock. The old map had been opened and folded so often the route wore a softness like cloth. He touched the plastic above the square, then drew his finger along the faint line toward the fire road. Under the case, the line looked deliberate. On the ground it would be alder, blowdown, wet leaves, rotten corduroy maybe, old tire ruts full of black water. A human mark did not mean human presence. It meant only that at some time, somebody had wanted the same thing they wanted now: a way through.

"If the road is gone?" Joel asked.

Mike looked at him with a flash of relief. A practical question was a handhold.

"Then we turn around before dark."

"Before dark," Will said.

"Yes."

"We have become excellent at arriving places before dark."

Mike folded his lips in, held them there, and let the line pass. That was new too. He did not have energy to punish every challenge. Or he had begun to understand that every argument became inventory for whatever listened.

Joel took the notebook from the plastic bag. Its pages had dried in warped ridges near the spine. He turned past the columns from the night before. Alex. Mike. Will. Camp. Joel. The names made a poor shelter. They had held facts for a few hours and nothing more.

On a clean page he wrote:

CABIN PLAN - DAYLIGHT ONLY.

ALL THREE.

NO SCOUTING.

Mike watched the pencil. "Add return by dusk."

Joel wrote:

TURN BACK IF NO CABIN / NO TRACE BY MIDDAY.

Will made a small sound.

Joel looked up.

"Midday is optimistic," Will said.

"Then say it with the pencil."

Will took the notebook. He held the pencil awkwardly in his bandaged hand, then shifted it to the other and wrote in block letters:

OLD MAP MAY BE WRONG.

He pushed the notebook back.

Mike read it. "That's useful."

"It is."

"It's just pessimism with handwriting."

"No, it's a constraint."

Joel wrote beneath it:

ASSUME WRONG UNTIL SEEN.

That quieted them for a moment. It was the rule they had been making without wanting to admit it applied to more than voices. Assume wrong until seen. A friend's voice. A latch. A boat knock. A square on a map. A line through trees. The world had become a thing that needed witnesses.

Mike looked away first. "Pack."

The word had enough command in it to organize the next hour.

They made coffee because coffee was a normal human act and because hands needed heat. The stove click made all three of them glance down before flame came. Will pointed at it with two fingers, the small ceremony from the night before repeated without speech. Seen. The blue flame flattened in the lake wind, then steadied when Joel set his pack as a shield. They drank from metal cups that tasted faintly of old rice. No one wanted food, so they ate bars and the last of a bag of trail mix by count, three shares laid on the map rock with one bright raisin rolling toward the edge.

Joel almost stopped it with Alex's name in his mouth.

He caught the raisin instead and put it on Will's pile.

Will saw. His face changed and changed back. He ate it without comment.

Packing for a possible exit was harder than packing for a route. A route allowed redundancy. You carried extra socks, extra line, an extra headlamp, a book you thought you might read on a point in evening light. Escape made every object argue for itself. The first aid kit mattered. The repair kit mattered if they came back to boats. The stove mattered if they were out overnight and wet. Food mattered. Sleeping bags mattered, though Will's was still damp at the foot and would add weight without granting much mercy. The tarp mattered. The tent body did not. A spare paddle did not, unless the road failed and the boats waited and the cracked paddle gave up. A fishing rod had no argument left in it.

Will held his rod tube for a long time, then set it under the gear shelter.

"Don't say anything," he said.

Mike lifted both hands.

"I mean it."

"I didn't say anything."

"Your face said a short essay."

"My face is conserving language."

Will stared at him, then gave one dry breath. Not a laugh. A failed start at one.

The small almost-laugh frightened everyone less than the real one would have. Joel felt the old friendship move under the fear like water under late ice. Still there. Still dangerous to trust.

They sorted the food barrel into a smaller dry bag and two side bags. Mike wanted to take more than they could carry and less than they might need. Will wanted the bear line, not because he believed in proper storage anymore but because abandoning ordinary precautions felt like inviting every kind of failure at once. Joel wanted weight low and simple. The argument had no clean winner. They took two days of food, the line, and the barrel lid's smell on everything.

The food barrel latch snapped when Mike closed it for the last time.

All three men looked toward the hanging barrel in the trees.

It hung open.

The sound had come from Mike's hands. They had seen it. Still, Will's shoulders rose to his ears.

"Mine," Mike said.

"Seen," Joel said.

Will nodded too fast.

They cached what they could not carry under spruce boughs and the low fly of the failed gear shelter. Cache was too grand a word for a pile of wet equipment hidden from nothing that wanted it and very little that understood it. Joel packed the tents in one dry bag, the rod tube, the spare cookware, extra clothes too wet to justify their weight, Alex's remaining odds and ends sealed inside the cracked headlamp bag because no one could decide who should carry them. He put Alex's hot sauce bottle there too. It had rolled out from a side pocket when Mike shook out a pack, red plastic bright against the moss.

Will looked at it. "He would haunt us for leaving that."

No one answered. They were already being haunted by less.

Joel slipped the bottle into the bag. The cap made a tiny click against the headlamp case. He saw it. He wished seeing helped with memory.

Down by the shore, they worked on the canoes. The damaged one went first, dragged higher above the waterline with all three of them on the gunwales. The repaired thwart creaked under its own accusation. Mike flinched at the sound, then set his jaw and lifted again.

"Up here," he said.

"That's visible from the water," Will said.

"Good. We need to find it again."

"Also visible to anything with eyes."

"Have you seen eyes?"

Will looked at him. "Is that the standard now?"

Mike did clear his throat then, once, quiet and real.

Joel hated how his body reacted to it. Every muscle around his ribs tightened before thought arrived. Mike was three feet away. His mouth had made the sound. His hand was on the canoe. The sound belonged to him and still did not feel safe, because belonging had become a thing that could be counterfeited.

"We need them not to float away," Mike said. "We need to find them if we come back. We don't need to build a museum exhibit."

"I'm just saying."

"I know. You are always just saying."

The old rhythm rose and failed. Alex should have cut in there. He would have given Will a title, made Mike defend the stupidest possible interpretation of his own words, turned the canoe cache into a bit about survival influencers with discount hatchets. Joel could hear the shape of it without sound. That was grief's cruelty: it made an imitation inside you and called it memory.

They pulled spruce boughs over the hull. The green needles left resin on Joel's fingers. He tied the painter to a black spruce trunk, then retied it higher when he saw the old water mark on the bark. The knot came out ugly. He made himself redo it. Fatigue wanted him to accept almost anything. The woods had already accepted too much from them.

The second canoe they carried farther into brush and turned upside down between two downed logs. It was the better boat, and hiding it felt wrong. Leaving it visible felt worse. The paddles went under it except for the cracked one, which Mike insisted they carry in case a pole or splint became useful. Joel took it because it was his damage and because the orange cord on the shaft made it easy to track by sight.

Mike kept his PFD on.

Will watched him buckle it over his rain jacket. "We are not walking through a lake."

"Pockets."

"You have pockets on your actual body."

"Key's in this one."

Mike touched the zip pocket on the left side of the PFD. The gesture was quick but not quick enough to hide. The Highlander key. The staged exit. North Road Bridge sitting somewhere beyond all this with Mike's old vehicle waiting beside a road that still belonged to the human world.

"Put it in the notebook," Mike said.

Joel looked at him.

"If something happens," Mike said.

For a second no one moved.

That was the first time Mike had allowed the sentence to reach that shape. Not if we get delayed. Not if the cabin is nothing. If something happens. The plainness of it stripped the morning colder.

Joel opened the notebook and wrote:

HIGHLANDER KEY - MIKE PFD LEFT ZIP.

He showed the page to both of them.

Will said, "Maybe one of us should carry it."

Mike's hand stayed over the pocket. "No."

"That is not a plan, it's ownership."

"It's my car."

"It's our exit."

Mike turned on him. "You want to do this now?"

Will's face went hard, then tired. "No."

Joel did not say that Will was right. He did not say Mike was not wrong. The key was a practical object and a symbol and a future problem. Saying that aloud would only make all three versions heavier. He wrote instead:

KEY LOCATION KNOWN.

Mike read it and nodded once, as if the page had ruled in his favor.

They took one more pass through camp. Put out what had never been a fire. Tighten the gear tarp. Hang the remaining food barrel high enough to satisfy ritual. Mark the canoe cache on the map by sight: bent cedar, split granite face, three dead spruce in a row like black fingers. Joel sketched the shape in the notebook because paper could remember without making sound.

The emergency camp looked smaller once packed. Not safer. Just reduced. The root shelf, the bad hammock trees, the stones where the stove had sat, Will's wet footprints pressed dark into the duff. By evening, if rain came, much of it would disappear. By another week the site would become only disturbed moss and a few crushed ferns. The woods did not need malice to erase them. Erasure was one of its ordinary skills.

Will stood at the edge of the shelf, looking uphill. "Where does the trace actually start?"

Mike glanced at the map, then at the slope. "Old landing path. Should cut above the inlet, then bend toward the portage."

"Should."

"Yes, Will. Should. As in, not guaranteed by God."

Will's mouth opened. Joel thought he might make some church joke because old reflexes chose bad moments. Then Will closed it.

Joel was grateful and sorry at once. He and Will had grown up in rooms where men spoke certainty from pulpits and boys learned to say amen before they understood the question. Joel had spent half his adult life removing that tone from his own head. Mike had a different version of it, trail-made and brash instead of holy, but it worked the same way under pressure: say the thing firmly enough and maybe the world would line up beneath it.

The world did not line up. It lay wet and irregular above them.

They climbed.

The first stretch was not a trail but a direction. Spruce branches slapped water against their sleeves. The ground rose in slick shelves of granite broken by moss. Packs caught on dead limbs. Twice Mike pointed with his whole hand and the others followed because none of them had anything better. Joel kept the lake behind him when he could see it, a gray glint through trunks. When he could not, he trusted the slope. Trust was too strong a word. He used the slope.

No one spoke.

Silence made simple movement clumsy. A branch whipped back because Mike forgot to hold it. Will stopped too abruptly and Joel nearly stepped on the backs of his sandals. Joel wanted to say sorry and did not. Will lifted his hand without turning, two fingers bent back. Fine. Or stop. Or I know. They were inventing a language under duress, and like all inventions made in fear, it broke often.

After ten minutes they found the old path.

It appeared all at once and then, under looking, became almost nothing: a flattening between young spruce, grass in the center, alder leaning inward from both sides. Along one edge a rotten cut stump wore moss over the saw face. Another stood beyond it at the same height. Human repetition. Not enough to save them, but enough to be followed.

Mike stopped at the entrance and looked back.

There it was, the thing he wanted: proof. Not rescue. Not yet. But a mark on paper answering a mark on the ground. For a moment his face almost softened.

Will saw it too. He could have taken it from him. The correction was available. A path was not a cabin. A trace was not a road. An old cut stump was not a promise. Joel could see each sentence arranging itself behind Will's eyes.

Will looked down instead and adjusted the strap on his pack.

Mike said, quietly, "Okay."

The word did not belong to certainty. It belonged to relief.

Joel wrote in the notebook while standing:

TRACE REAL.

Then, after a pause:

NOT PROOF OF CABIN.

He showed it to Will first.

Will nodded.

He showed it to Mike.

Mike read it and looked away, but he did not argue.

They entered single file: Mike first, then Will, then Joel. That order was Mike's without being discussed. He wanted the front because the front gave him decisions. Will in the middle because someone needed to watch him and because Joel, behind, could see both living men at once. Joel did not know if Mike had thought of that part. He hoped not. He wanted one good decision from the day that had not come from control.

The trace held for fifty yards. Grass dragged cold across Joel's calves above his sandals. Mud took the prints of all three men and filled them slowly with brown water. Once a raven knocked loose from a tree ahead and beat away through branches, black wings loud in the damp air. All three stopped. All three saw it. The bird went on being a bird.

Mike lifted one hand without turning. Keep moving.

They did.

The woods were different away from water. At the lake every sound had space to reveal itself, even when it lied about distance. Here sound came already handled. Branches broke close but unseen. Drops fell from high needles and struck leaves at ankle height. Their own packs produced small betrayals: buckle tick, nylon brush, the cracked paddle knocking once against Joel's water bottle until he caught it with his hand.

The trace bent left around a blowdown and narrowed. Will put one hand back, palm low. Joel stopped. Mike stopped because Will had stopped, and the three of them stood in a line among alder with their breathing loud and useless.

From the brush ahead came Mike's throat clear.

Not behind Joel. Not from the living man ten yards ahead, turned half-profile in a gap between branches. Ahead of him. Beyond him. The same small preparation of the throat before instruction, before reassurance, before a plan. Familiar as the first click of a stove.

The living Mike did not move.

Joel saw the back of his neck go pale.

Will turned his head by inches until Joel could see one eye behind the smeared lens of his glasses. Question, fear, the old terrible wish to ask if the others had heard.

Joel shook his head once.

No.

The woods waited with them.

Then Mike's voice came from the brush beyond Mike, close enough to seem useful.

"Just keep moving."

The sentence entered the old trace and took up space there. It had Mike's impatience, his route confidence, the clipped end that turned suggestion into order. It was not loud. It did not need to be. It sounded exactly like the thing a competent man would say when the others froze too long.

Mike's real hand lowered slowly to his side.

Joel could see him wanting to answer himself. Anger moved through his shoulders. Shame too, maybe. There was something obscene in having your authority borrowed while you were still alive to hear it.

Will's lips parted.

Joel stepped forward and put two fingers against the back of Will's pack.

Will closed his mouth.

For a while nothing happened. Rainwater ticked from alder leaves. A mosquito worked near Joel's ear and he let it whine because swatting made sound and because some part of him now believed even irritation could be learned. The false voice did not repeat. That restraint made it worse. Repetition would have been a machine showing itself. One use felt chosen, though Joel knew want and choice were words they had no right to spend.

Mike turned at last. His eyes moved from Will to Joel, then down to the notebook in Joel's chest pocket.

Joel took it out. His hands shook enough that the pencil point broke on the first stroke. He turned it and wrote with the splintered wood:

MIKE THROAT CLEAR - AHEAD OF MIKE.

MIKE VOICE - "JUST KEEP MOVING" - NOT MIKE.

He added:

WE SAW MIKE.

Mike read the page. His jaw worked.

Will pointed to the line, then to Mike, then held both hands apart as if measuring a distance. Maybe asking how far. Maybe saying too close. The new language failed him there. He wanted words with edges.

Joel wrote:

NO ANSWER.

Will nodded once, hard.

Mike kept looking at the sentence that denied him. Not Mike. Joel expected him to object, not because it was wrong but because seeing it written made the theft official. Instead Mike touched the PFD pocket where the key sat and turned back toward the brush.

He did not say the thing again. He did not say anything.

He lifted one hand, palm open this time, not command exactly. Signal. Then he stepped forward into the trace because standing still had its own dangers and because the map's square was still ahead and because every choice left something hungry behind it.

Will followed.

Joel looked once over his shoulder. Through the trees he could no longer see the lake or the canoes or the bad camp they had made on the wrong shore. Only spruce, wet alder, the path closing behind them as branches settled back into place.

He put the notebook away and followed the two living bodies he could still see.


# Chapter 15 - The Trace

The trace disappeared three times.

The first loss happened so gently that Joel did not know it had happened until Mike stopped. The old path had been grass and mud a minute before, two shallow wheel ruts holding brown water, alder leaning in from both sides. Then it was only wet brush. No ruts. No cut stumps. No straightness. The woods took the human mark into itself without drama, as if it had been waiting for them to blink.

Mike lifted one hand.

Will stopped behind him. Joel stopped behind Will. The order still mattered. Mike first because Mike could read a route and wanted decisions where he could touch them. Will in the middle because he was more likely to turn toward sound. Joel last because he could see both living men, and because by then seeing had become the only mercy left.

No one spoke for a long moment.

Rain from the night before still lived in the branches. It fell one drop at a time onto hoods, pack lids, the backs of their bare calves above the hiking sandals. Mosquitoes had returned with the mildness after the weather. They worked at wrists and ears and the seams where collars had loosened. Joel let one land on the back of his hand and fill itself. Slapping it would make sound. That was ridiculous. It was also what his body believed now.

Mike crouched and pushed wet grass aside with the cracked paddle Joel had carried from camp. He used it like a staff, like a probe, like proof that one broken thing could be repurposed if a man refused embarrassment. The orange cord on the shaft had darkened in the wet. It touched a rotted stump hidden under fern.

Mike pointed.

The stump was cut flat, or had once been. Moss had rounded the saw face until it looked almost natural. Beyond it another stump stood at the same height, twenty feet off, and beyond that the ground dipped in a shallow line. Human repetition. The old road was not gone. It had only withdrawn under growth.

Mike looked back.

Will did not give him the satisfaction of a nod. He adjusted the bandage on his hand instead. The gauze had gone slack and dirty around his palm. He kept flexing the fingers, then remembering the cut and making them still. His face had the waxy look of a man running a fever or anger or both.

Joel took the notebook from his chest pocket and wrote while the paper rested against his thigh:

TRACE LOST / FOUND BY STUMPS.

Under it he added:

ALL THREE VISIBLE.

That line had become less a note than a prayer stripped of religion. All three visible. It did not ask anyone to intervene. It simply stated what still held.

Mike read it and tapped the stump with the paddle. Once. Soft.

Seen.

Then they went on.

The trace took them uphill at an angle that made no clean sense from the map. It might have been following old grade, might have been avoiding low marsh, might have been correcting around a place where the road had always been bad. The map did not show such things. It showed intent. It did not show water sitting black in a rut for thirty years, or a poplar dropping across both wheel lines and becoming soil, or spruce seedlings growing where tires had once pressed the earth open.

Will wanted to say this. Joel could feel the want in him the way he felt heat from a stove. It came off his shoulders. It worked in his jaw. Twice he raised his good hand as if to stop Mike and begin a diagram in the air. Twice he lowered it.

The third time he failed.

"Old logging roads move," Will said.

Mike did not turn.

Will's voice came out low, almost reasonable. That made it more dangerous. "I mean, not move. Obviously. But the map can be right and wrong at the same time. It can mark a legal line, a winter route, a skid road, a thing somebody drove once and then--"

Mike stopped.

Joel watched the back of his neck. The tendon there tightened.

"Write it," Mike said.

Will blinked. "What?"

Mike turned halfway. "You have a theory, put it in the book."

"It's not a theory. It's just--"

Joel held the notebook out.

Will looked at it as if it had insulted him. Then he took it. His bandaged hand made the pencil awkward, so he used the other, pressing too hard.

MAP CAN BE RIGHT AND STILL NOT HELP.

He shoved the notebook against Joel's chest.

The sentence looked better than it had sounded. Writing removed tone. It took Will's need to correct and left something they could use.

Mike read it. His mouth moved once, no sound. Joel thought he might argue with the page, which would be a new low for all of them. Instead Mike nodded, one hard dip, and turned back to the brush.

They kept moving.

The second loss of the trace came in blowdown.

A wind years old had taken a strip of spruce and laid the trunks in the same direction, root plates standing shoulder-high and black with soil. The old road ran into the mess and did not come out where it should have. Branches had dried into gray nets. New alder filled the gaps. Their packs caught on everything. Sleeping bags bulged under rain covers. The pot in Joel's side pocket knocked against his water bottle until he stopped and tightened the strap with fingers made stupid by cold and fatigue.

In ordinary time they would have complained loudly. Alex would have turned it into a bit about premium wilderness experiences, about how people paid good money to crawl under dead trees and lose shin skin. Will would have explained something about windthrow. Mike would have said people were soft now. Joel would have let them make noise and would have been privately glad for it.

Now the work happened in fragments of breath and gesture.

Mike pointed under one trunk. Will shook his head and pointed over. Joel looked at the root plate and saw a dark hollow beneath it where water had run, or where an animal had used passage, or where nothing should put a human head. He touched Will's pack and pointed over.

Over, then.

They climbed.

The bark came away wet under Joel's palms. His sandal slipped on a branch polished by rain and his shin hit hard enough to make him see white at the edges. He opened his mouth around a curse and closed it on air. The pain moved up his leg and became heat. He held still until it settled into a shape he could walk with.

Ahead, Will made a small dry cough.

Joel saw him do it. Saw his shoulders jerk once. Saw the hand rise halfway toward his mouth and stop too late. Will turned back immediately, eyes wide behind smeared glasses.

Sorry, his face said.

No one wrote it down. There was nothing useful in punishing a cough after the body had already betrayed him.

They pushed another twenty yards through the blowdown.

Then the same cough came from ahead of Mike.

Not loud. Not theatrical. It did not echo. It was Will's cough exactly, the small irritated clearing before correction, before a sentence beginning no or actually or that is not how. The living Will stood six feet in front of Joel with one hand on a dead branch and the other pressed against his own mouth as if he could take the sound back into himself.

Mike froze.

Joel froze.

The cough did not repeat.

The woods resumed its ordinary noises around the absence: water ticking from needles, the faint rub of pack cloth where Joel's weight shifted, one far raven knocking once and then nothing. Ordinary sound had become a set of suspects. Each one had to show its hands.

Will looked at Joel.

Joel shook his head.

No answer.

He took the notebook out. His hands were wet enough that the page softened under his thumb.

WILL COUGH - AHEAD.

WILL SEEN.

Mike read it. His face stayed closed, but Joel saw the arithmetic going on behind it. If Will could be in one place and Will's voice in another, then Mike's own voice from the trace behind him had not been an exception. It had been the beginning of a new category.

Will wrote beneath Joel's lines:

IT CAN USE LIVING.

Then, after a pause, he added:

NOT JUST DEAD.

The page looked colder with that on it.

Mike stared at the words for too long. Then he reached up and wiped rain from the bill of his cap with two fingers. "We knew that."

His voice sounded too large in the blowdown.

Will flinched. Joel did too.

Mike's jaw set as he heard himself hearing himself.

"Write," Will whispered.

It was barely a sound, but it was a sound. He knew it. Shame went through his face so quickly it would have been easy to miss in any other life.

Joel took the pencil and wrote:

LESS TALK.

He underlined it once, not as command. As fact.

They found the trace again where the blowdown thinned. Mike found it, really. He angled left when the easier ground went right, cut around a wet hollow, and stopped beside a line of stones sunk under moss. The stones had been laid by hand. Maybe to hold a culvert, maybe to harden mud, maybe by men who had never imagined three strangers decades later reading their labor as if it were scripture.

Mike touched the stones with the paddle tip.

There was no triumph in the gesture now. Only use.

The trail rose. The woods changed by degrees. Black spruce gave way in patches to jack pine and young birch. The ground under the moss became sandier, then wet again. Grass grew in the center of the old grade. In places the ruts were clear enough that Joel could imagine tires: a pickup, a maintenance truck, a ranger's vehicle with the door hinge squealing and a man stepping out to clear brush from a sign. Imagination had become suspect too. It could give the woods shapes it had not earned.

At midday, or what Joel guessed was midday from the pale place above the clouds, they stopped beside a granite knob with enough open surface to sit without soaking through immediately.

No one wanted to stop. All three needed to.

Mike checked his watch. The face was scratched and fogged at the edge. He checked the compass next, then the map in its cloudy case. He did it with the care of someone handling evidence after being accused.

Will sat on the rock and peeled the dirty bandage from his palm. The cut had opened. Not badly, but enough. Blood made a bright line in the wet crease below his thumb. He looked at it with irritation, as if his hand were making a poor argument.

Joel crouched by him and took out the first-aid kit.

Will let him clean it. That was how tired he was.

Alcohol made Will's fingers jerk.

"Sorry," Joel said, before he could stop himself.

The word hung there.

It was small. Human. Useless. It had crossed the air and become available.

Joel felt Mike look at him.

Will looked at the ground. "Seen," he said, too softly.

Joel nodded. He wrote anyway:

JOEL SAID SORRY - TO WILL HAND.

It felt foolish to inventory kindness as if it were a dropped wrapper. It felt worse not to. The notebook had become a ledger of what they had given away.

They ate bars because the body would not negotiate forever. Mike divided them by sight on the rock: three damp rectangles, two with chocolate gone gray at the edges, one oat thing that Alex would have refused with a face elaborate enough to make a meal out of it.

Joel saw the empty fourth share before anyone made it. There was no fourth share. There had not been since Second Camp. Still, his hand paused with the wrapper half open.

Will saw him pause and looked away.

Mike chewed without looking at either of them. His beard held crumbs. He was still wearing the PFD over his rain jacket, orange shoulders dulled by mud, straps dark with wet. On land it made him look both prepared and absurd, like a man who had misunderstood the category of danger. But the pockets mattered. The Highlander key mattered. North Road Bridge mattered somewhere past water, road, time, and whatever had learned to speak with them.

Mike's hand went to the left zip pocket.

Joel saw the gesture before Mike knew he had made it. So did Will.

Will raised his eyebrows.

Mike caught him and lowered his hand. For one second the old answer formed in his face. Some blunt defense. Some managing sentence.

He said it anyway.

"Truck doesn't help if we lose the key."

It was a reasonable sentence. It was even necessary. Spoken aloud, it became material.

The three of them listened to the woods after he said it.

Nothing answered.

That did not make it safe.

Joel wrote:

MIKE: TRUCK DOESN'T HELP IF WE LOSE THE KEY.

Then, because the practical mattered more than the fear:

KEY STILL MIKE PFD LEFT ZIP.

Will read it and nodded.

Mike read it too. "Good."

His voice was low. Tired. Almost grateful. Then he seemed to hear the softness in himself and closed down again.

They rested five minutes more. Maybe seven. Time had lost its clean increments. It had become distance, light, water, breath. Joel's watch still worked, but he had stopped trusting numbers to mean what they had meant before Alex died. A mile on a map was not a mile in alder. Noon did not promise afternoon. A cabin on paper did not promise shelter. A living voice did not promise a living man.

Will took the notebook and wrote:

DISTANCE DOESN'T MEAN TIME.

Then, under it:

TIME DOESN'T MEAN SAFE.

He stared at what he had written and gave Joel the pencil without comment.

Mike folded the map and stood.

The third loss came after the old road dipped into cedar.

It was darker there though the day had not darkened. Cedar branches held the wet close and low. The ground turned soft enough to take the whole shape of their sandals and then drink the prints back in. Each step made a quiet sucking sound. Their packs brushed both sides of the corridor. The trace, if it was still a trace, narrowed to the distance between discomfort and refusal.

Mike slowed. Then stopped.

Will almost walked into him.

Ahead, the grade split around a mound of deadfall. Left looked lower and wetter. Right looked higher and wrong. Straight was impossible. The map did not acknowledge choice at that scale. It offered a line. The ground offered a place to be mistaken.

Mike took out the compass.

Will leaned to look and stopped himself halfway. His restraint looked painful.

Joel watched Mike align the compass with the map case against his forearm. Mike was good at it. Even exhausted, even afraid, his hands knew the work. He sighted past two spruce trunks, checked the slope, checked the light, checked something in the ground Joel could not see. Competence again. The terrible comfort of it.

Will could not stand it.

"Contour says higher," he said.

Mike closed his eyes.

Will pressed on, softer but still speaking. "I'm not being an asshole. The old road's probably staying out of that wet. If there was a culvert, it's gone. If the line bends, it bends around drainage. That's all I'm--"

"Will."

"No, I'm trying to help."

"Then help quietly."

"Quietly doesn't change the contour interval."

Mike turned.

There was anger in him, but the larger thing was shame. Joel saw it and did not want to. Mike heard every correction now as proof that he had brought them here wrong. The map, the route, the missing beacon, the cabin square, Alex under stones back at Second Camp, all of it had begun to gather around him. Will was not wrong. That was part of the cruelty. A wrong accusation could be dismissed. A useful correction could enter the body and stay there.

Joel put the notebook between them.

Will looked at it, then at Mike. His mouth made a hard line. He wrote:

HIGHER PROBABLY BETTER.

Mike read it.

"Probably," he said.

Will snatched the notebook back and added:

NOT CERTAIN.

Mike gave one breath through his nose. Not quite a laugh. "That's new for you."

Will's face flickered. Under other weather that might have become something. A jab returned. A little pressure released. Here it collapsed almost at once.

From the left, through the wet cedar, Mike's voice said, "It opens up after this."

All three men went still.

The living Mike stood in front of Joel, visible in profile, map case in one hand, compass in the other. His mouth was closed. His eyes had gone flat with shock.

The false voice had come from the lower, wetter option.
