Will picked out raisins with the automatic disgust of a normal man, stared at them in his palm as if the old preference had come from someone else, and put them in his mouth anyway.

Joel almost smiled.

Will saw the almost and looked down.

That was the morning's first mercy: a joke too small to say, therefore safe.

They inventoried properly in the gray light.

Will laid items on the floor in rows. Food. Filter. Lighter. Pot. Headlamps. Spare batteries, two good and one questionable. First-aid bag, lighter than it should have been. Map case damp at one corner. Compass. Joel's knife. Will's fishing pliers, useless except as pliers now. The hatchet. The key. Will's truck key, still sealed in the dry pouch in his pack, a second vehicle on the wrong end of the route and therefore only a fact.

Mike's absence took up more floor than any object.

Will paused with Mike's small repair kit in his hand. He had found it in the side pocket of the pack Mike had left inside when he forced his way out. A coil of wire. Zip ties. A roll of black tape flattened from use. A little tube of epoxy. Mike had mocked expensive gear and kept every cheap fix that might hold for one more day.

Will set the kit down carefully.

Joel wrote:

TAKE.

Will nodded.

Then he wrote:

BODY?

The word sat between them.

Joel did not answer quickly. He looked out the open door. The clearing waited with terrible patience. The brush beyond the road was close enough that a man could throw a stone into it. Close enough that a living Mike, hurt and hidden, should have been able to crawl, call, curse, make any human sound they could trust.

But the woods had already used him cleanly.

Joel wrote:

NO VISUAL. NO RECOVERY.

Will's face closed.

He took the pencil.

WE LEFT ALEX.

The letters dug hard into the damp page. The X at the end of Alex tore the paper.

Joel looked at the word and saw the groundsheet at Second Camp, stones along the edge, Alex's hand not fully covered until Mike went back and fixed it. The note they had left. The absurd formality of naming a suspected bear attack in pencil while the woods listened.

He wrote:

WE MARKED HIM.

Will struck one line through that. Not hard enough to erase it. Hard enough to reject the mercy.

Joel let him.

From outside, to the right of the door, Will's voice said, "No, no, no."

Will stopped moving.

The pencil rolled from his fingers and tapped the floor.

The living Will was inside the cabin, kneeling by the food row, mouth open and silent. The voice had come from outside near the wall, thin and panicked, exactly Will's panic and not his breath. It had the shape of him without the body. It had stolen even the way his fear doubled back on itself before reaching a sentence.

Joel lifted one hand. Slow.

Will nodded in tiny increments, eyes fixed on Joel.

The voice came again, outside and closer to the open door. "No, no, no."

Will put his good hand over his mouth.

Joel wrote without looking down:

NOT YOU.

The pencil snagged and made the T too long.

Will looked at the page. His chest hitched once. He shook his head, not in denial, but in a kind of insulted wonder. His own terror had been taken from him and returned like a tool.

Joel reached for the door.

Mike cleared his throat from the road.

Joel froze.

The throat-clear was worse in daylight. Night had given it room to be a sound among sounds. Morning made it administrative. A man about to make a decision. A man ready to turn uncertainty into instruction.

"Just keep moving," Mike said.

Will's eyes went to Joel's hand on the door.

Joel did not close it. He did not open it wider. The sentence hung in the wet air with all its old usefulness. On the first muddy portage it would have been correct. On the beaver dams it had kept Alex from making every log a debate. In rain and bad footing and fatigue, it had been a good rule. It was still a good rule, and that was why it could kill them.

Joel took the notebook and wrote:

RIGHT WORDS CAN BE WRONG SOURCE.

Will stared at the line for a long time. Then he wrote underneath it:

RIGHT SOURCE CAN BE WRONG WORDS.

Joel looked at him.

For the first time since Mike disappeared, Will looked almost present. Ruined, wet-eyed, trembling, but present. His mind was still reaching. Still making distinctions. Still himself in the way that would later endanger him, because the best part of a man was rarely separated cleanly from the worst.

Joel tapped Will's sentence once.

YES.

They closed the door long enough to pack.

Packing without speech made them slow and mean in small ways. Joel pointed at the food bag and Will thought he meant the filter. Will held up the map and Joel shook his head too hard because the road trace did not get another chance to lie. Will tried to strap Mike's repair kit to the outside of his pack one-handed and failed twice before Joel reached for it. Will jerked away, then held it out. Apology without apology. Acceptance without saying thank you.

The little economies of speech had held the group together for years. Move. Grab that. You got it? Quit blocking the path. Nice work, genius. They had been rude with each other because rudeness saved time and hid care. Without it, even help had to be negotiated by hand.

Joel wrote lists.

TAKE: FOOD / FILTER / MAP / COMPASS / KEYS / REPAIR / FIRST AID / HATCHET.

LEAVE: EXTRA POT / MIKE WET CLOTHES / BROKEN STRAP / CABIN JUNK.

Will wrote beside MIKE WET CLOTHES:

NOT JUNK.

Joel crossed out CABIN JUNK, leaving the clothes unmarked.

Will took Mike's knit cap from a nail by the stove. It was damp and smelled like smoke, sweat, and the sour old wool smell of trips. He held it once against his chest, then shoved it deep into his pack where Joel would not have to see it and where the woods could not see it either.

Joel did not stop him.

They needed to decide route.

Will wanted, by the look of him, to talk through every option. He even opened his mouth once and shut it hard, teeth clicking. Joel felt for him then, with a tiredness almost like love. Will's mind did not become still because the woods had made thinking dangerous. It only grew louder inside him.

Joel drew four crude lines on a page.

ROAD TRACE FORWARD.

STAY.

BACK TO CANOES.

CUT CROSS-COUNTRY.

Will took the pencil.

ROAD MAY HIT REAL ROAD.

Joel wrote:

OLD MAP. DARK SOON IF WRONG.

Will wrote:

IT IS MORNING.

Joel answered:

WE ARE SLOW.

Will looked at his bandaged hand.

Staying required no argument. Will crossed it out himself.

Cutting cross-country lasted longer. They both stared at it because it had the false cleanliness of a line on paper. A direct line from cabin to take-out, perhaps, if a man ignored swamp, blowdown, water, distance, no trail, no exact bearing, injury, and the fact that the woods had already proven it could stand between sound and source. Joel wrote:

NO.

Will underlined it.

That left the road trace forward and back to the canoes.

The old road beyond the cabin might go somewhere human. It might not. It might become muskeg, vanish in second growth, cross private logging land, end at a washout, or loop toward a place no one had used in twenty years. Mike had needed it to mean exit. The map had needed nothing. It had only carried an old mark forward until men mistook memory for promise.

The canoes were known. Damaged, awkward, frightening, but known. Water connected to the route. The planned route connected, eventually, to North Road Bridge and the Highlander key in Joel's pocket. The river did not become safe because it was mapped. But it was the shape of the trip they had left, and all the remaining exits were attached to it.

Joel wrote:

BACK TO BOATS.

Will stared at the words.

Then:

MIKE SAID KEEP MOVING.

Joel understood the accusation. Mike had wanted movement. Mike had died moving. The woods had Mike's sentence now. Every practical act could sound like obedience to a dead man or to the thing wearing him.

Joel wrote:

WE CHOOSE DIRECTION BY SIGHT.

Will breathed through his nose. Once. Twice.

Then he wrote:

HOW DO WE KNOW IT ONLY USES WHAT IT HEARD?

Joel almost wrote a clean answer. Because it repeats exact phrases. Because it misplaces context. Because it has not said anything new with new facts. Because it says "we're fine" when no one is fine, "map says there's a cabin" after the cabin has failed, "answer if seen" without knowing why the phrase mattered. Because if it could invent freely they would already be dead, or maybe that was not evidence at all.

He looked at Will's question for a long time.

It was fair. It was Will. Even in ruin he wanted the terms exact.

Joel wrote:

WE DON'T.

Then:

ACT LIKE IT.

Will read it. A sound came out of him before he could stop it. Almost a laugh. Almost not. It was ugly and small and human.

Joel looked toward the door.

No echo answered.

That felt like luck, which meant it was not a thing to trust.

They finished packing.

Before they left, Joel made one more page. He wrote large enough that Will could read without taking the notebook from him.

DO NOT ANSWER UNLESS SEEN.

NO NAMES.

NO HELP UNLESS BODY SEEN.

WRITE WHEN POSSIBLE.

IF VOICE FROM TREES, STOP.

Will took the notebook and added:

NO THEORIES OUT LOUD.

Then, after a pause:

NO COMFORT OUT LOUD.

The second line hurt more. It sat there among the practical instructions like an exposed nerve.

Joel wanted to cross it out. He wanted to tell Will there were kinds of comfort the woods could not use. A hand on a shoulder. Food pushed toward him. A turn taken under a heavier pack. He wanted to say they were not empty just because they could not speak. He wanted to say Mike and Alex had not been reduced to sound by the thing that killed them.

He said nothing.

Will looked at him and understood enough.

They moved the table aside.

The door opened on the clearing. Midmorning had come without warmth. Low cloud pressed the spruce tops. Rain had become mist and hung in the air rather than falling. The old roadbed ran away from the cabin in two directions: one toward the unknown forward trace where Mike had vanished, one back the way they had come, toward mud, blowdown, the cache, the canoes, water, all the work still required to be alive.

Joel stepped out first.

He kept the cabin at his back for only three seconds, then turned so Will could pass and so neither of them lost sight of the other. Will crossed the threshold with the hatchet in his left hand and his pack sitting wrong on one shoulder. He looked smaller under it. Or the pack looked larger now that there was no third man to divide the route.

Two men.

The phrase had seemed like inventory in the night. In daylight it became a condition. Two men meant every task took longer. Two men meant one injured hand mattered. Two men meant no one could scout, no one could watch both directions, no one could carry grief as a private weight because the other man would see it bend him. Two men also meant the group was not yet gone. A line still existed between bodies. Joel could look at Will and know one sound in the world had a source.

At the clearing edge, Will stopped and looked back.

The cabin stood as it had stood before they found it: useless, square, patient, almost human from the outside because men had made it and then left it for the weather. In daylight the open door looked less like shelter than a mouth that had failed to say anything helpful.

Joel touched Will's shoulder.

Will whispered, "That's not how this should work."

Joel closed his eyes.

Will knew as soon as he said it. His face folded with self-disgust. He lifted his good hand to his mouth, too late.

For a moment there was only the mist. Water dripping from spruce. The soft complaint of Will's pack strap as he shifted under it. Joel could almost believe the sentence had gone no farther than the clearing.

Then from inside the cabin, in Will's own voice, came the same words, softer and cleaner.

"That's not how this should work."

Will went still.

Joel did not look into the doorway. He looked at Will. Body in sight. Source confirmed. The living Will stood in front of him, white around the mouth, breathing hard through his nose.

Joel opened the notebook and pointed to the first line.

DO NOT ANSWER UNLESS SEEN.

Will read it. He nodded once.

Behind them, from the cabin, Will's stolen voice repeated, patient as a lesson, "That's not how this should work."

They walked.


# Chapter 19 - Written Rules

Traveling silently made them clumsy.

On water, men could point. On a portage, under packs, in brush, with footing bad and sightlines worse, speech was the tool that kept bodies from breaking. Step left. Duck. Wait. Loose rock. Your strap. Snake, maybe. Don't put your foot there.

Without speech they had hands, eyes, and the notebook.

It should have been enough. Men had traveled before language, Joel thought. Men had crossed worse country with less. But those men had known how to look at each other. They had not come out of a lifetime of filling every awkward gap with correction and jokes and small insults. They had not made friendship out of interruption. They had not given the woods a vocabulary and then tried to survive by taking it back.

At the edge of the cabin clearing, before the trace closed around them again, Joel stopped Will with two fingers on his sleeve.

Will turned too fast, hatchet lifting in his good hand.

Joel shook his head and pointed down.

Not danger. Stop.

Will understood on the second try. Understanding had become slower in him, not because he was stupid, but because every gesture now had to compete with what his body wanted to do. Listen. Turn. Answer. Explain. His eyes kept trying to go past Joel into the trees, to the places where Mike's voice had been and might be again.

Joel took out the notebook.

The pages had swollen at the corners. Damp had made the pencil lines dark and slightly furred. The old grocery lists and route notes had become a different kind of document: less record than wall.

He wrote:

VISUAL BEFORE VOICE.

NO ANSWERING FROM TREES.

NO NAMES.

POINT FIRST.

WRITE IF POSSIBLE.

Will read the lines. His lips moved once, silently, on the words. Joel hated that, then hated himself for hating it. A man reading a page was not a man speaking to the woods. It was only a man trying to keep rules inside his head.

Will took the pencil.

NO THEORIES OUT LOUD.

He underlined it until the damp paper almost tore.

Joel nodded.

Will held up one finger and wrote again:

NO COMFORT OUT LOUD.

The line was already in the notebook from the cabin, but Will wrote it again as if repetition might make it bearable. He stared at the page afterward. The sentence embarrassed him. It embarrassed Joel too, because it made plain what they were refusing each other. Comfort was not just a thing said in soft voices by better men. It was all the little sounds they had lived inside. Alex making an ugly joke so no one had to admit the hill was brutal. Mike saying they were fine, and being wrong, but giving them something to lean against for thirty seconds. Will explaining a knot no one had asked about because silence had gotten too honest.

Joel took the pencil back.

TOUCH OK.

Will read it. His face tightened. For a second Joel thought he would cross it out. Instead he gave one hard nod and folded his torn rule page into a small rectangle.

Joel tore the page out carefully and handed it to him. Will put it in the chest pocket of his rain shell, under the zipper, then tapped the pocket twice. The gesture was nearly military. It would have made Alex say something. It would have made Mike call them idiots. It would have made Will, two days ago, make a joke about laminated procedure cards and ISO compliance.

None of those men were available.

They moved.

The trace leaving the cabin looked clearer from the clearing than it did inside the trees. That was one of the old map's small cruelties. From a distance, human work held its shape. Up close, it became roots, wet leaves, broken alder, stones turned slick under moss, and the low black claws of spruce that caught at straps and sleeves. Mike had read the line because Mike knew how men marked land. Joel had to read it now by absence: a gap where trees were younger, a run of stones too flat to be accidental, the shallow trough of old wheels holding rain.

Will went first for the first twenty yards and then stopped, turned, and pointed at Joel with irritation.

Joel shook his head.

Will pointed at his own injured hand, then at Joel's pack, then at the hatchet.

Joel understood: if Will went first, he could not see Joel; if Joel went first, Will could use the hatchet and still be watched.

The old reflex rose in Joel. Fine. You go. Something small and practical. Two words. Safe, maybe. But safe had become a word for people who still thought intention mattered.

Joel stepped ahead.

They settled into an order. Joel first, Will close enough that Joel could hear his breathing but not so close that one stumble would take them both down. Every fifteen steps Joel stopped and turned. Will had to be there. Not a voice. Not a cough. Not brush moving in his shape. A body, seen. Face, pack, hatchet, bandaged hand, the corner of the folded rule page making a pale square under his pocket flap.

After the third stop Will rolled his eyes.

Joel let him.

After the sixth stop Will stopped rolling his eyes.

The woods took them back without ceremony. The cabin disappeared behind brush and then behind the idea of itself. Rainwater held in the spruce boughs and let go when they brushed beneath. It fell cold down Joel's neck and into the collar of his shirt. His socks were wet inside hiking sandals. Grit worked under the straps and rasped the skin raw at the sides of his feet. His hips hurt from the pack. His shoulders had a deep packed pain that no shifting relieved.

Daylight was better than night because they could see each other. It was worse because seeing proved how little was there.

Mike's voice followed after the first half hour.

"Just keep moving."

It came from behind them, not from the cabin exactly, but from the direction of the clearing. Low. Tired. Irritated with delay. It sounded like a man who had been given the wrong crew and was making the best of it.

Will stopped.

Joel turned and put two fingers to his own eyes, then pointed at Will.

See me.

Will stared at him.

Mike said, closer this time, "Just keep moving."

Will's mouth folded inward. His good hand closed around the hatchet handle so hard the knuckles lost color.

Joel held the notebook up without opening it. The object itself had begun to mean the rule.

Will nodded once.

They moved.

The thing did not sound angry when they failed to answer. That was part of its wrongness. It did not have to be angry. It could try again. It could wait. Or perhaps waiting was not a thing it did; perhaps the woods simply held every sound they had given it until one shape fit a gap.

For a while it used Mike only in fragments. A throat-clear from the low brush behind Joel's right shoulder. A small curse from the slope above them. The scuffing exhale Mike made when lifting a heavy pack. Once, from no clear direction, the wet click of a zipper pull against plastic, a sound so much like Mike's torn PFD pocket that Joel's hand went to the Highlander key before he could stop himself.

The key was there. Hard against his ribs. Black fob. Bent metal. Torn orange nylon.

He left his hand over it for three steps, then made himself let go.

Will saw. He did not write anything. That was mercy.

The trace dropped into a shallow swale where water had gathered overnight. The mud was black and shining. Old leaves floated on it with their stems up like insect legs. Joel tested one side with his foot and sank to the ankle. He pointed left. Will pointed farther left. Joel looked, saw the stones, and nodded.

They crossed one at a time.

On the far side, a boot scraped stone ahead.

Both men stopped.

The sound had been plain and close: rubber sole dragged over wet granite, a small correction of balance. Human. Ordinary. It came from thirty yards ahead where the trace bent around a knuckle of spruce and jack pine.

Joel looked back.

Will was behind him. Body in sight. Hatchet in hand. Breath white in the cool air. Not moving.

The boot scraped again.

Will lifted the hatchet slightly.

Joel shook his head.

He wrote on the open page:

BOOT SOUND AHEAD. NO BODY.

Will read it, then looked toward the bend.

The sound came again, but this time behind them.

Joel felt his shoulders rise, uselessly, as if he could make less of himself for the sound to hold. A scrape ahead meant someone in front. A scrape behind meant someone following. A scrape in both places meant the sound had become detached from walking. It was only boot scrape now, a little tool the woods could set down wherever it wanted them to look.

Will wrote:

G4.

Joel frowned.

Will tapped the note and then the page where Joel had once made sound columns. Gear, camp, voices, body. G4 was Will trying to classify under pressure. Joel would have laughed another time. He might have said, You numbered the monster? Will would have said, There is a difference between numbering and taxonomy. Alex would have made it filthy. Mike would have told them to shut up and walk.

Joel wrote:

NO CODES OUT LOUD.

Will's expression, for one second, was almost offended professional pride. Then it broke into something like grief and went flat again.

They went around the bend with the hatchet ready and the notebook open. Nothing waited there but stone, spruce roots, and water collecting in old tire ruts.

The entity adjusted after that.

A pack shifted behind them when both packs were fixed on their backs. A dry bag buckle clicked from the brush where there was no dry bag. A branch cracked exactly when Joel looked down to place his foot. Twice a small twig snapped from just beyond Will's left shoulder, not loud enough to be a lure, only loud enough to make him spend attention.

Silence helped. Silence did not save them.

The woods still had the world's old language. Weight on moss. Water off leaves. Bark rubbed by wind. A bird going quiet too quickly. A squirrel or something like one moving in the litter. Real sounds and made sounds came wearing the same clothes. The difference existed somewhere beneath them, perhaps, but Joel did not know where to put his hands.

Late morning thinned into noon without light changing much. The sky stayed low and pale. Mist caught in the trace and made distance uncertain. Every tree seemed to begin twice: once where it stood and once where the wet air repeated it.

They stopped beside a fallen spruce because Will's hand had bled through again.

Will tried to keep going. He made two short chopping gestures with the hatchet and then pointed ahead.

Joel pointed to the blood at his bandage.

Will looked at it as if it had betrayed him.

They chose the side of the spruce away from the open trace. Joel made Will sit with his back to the trunk and his face toward Joel. No one turned away. No one got privacy. Privacy had become another old luxury, like jokes, like hot food, like the ability to say a friend's name because you missed him.

The bandage came loose wet and sticky. Will looked above Joel's head while Joel worked. The cut itself was not terrible in the old scale of things. A torn palm, swollen, dirty at the edges despite their rinsing. In another life it would have been urgent care, a tetanus booster, three jokes from Alex about Will finally using his hand for something, Mike calling it a princess wound, Joel telling Will to stop touching things.

Here, it was one more piece of the route trying to take time.

Joel rinsed it with clean water from the bottle and used more gauze than they could spare. Will breathed hard through his nose but did not make a sound. Pain had become safe if it stayed inside.

When Joel finished, Will took the notebook.

IT CAN USE SEQUENCE.

Joel read it and looked up.

Will wrote beneath it:

COUGH THEN PHRASE. BUCKLE THEN MIKE. THROAT CLEAR THEN ORDER.

His handwriting was worse with the injured hand, blockier, angled upward across the page.

Joel wrote:

MAYBE.

Will took the pencil back too quickly.

NOT MAYBE.

Then:

IT LEARNED ASSOCIATIONS.

Joel looked at the words, then at the trees. He could feel the shape of Will's thought. It was clean and dangerous. The thing did not merely repeat isolated sounds. It had heard Mike clear his throat before certainty and had used the throat-clear to prepare their bodies for an order. It had heard buckles before gear and food and movement, and used them to pull eyes away. It had heard Will's cough before explanation, Alex's laugh before attention, Mike's reassurance before surrender.

Maybe it understood. Maybe it did not. Maybe association was not understanding. Maybe humans were only association with a better vocabulary.

Joel wrote:

ONLY WHAT IT HEARD.

He stared at the line after he wrote it. It was too certain.

He added:

WE THINK.

Will read both lines and looked at him for a long time.

Then he wrote:

IF IT LEARNS ASSOCIATION, WRITING IS DIFFERENT.

Joel waited.

Will added:

WRITING HAS NO TONE.

Joel nodded before he meant to. That was true. Written words were not safe because words were pure. They were safe because they arrived stripped. No throat-clear. No panic. No old affection curled around insult. No Mike making certainty feel like a hand on your back. No Alex turning risk into performance. No Will's nervous cough asking everyone to make room for his thinking.

Writing removed the part of speech that the thing used best.

It also removed the part that made speech bearable.

Joel wrote:

GOOD.

Will took the pencil.

BAD ALSO.

Joel almost smiled, then did not. He tapped the line once.

YES.

Will closed his eyes for a moment. His face, dirty and drawn, looked younger with his eyes shut. Joel saw the boy from church basements and lock-ins, from bad carpet and orange drink, from Sunday mornings when adults talked about being called and Joel had already begun to suspect no one was answering from anywhere. Will had always talked through discomfort. He had whispered jokes during prayer, corrected youth leaders on historical details no one cared about, coughed into his fist before saying something that would make Joel shake with silent laughter.

Joel had loved him first as a voice.

That was the problem now.

Will opened his eyes and wrote:

IF IT CAN USE SEQUENCE, IT MAY UNDERSTAND MORE THAN REPEAT.

Joel wrote:

DON'T TEACH IT.

Will's anger came so fast it was almost a sound. His shoulders lifted. His mouth opened and closed. He pointed at the sentence, then at his own head, then at the trees. A furious little pant came through his nose.

Joel understood. Will was not trying to be reckless. He was trying to be useful in the only way that still felt like himself. The route needed someone to think about mechanism. Joel's rules were blunt instruments. Will wanted edges. He wanted to know whether they were facing an animal, a system, a mind, a trap with inputs, a thing that learned like a person or only echoed like water under a bridge.

Joel wanted that too.

He wanted it less than he wanted Will alive.

He wrote:

THINK IT. DON'T SAY IT.

Will looked at the page. The anger did not leave, but it had nowhere to go. He handed back the pencil and let Joel help him stand.

They ate while walking. Half a crushed bar each, chewed without appetite. Joel's stomach took the sugar and stayed hollow. Will drank too fast and coughed once before he could stop himself.

The cough went out into the wet woods.

Both men froze.

For three seconds there was only the drip of spruce and their own breath.

Then Will's cough answered from ahead.

Not louder. Not exaggerated. Just the small dry correction of his throat, placed beyond the next bend where Will was not. The sound had none of his pain in it. That made it worse. It was Will with the inconvenience removed. Will as function.

Will's eyes went wide with an insult so intimate Joel felt ashamed to see it.

Joel lifted the notebook.

Will shook his head once, hard, before Joel even opened it.

I know.

The cough came again, ahead and slightly left.

Will pointed to himself, then to the bend, then made a little pulling motion with his fingers. It wants me.

Joel nodded.

Then he pointed at Will's feet, then at his own eyes, then down the trace.

Walk where I can see you.

Will swallowed. The motion looked painful. He fell in behind Joel.

For the next hour, the trace became an argument conducted entirely in muscle.

Will wanted to move faster. Joel slowed down whenever sightlines bent. Will wanted to test side openings, to see if the old trace had forked or if the water might be closer through the lower ground. Joel kept to the known line unless he could see the return. Will's frustration came through his hands: a sharp point, a flat palm, fingers spread in the universal sign for Are you serious. Joel's replies became equally spare: stop, wait, look, no.

They were worse without speech. Less kind. More honest in the ugly ways. A spoken argument could soften itself with tone. A hand refusing another man's idea had no softness. It was only refusal.

Once, after Joel rejected a lower deer path that might have cut time, Will held the notebook against his knee and wrote:

MIKE WOULD CHECK.

Joel read it and felt the old heat rise behind his eyes.

He wrote:

MIKE DID.

Will stared at the words. Joel regretted them at once and did not cross them out. The sentence was cruel and true and therefore almost unusable. Written words removed tone, but they did not remove damage.

Will looked away first.

Joel added:

STAY ON TRACE UNTIL WATER.

Will nodded without looking at him.

The trace climbed. The ground became stone under thin soil, then dropped through a stand of young birch where yellow leaves had begun to turn early from stress. The leaves trembled although there was little wind. Joel kept waiting for the sound of water. He wanted the canoe cache with a force that made it nearly holy. Boats meant danger, yes. Open water, fog, wind, hull knocks from nowhere. But boats were objects. They obeyed weight and balance. They moved if paddled and sat if dragged ashore. Even damaged, they belonged to a world of force.

The trace gave them no water.

It gave them a raven instead, somewhere above the trees, its call cracking open the low sky. Once, twice. Ordinary enough. Then, after a pause, a third call came from ground level to Joel's right.

Will did not turn.

Joel did, half an inch, before he caught himself.

The raven call from the brush repeated. It was good. Too good for comfort. It had the rough wooden quality of the bird, the little downward break at the end. But it came from under the spruce at the height of a man's chest.

Joel wrote:

BIRD SOUND WRONG HEIGHT.

Will read it, then surprised him by writing:

DON'T NEED TO CLASSIFY EVERY ONE.

Joel looked at him.

Will's mouth twitched, bitterly. He tapped Joel's earlier line: DON'T TEACH IT.

Fair, Joel thought.

He did not write fair.

They went on.

By midafternoon the day had narrowed to the next twenty feet. Joel stopped thinking in miles. He thought in visible ground. Rock to root. Root to mud. Mud to the dark gap between alders. Stop. Turn. Confirm Will. Move.

Fatigue made the rules harder, not easier. A tired body loved sound because sound was faster than sight. It could locate without turning. It could believe before checking. Every human habit favored the thing. When Will's pack strap slipped, Joel heard the shift and almost said, Strap. When his own foot slid on wet stone, his throat made the beginning of a warning to Will before he swallowed it. The unsaid words collected inside him. They made him feel crowded, as if silence were not emptiness but a room filling with men who wanted out.

Once, Will stumbled.

Joel heard the wet skid behind him and turned in time to see Will go to one knee. The hatchet hit stone with a flat metal tick. Will's injured hand landed in mud. His face went white.

Joel moved toward him.

From the trees behind Will, Alex said, "Hold up."

Will's head jerked.

Joel lunged and caught his shoulder before the turn became a step.

For one instant Will fought him. Not hard, not knowingly, but with the blind force of a body hearing a dead friend exactly where a dead friend should not be. Alex's voice had come with effort in it, with that old half-laughing complaint he used when he wanted everybody to wait and watch him make some ordinary inconvenience entertaining.

"Hold up," Alex said again, farther back.

Will made a sound into his closed mouth. It broke there.

Joel turned him physically until Will faced him. He put both hands on Will's shoulders, then pointed at his own face.

Here.

Will's eyes found him.

Joel pointed at Will's chest.

Here.

Alex laughed once from the brush. A hard bark, too bright in the damp air.

Will's eyes filled. He shut them and opened them again.

Joel opened the notebook against Will's chest and wrote awkwardly:

NOT ALEX.

Will nodded.

Joel added:

IT WANTS OLD HABIT.

Will looked at the line and put his muddy good hand over it for one second. Not to hide it. To hold it down.

After that, they stopped less often but looked more carefully. Their world had become two linked circles of sight. Joel's around the trail ahead. Will's around Joel's back. When one circle bent, the other tightened. It was not enough. It was what they had.

The old trace began to smell different near late afternoon.

At first Joel thought it was only wet cedar giving way to alder. Then came the mineral smell of standing water, cold and iron-dark. The ground softened. The old tire troughs filled and joined. Through the trees, low to the left, light changed. Not brightness exactly, but an opening. Sky reflected somewhere it should not have been if they were still high on the trace.

Water.

The word rose in Joel with such force that he almost said it.

He stopped instead and pointed.

Will came even with him, close enough that their sleeves touched. He saw the paler break through the trees and shut his eyes for one beat. Relief moved over his face before he could discipline it. Not happiness. Nothing so clean. Only the body understanding that one form of work might soon become another.

Joel wrote:

WATER CLOSE. CACHE MAYBE.

Will took the pencil.

OR WRONG WATER.

Joel nodded. Good. Painful, but good.

The trace forked badly there, or seemed to. One path continued down toward the low shine. Another held higher through spruce and might have been the way they had come in. A third was not a path at all, only a place where brush had been bent by weather or animal movement or something choosing to make a suggestion.

Joel crouched and looked at the ground. Mud held too many marks and not enough. Their own tracks from yesterday should have been there, but rain had softened every edge. Mike's tread, Will's sandals, Joel's sandals: all of it had become pressure and water. He found one cut stem where Mike had cleared brush with the hatchet on the way in. He found a smear of gray mud on a stone at knee height where a pack might have rubbed. He found nothing that felt like proof.

Behind them, his own voice said, "Wait. Just wait."

Will stopped so hard Joel heard his pack settle.

Joel did not move.

The words had come from behind and slightly uphill, in the trace they had just descended. Low, flat, practical. Not a scream. Not a plea. Not even fear. A small caution at a landing. A man stopping another man from shifting a canoe too soon. Joel knew the phrase before memory supplied the scene: South Access, gravel under the hull, Alex laughing, Mike impatient, the truck still visible through black spruce. Day 1, before anything had yet become evidence. Joel's hand on the canoe, his own voice saying it once, maybe twice.

Wait. Just wait.

His voice sounded younger from the trees.

Not much younger. Only less used.

Will turned slowly toward Joel, not toward the sound.

That saved him.

Joel could see the calculation in Will's face. Body in sight. Source confirmed. The living Joel was in front of him, crouched by the fork with the notebook in one hand and the pencil in the other. The voice behind them was therefore not Joel. The rule held.

The rest of Joel did not.

Until that moment, some private part of him had kept itself outside the inventory. Alex was grief. Mike was authority. Will was panic and explanation. Joel was witness, recorder, the man who had given the woods less. The man who could still keep a little clean distance from what had been stolen.

Now the distance closed.

The thing had heard him too.

It had been near enough on the first day, or sound had carried, or the route itself had been listening long before the men knew to be quiet. It had his low practical caution. It had his small refusal. It had a piece of him from a morning when he still thought the danger was loose straps and bad footing and the ordinary stupidity of men around boats.

His hand began to shake.

Will saw it.

Will took one step toward him, then stopped, asking permission without words.

Joel wanted to say no. He wanted to keep his fear private because privacy felt like the last place a voice could not enter.

He nodded.

Will put his good hand on Joel's shoulder. The pressure was clumsy, too hard, then lighter. It was comfort without sound. It did not fix anything. It did not become language. For that reason Joel let it stay.

From uphill, in Joel's voice, came again, "Wait. Just wait."

The timing was wrong now. Too patient. A phrase made for one second stretched into a rule.

Joel looked at Will. Will looked back.

Neither answered.

Joel opened the notebook. The page blurred once and cleared.

He wrote with a hand that did not feel fully attached:

IT HEARD ME TOO.


# Chapter 20 - His Own Voice

After Joel heard himself, the route lost its last outside.

Before that, some private part of him had believed the thing belonged to Alex's death, to Mike's mistake, to Will's noise, to the brush around Second Camp. A man could bear almost anything if he could make it start somewhere after his own innocence.

His voice proved otherwise.

It had been near from the beginning, or near enough. At the landing. At the first water. At the places they had felt almost safe because nothing had yet happened. It had been present while they made ordinary mistakes and mistook ordinariness for safety. It had heard a small caution said by a quiet man at a gravel put-in, and it had kept it, or the woods had kept it, or whatever moved through the woods had no difference between hearing and keeping.

Will kept looking at him as if Joel might explain what selfhood sounded like when taken.

Joel had no explanation. He had only the notebook.

They stayed crouched at the bad fork for too long. The lower path shone with water through alder. The higher trace vanished and returned in pieces. Behind them, uphill, his own voice had gone quiet. That did not help. Silence after a voice was not absence anymore. It was only the place a voice might return from.

Will took the pencil carefully, the way a man might take a blade from a child. His injured hand made his grip awkward. He wrote on the damp page:

NOT YOU.

Joel looked at the words.

Will underlined them once, then added:

ONLY SOUND.

Joel felt something in him rise hard enough that it almost became speech. No. Don't. Stop making it smaller. Stop trying to help me by lying in a different medium.

His mouth opened.

Will saw it and went still.

Joel shut his mouth so sharply his teeth clicked. The sound seemed enormous. A small white bite of enamel in the wet air.

He took the notebook back and wrote:

IT IS USEFUL SOUND.

Then, because that was not enough:

THAT IS WORSE.

Will read it and did not argue. The mercy of that cost him. Joel could see it in the muscles at his jaw, in the way his eyes wanted to move past the page and make a theory out of everything.

Joel pointed to the lower path, then held up one hand. Wait by sight. He crouched and studied the mud.

The rain had taken most of yesterday. Their tracks had come down this way or not. Mike had led. Will had followed, talking then because they still allowed talking, because the cabin had still been a possible answer, because Alex had been dead but Mike alive, which had seemed at the time like a different category of disaster. Joel looked for Mike's boot tread and found only oblong pressures filled with brown water. He looked for the diagonal scratch of Will's sandals and found roots, leaf rot, and a black feather stuck to mud by its own wet barbs.

The feather made him remember the raven at the put-in, its call breaking off over the trees while Alex snapped a strap and said something ugly about wilderness ambiance. Had the thing heard the motel ice machine and the room door and Will coughing behind the wall? Had it heard Mike clearing his throat before he was even in the woods? Joel hated the thought because it made the trip infected backward. The old landing, the first portage, the joke about poems, the food barrel latch, the hollow knock they had blamed on rope. All of it rearranged itself under the pressure of his voice from the trees.

Nothing had changed in the past.

Everything had.

Will touched Joel's sleeve and pointed.

On the left edge of the lower path, a cedar branch had been cut clean through at knee height. The cut was pale under rain, not new-new, not old. Hatchet work. Mike's work, probably. Yesterday, on the way in, he had been making the old trace more real by cutting what resisted it.

Joel nodded. Lower.

They moved toward the water.

The ground softened within twenty yards. Mud took their footprints and folded them into itself. Alder crowded the path and slapped water onto their sleeves. Joel went first, then stopped, turned, and confirmed Will by sight. Will had stopped rolling his eyes at the checks. He endured them now with a concentration that looked almost angry. Joel understood. To be watched every fifteen steps was to be reminded every fifteen steps that you could become a sound in the wrong place.

Once, from behind them, his own voice said, "Wait. Just wait."

It was placed well. Not too close. Not theatrical. The exact distance a man might stand behind another on a bad portage, asking him not to shift a canoe too soon.

Joel did not turn.

Will did. Not toward the voice, but toward Joel.

That was the rule doing its work. It should have comforted him. Instead it made him feel briefly, stupidly, jealous of the rule. Will trusted the page faster than he trusted Joel's face now, and perhaps that was why they were alive.

Joel lifted two fingers to his eyes. Will nodded.

They kept going.

The water smell strengthened. It was not open lake, not wind and distance. It was narrow water, tannin and mud, old leaves soaking under alder roots. The kind of channel that took a canoe because it had to, not because it welcomed one. Through the trees came the dull shine of it, broken by black stems.

Joel had imagined the canoe cache as an object. Two boats, gear under spruce, the route returning to force and balance. He had wanted the cache with a child's stupid faith: if they reached it, one kind of terror would end. The woods had allowed him that desire because the woods did not care what men wanted before it ruined the wanting.

The cache was not where it should have been.

For three seconds Joel thought they had missed it. Then he saw the split granite face, the three dead spruce in a row, the bent cedar Mike had marked on the map. He saw the low roof of boughs they had made over the gear. He saw the dark line where the better canoe's hull should have been hidden under spruce.

The line was wrong.

He stopped so abruptly Will bumped his pack.

Will's good hand came up, caught Joel's strap, and then released. He looked over Joel's shoulder.

The damaged canoe was still under spruce boughs near the high-water line, the aluminum patch on its thwart dull through the green. The boughs over it had been moved. Not torn off. Not scattered. Moved aside and laid in a neat fan on the mud, their cut ends aligned as if someone had admired the logic of covering and then misunderstood disorder.

The better canoe had been dragged six or seven feet from where they had left it. It sat half out of brush, angled toward the water, its keel resting in a fresh smear of mud. The painter rope ran from its front handle to an alder stem and looped twice around the trunk. The knot at the end was neither a bowline nor a trucker's hitch nor any of Will's tidy canoe knots. It was a hard, wet clot of rope, ugly and effective.

Joel could hear Will breathing through his nose.

The food barrel was where it had been, hung badly and too low from a spruce limb because none of them had cared about animal standards when the cabin had still existed as hope. Its blue side showed through brush. The black lid ring appeared closed.

One dry bag had been pulled partly from under the gear tarp and left in the mud. It was the long one with the tent bodies and some clothing, the one they had decided was too heavy to carry inland and not important enough to guard. Mud covered one end. Water had pooled around the roll-top.

The rod tube lay beside it.

Will stepped forward.

Joel caught his sleeve.

Will's face came around fast. For a moment Joel thought he would wrench free. Not because of the canoe. Because of the rods. They were a piece of Will from before: his hands working line through guides, his voice explaining why a lake with no visible structure might still hold fish along the downwind shore, his body made quiet by attention rather than fear. Fishing gave his talking a place to go. It made waiting active.

Joel shook his head and pointed to the ground between them.

Stop. Look first.

Will swallowed. He nodded once, too hard.

They stood at the edge of the cache and inspected it like men approaching an animal that might only be sleeping.

No tracks made sense. Their own prints from yesterday had been softened by rain and overrun by new disturbances: long smears in mud where the canoe had dragged; two round punched places that might have been the ends of branches; shallow half-moons from Will's sandals or Joel's or Mike's, all blurred into each other. Near the alder stem, the mud held a line of compressed grass as if someone had knelt. Or as if the rope had been pulled low. Or as if a body had crouched there without leaving a body-shaped truth.

Joel pointed to the better canoe. Then to Will. Then made a slow circling motion with one finger: inspect.

Will shook his head and pointed at Joel.

Joel understood. He was in back. He would steer. He needed to decide if the hull was trustworthy.

They approached together.

The rope knot tightened when Joel put weight on it. It had been tied by force, not skill. The painter had bitten into the alder bark. Two loops crossed each other, then a series of jammed half-turns had been pulled down until the wet rope hardened. A human hand could have done it in panic. Mike could have done it with one glove on and no patience. Alex could have made worse and called it fine. Will would never have left such a thing unless he wanted to make a point about other people's standards.

Will crouched beside it. His mouth shaped, impossible to stop, Can it--

Joel put two fingers hard against Will's shoulder.

Will closed his mouth.

The question hung between them anyway.

Can it tie knots?

Joel took the notebook out.

DOESN'T MATTER.

He added:

CUT IT.

Will stared at the page a second longer than necessary. Then he took the hatchet from his belt.

Cutting instead of untying felt wasteful and correct. They had begun the trip as men who preserved rope, packed spare cord, debated knots, saved lengths that might be useful later. Now Joel held the painter taut against the alder while Will chopped carefully through the outer loop. The first strike thudded into wet fiber. The second opened white strands. The third severed the line and left the knot clamped to the tree like a fist.

The loose rope fell into mud.

From the water, behind the reeds, came a hollow knock.

Both men froze.

The loaded canoe was not loaded yet. The damaged canoe sat under boughs. The better canoe lay at their feet. No hull moved.

Another knock came from farther out, beyond the first bend of the channel where fog had collected in a low seam above the water.

It was the old sound. Hull on stone. Paddle against gunwale. Rope tapping aluminum. Ordinary enough to have a dozen causes until all the causes were in sight and still.

Will wrote with the pencil braced against his thigh:

BOAT SOUND / NO BOAT.

Joel nodded.

Then the food barrel latch clicked.

The barrel hung fifteen feet away, visible through spruce, black ring closed, blue body swaying not at all.

Click.

Will turned his head only far enough to see it. His discipline had become painful to watch.

Click.

The sound came now from behind the damaged canoe.

Joel wrote:

BARREL VISIBLE.

Will tapped the line once. Yes. I know. The tap left a crescent of mud on the paper.

They worked.

Work was the only answer that did not add material. Joel checked the better hull first. No crack he could see. No puncture. No new gouge deep enough to matter. He ran his hands along the outside while Will held the boat tilted. Cold water spilled from a shallow pocket near the seat and ran over Joel's knuckles. The caned front seat had one broken strand. The back seat was sound. The thwarts held. The shortened painter could be retied later, badly but by them.

The damaged canoe was worse. Mike's repair on the cracked thwart still held when Joel pressed it, but the aluminum splint had shifted and one wrap of cord had loosened. It would float. It might carry gear. It might fail under portage strain or current. Two men with two canoes made less sense than two men in one boat, but one boat meant all weight, all risk, all hope in a single hull.

Joel set his palm on the better canoe.

Will looked at him.

Joel held up one finger.

One boat.

Will looked at the damaged canoe, the gear, the fog, the channel. His face did not protest. His body did. He shifted once as if the old argument had entered his knees. Two boats gave redundancy. Two boats let one man survive if the other went wrong. Two boats also meant more loading, more rope, more distance between them, more turns of the head, more sounds made by things they could not see at the same time.

Will took the notebook.

ONE BOAT = IF LOSE IT WE DIE.

Joel wrote:

TWO BOATS = WE SEPARATE.

Will read it. His mouth went tight.

Joel added:

STAY VISIBLE.

Will nodded.

They made a pile of what would go. Food barrel, because hunger was not optional. Filter. Stove and one pot. Map case, though grit had gotten into the seal and the paper inside felt damp at the edges. First aid. Tarp. Sleeping bags, one still damp enough to weigh like an animal. Mike's repair kit. The hatchet. Two paddles and the cracked repaired paddle as spare. Alex's cracked headlamp, less an object now than a debt. The Highlander key stayed in Joel's inner pocket, zipped behind fabric, checked once by touch and then not touched again.

They left the mud-soaked tent bag. Too heavy. Too wet. Too much time. They left extra clothes that were no longer extra in any meaningful sense. They left cookware, a small sack of trash, the bent tent stakes, the roll of cord that had sat in water and knotted itself into uselessness. They left Alex's hot sauce bottle in the cracked headlamp bag because neither of them could decide to carry it and neither could throw it away.

Will picked up the rod tube last.

Joel looked away at first, giving him privacy he knew was false. Privacy required a world where a man could step ten feet off and still remain himself. Still, Joel turned to the canoe and tightened a strap that did not need tightening.

Behind him, plastic scraped mud.

Will had opened the tube and taken out the two rods. One had a fine crack near the second guide. The other looked all right. He held them awkwardly with his bandaged hand, their tips trembling in the damp air. The reels had grit in them. Line clung in wet loops.

For one moment, with the rods in his hand, Will looked almost normal. Not better. Not safe. But placed in a shape Joel recognized. A man at a lake, making small decisions about weight and lure and angle. A man who would say, quietly and with too much precision, that fishing was not about catching fish, not exactly. It was about keeping an instrument in the water long enough for the world to tell you something.

Will looked at the channel.

The channel gave them nothing but fog and the second hollow knock, softer now, from where no canoe moved.

Will closed the rod tube.

He did not ask.

He laid the rods under the damaged canoe they were abandoning and covered them with the same boughs that had been moved aside. His hand worked slowly because of the bandage. He put one spruce bough, then another, then corrected the first because a tip still showed. The care broke Joel worse than a sob would have. Will could leave them, but he could not leave them uncovered.

When he finished, he stayed crouched.

Joel waited.

Will took the notebook and wrote:

TELL ME THIS IS NOT STUPID.

The sentence sat there, too human and too dangerous even in pencil.

Joel wanted to write, It isn't. He wanted to write, You can buy new rods. He wanted to write some ordinary lie about objects and survival and later. But later had become a superstition, and the rods were not the point.

He wrote:

IT COSTS.

Will read it. His face changed once and closed.

He wrote:

YES.

They loaded the canoe low. Food barrel near the center. Heavy bag against it. Sleeping bags compressed badly under the thwart. Map case flat and reachable. Hatchet tucked beside Will but not under anything. Spare paddle lashed where Joel could see it. Every buckle they closed sounded too loud. Every strap pull was a small confession. The thing had all of these sounds already. That did not make giving them again feel harmless.

From the alder behind the cache, Mike cleared his throat.

Will did not look.

Joel did, because the sound went through him before the rule caught it. Only a quick glance. Brush. Wet black stems. No body.

"We're fine," Mike said.

Joel's hand closed on the canoe gunwale.

The words had once been a gift even when false. Men used false gifts constantly. We're fine. Almost there. Weather's moving off. This will hold. Now the phrase had no structure inside it. It was an empty shape wearing Mike's mouth.

Will touched Joel's sleeve. Two fingers. Gentle.

Joel looked back at him.

Will pointed at the notebook.

Joel opened it, but Will took the pencil.

NOT MIKE.

Then, below that:

NOT JOEL.

Joel stared at the second line.

Will underlined nothing. He did not try to comfort him again. He had learned that distinction at least: the page could mark fact without trying to repair fact.

Joel tapped both lines.

They carried the canoe to water.

The launch took too long. Everything silent took too long. Joel held the back while Will steadied the front with one good hand and one bandaged hand wrapped in plastic. The mud sucked at Joel's sandals. Cold water entered over the straps and took the heat from his feet at once. The canoe rocked under the weight. The food barrel shifted. Joel stopped it with his knee and almost said wait.

The word rose in his throat with the exact shape the woods already had.

He swallowed until his throat hurt.

Behind them, from the cache, his own voice said, "Wait. Just wait."

This time it sounded nearer. Not better. Nearer.

Will's eyes went to Joel's face. Not to the voice. The rule again. The rule, working.

Joel shook his head once.

Together they pushed the canoe free.

A rope tapped somewhere in the fog. Then another tap, nearer the far bank. The shortened painter lay visible across the front pack, wet and muddy and theirs. The sound went on anyway, a patient little knock against nothing they could see.

Will climbed in first, awkward and careful, placing his knees and then sitting low in the front. Joel held the canoe while it tried to turn broadside. He looked once at the cache: damaged canoe under spruce, rod tube hidden, wet gear abandoned, the strange knot still clenched around the alder stem. A place of human decisions already losing their shape.

The food barrel latch clicked from the trees.

Joel stepped in.

The canoe dipped, steadied, and took them.

For a moment they drifted without paddling. The channel ahead was narrow and dark. Fog took the far bank in pieces and gave it back as alder, then shadow, then nothing. Behind them the cache disappeared faster than it should have, not by magic, only by the ordinary fact of bends and brush and water accepting whatever men pushed onto it.

Joel lifted his paddle.

Will turned enough for Joel to see his face.

No words.

Joel set the blade in the water, and the canoe moved.


# Chapter 21 - Bad Boat

The bad boat was not bad enough to abandon and not good enough to trust.

That was the shape of everything now.

Joel sat in back with the Highlander key zipped inside his jacket and the shortened painter rope coiled where he could see it. Will sat in front, shoulders hunched, his injured hand wrapped in a plastic bag and taped at the wrist. The bag made a soft white shine whenever he lifted the paddle. It was the loudest thing about him.

The canoe rode low.

Food barrel just ahead of center. Tarp and damp sleeping bags wedged under the middle thwart. Map case flat in the bottom with grit still caught in the seal. Stove, pot, filter, first-aid kit. Mike's repair kit. Hatchet by Will's right knee. Spare paddle lashed on Joel's side where his hand could find it without searching. They had loaded the boat the way men loaded a sentence they were afraid to say, each object made to carry more than it should.

The canoe floated clean enough at first. That was worse than an obvious failure. A cracked hull would have decided for them. A split seam, a broken seat, a hole taking water from the first push. Instead the boat accepted the weight and settled into the tannin-dark channel as if consent meant safety.

Will looked back once.

Joel held up one hand, palm down. Easy.

Will nodded and faced forward.

They had decided on taps because taps were almost not speech. One tap on the hull meant stop. Two meant look. A hand flat meant wait. A pointed finger meant direction or danger or I mean that thing and not the other thing. A fist meant hold. A hand to the chest meant me. Two fingers to the eyes meant confirm by sight. They had written these into the notebook at the cache, though both men already understood that writing a rule did not make the body obey it.

The old canoe language was gone. Draw. Back. Left. Right. Hold up. Watch that rock. Wait. They had used those words for years, casually and without gratitude. Now every missing word made the boat worse.

Joel dipped his paddle and corrected the back end. Will pulled once on the right. The front nosed toward a black stitch of open water between alder stems. Fog lay low over the channel. It did not cover the water completely. It exposed pieces, then took them away.

Behind them, at the cache, the food barrel latch clicked.

Joel kept paddling.

Will's shoulders lifted, then settled. He did not turn. The blue barrel sat in the canoe in front of Joel, black ring closed, its side pressed against a wet dry bag. Visible. Accounted for. Heavy as fact.

Click.

Joel watched the barrel instead of the brush. It did not move.

Click.

Will tapped the gunwale once with two fingers. Not a signal. A reflex. He stopped himself and put his hand back on the shaft.

The channel bent left. Alder brushed both sides. Their branches stroked the gunwales with small dry fingernails, though the leaves were wet and shining. The canoe went through slowly because speed made sound and sound made decisions. Joel could feel every scrape in his knees.

He had trusted boats before this trip in the same unnoticed way he trusted floors. They were objects that held you above what would otherwise take you. Even when they rocked, even when they shipped water, even when Alex did some showy incompetent thing in front and laughed at his own balance, the boat had remained a boat. A human bargain with water. Shape, weight, skill, and buoyancy.

Now it was only one thin hull between two men and the cold fact beneath them. One boat. One route. One key in Joel's pocket. The arithmetic was simple and therefore indecent.

The first knock came from ahead.

Not loud. A hollow tap of wood or aluminum, placed somewhere beyond the fogged bend.

Will stopped paddling.

Joel let the canoe glide. He lifted his blade and held it over the water until drops fell from the tip. Drip. Drip. Drip. Each drop made a small ring and then no evidence.

The knock came again, farther right.

There could have been another canoe beyond the bend. There could have been a submerged log lifting with current, a branch tapping stone, a loose paddle in a boat they had not seen. There could have been a man with help, a ranger, another party, a fisherman who would look at them and understand none of it but still have a radio, a motor, a human explanation.

Joel knew how much hope sounded like a reason.

He touched two fingers to his eyes, then pointed at Will.

Will looked back and nodded. Visible.

Joel pointed ahead, then held up his flat hand. Wait by sight.

They drifted until the current gave out against a mat of drowned grass. The bend opened. No canoe. No person. No bright hull pulled into reeds. Only a fallen spruce leaning from the far bank, its root mass black and wet, and a crescent of foam caught against sticks.

The knock came once more from behind them.

Will's mouth tightened.

Joel shook his head.

They moved on.

Silence made them bad at everything. In ordinary travel, one word from the back corrected a bad angle before it became a problem. One curse made a submerged rock visible to the man in front. A laugh turned error into nothing. Without that, the canoe became a stubborn object. It found every shallow, every hidden branch, every place the current pressed sideways against alder roots.

Will's injured hand cost them time. He could paddle with it, but he could not grab quickly. When the canoe slid toward a snag, his good hand crossed awkwardly over the bad and the plastic bag squeaked against the shaft. He looked down each time as if betrayed by his own body. Joel knew that look. He had seen it on Will as a boy in church basements when a model rocket failed or a chess game turned because he had missed something obvious. Will did not hate failure most. He hated the moment when the world proved he had been using the wrong rule.

At the next shallow, the canoe ground to a stop.

Joel tapped once.

Will held still.

The boat had settled on gravel hidden under six inches of tea-colored water. The current pressed from the side, not enough to move them, enough to turn them if Joel gave it slack. He shifted his weight. The boat creaked.

Both men froze.

The sound was ordinary. A loaded canoe flexing under two tired men. But ordinary sounds no longer stayed in their assigned places. They went out into the woods and returned wearing someone else's need.

Joel set his paddle across the gunwales and leaned over the side. Water entered cold over his knuckles. The gravel under them was pale and small. No crack that he could see. No widening seam. The boat was hung, not broken.

He tapped the hull once, then pointed to himself and over the side.

Will shook his head sharply. His eyes went to the bank, then the fog, then back to Joel.

Joel understood the argument without paper. If Joel got out, they were slower. If Joel got out, there was a moment when the boat had one man in it and one man in the water. If Joel got out, cold took more from him. If they stayed, the current could turn the hull broadside and pin it harder. There was no choice that did not introduce a new way to fail.

Joel pointed to the shallow, then made a pushing motion. He pointed to Will, then the front rope.

Will's jaw moved once around a word he did not let out. He nodded.

Joel slid over the side into water that reached just above his knees.

The cold bit cleanly. It took the heat from his calves, then his thighs, then made a narrow cold line upward through his body. His hiking sandals sank into gravel and mud. He held the back handle with both hands and lifted enough to lighten the hull.

Will got out at the front more slowly. His bandaged hand stayed high. With the other hand he took the painter and pulled. The canoe rasped over gravel. The sound carried forward through the fog, a long rough admission.

From downstream, Alex said, "Hold up."

Will stopped with the painter tight across his body.

Joel shook his head once. Hard.

Alex said it again, farther away. Same brisk annoyance as a man whose rope had caught on a beaver dam. Same useful command. Same dead mouth.

Will's eyes shone in the gray light. Not tears exactly. Cold, exhaustion, the body's stupid water. He turned forward and pulled.

The boat came free.

They walked it another ten yards before the channel deepened. Joel climbed in first because if the back swung loose they would lose the boat's direction. His wet legs dragged mud over the bottom. Will got in with a quiet broken motion, planting his good hand, folding the bad one close, settling low.

Neither looked back.

For a while they made progress.

The channel narrowed, widened, and narrowed again. Beaver work had changed it since any map had cared. Old sticks silvered under water. Chewed points rose from the mud like sharpened stakes. On one bank a lodge humped under alder, its upper sticks black with rain. The place smelled of wet bark and old leaves and animal labor. The beavers had made a world suited to themselves. Men entered it by dragging aluminum across the evidence.

Twice they slid over submerged dams. Once Joel got out again and felt mud take his sandal to the ankle. Once Will nearly lost the front rope when his bad hand closed late and the painter burned across the plastic. His face went white. He did not cry out.

Joel stopped the boat with his thigh against the gunwale. The cold water pressed fabric to skin. He pointed to Will's hand.

Will lifted it. The plastic bag had torn at one taped edge. Water had gotten in. The bandage beneath showed a spreading gray.

Joel made for shore.

They found a strip of mud under spruce roots, not a landing but a place the canoe could be held. Joel kept one hand on the gunwale while Will knelt and unwrapped the bag. The wound across his palm looked angry, swollen at the edges, the skin too pale around it. Not infected yet, maybe. Or only not visibly enough for the word to help.

Will stared at it with the same offended attention he gave broken gear.

Joel took the notebook from inside his jacket. The cover was soft from damp. He wrote:

REWRAP. NO ARGUMENT.

Will read it and gave him a look that almost had humor in it.

He wrote beneath:

I WAS SAVING MY BIG SPEECH.

Joel surprised himself by almost smiling. The expression stopped before it reached his mouth. Humor had become another form of sound, one more thing the world might learn if they gave it air.

He took the first-aid kit from the top pack. They worked without speaking. Dry gauze, as dry as anything remained. Tape. A new plastic bag from the food scraps. More tape at the wrist. Will flexed his fingers and winced.

Across the channel, hidden in spruce, Mike cleared his throat.

The sound did what it always did. It arranged the air into expectation. Decision coming. Complaint coming. Some blunt pronouncement meant to reduce the number of possible worlds.

Will stared at Joel.

Mike said, "Just keep moving."

Not loud. Not commanding in a theatrical way. Almost tired. A better imitation than some because Mike had often sounded tired when he meant to sound certain.

Joel wrote:

NOT MIKE.

Will took the pencil.

IT KNOWS WHEN WE STOP.

Joel looked at the line.

He wanted to write: It knows sound, not time. He wanted to write: It can hear us stop. He wanted to write: We do not know what it knows. All of those were ideas shaped like small doors, and Will's mind would go through each one and open another.

Joel wrote:

MAYBE. MOVE ANYWAY.

Will read it. His eyes came up, sharp and resentful.

That was the first clear look of the afternoon: not fear, not grief, but distrust. Will was beginning to believe that Joel's restraint had become its own authority, another Mike phrase with less volume. Do not answer. Do not test. Do not say names. Keep moving, but in Joel's handwriting. The thought struck Joel with enough force that he almost wrote an apology.

He did not.

Apology was not the same as repair. It might not even be safer in pencil.

They pushed off again.

The fog thinned by degrees. The sky above it had a yellow-gray cast, late afternoon already draining from the day. Light came flat through spruce and made every wet thing show its edge. Joel checked the map when the channel widened enough to drift. The paper inside the case had dampened at the folds. He could still read the blue line, the faint bend, the small marsh sign, the next widening that might be a lake or might be another lie told at map scale. North Road Bridge was still beyond too many inches of paper. The Highlander key in his pocket pressed against his ribs when he leaned forward.

A key was an absurd object in wilderness. A little cut piece of metal and plastic waiting to become important only if a man reached a machine on a road. Before that, it was weight without use. After that, it might be everything. Joel kept touching it anyway, once each hour or each fear. The habit was dangerous because habits had sound. Zipper. Fabric. Fingernail against plastic. He forced his hand away.

The canoe bumped something under the surface.

A dull knock rose through the hull and into Joel's knees.

Will turned, eyes wide.

Joel pointed down: here. Real.

Then another bump came from the left bank.

Not echo. Not the same sound carrying. A separate hollow knock from alder roots six feet away, where no hull was moving.

Will's face changed. Joel saw the thought arrive. If it could make boat sounds from the bank, it could make damage where there was none. Or it could cover real damage with false evidence. Sound had become bad data.

Joel tapped once. Stop.

They held still in the current. The canoe turned a few degrees. Joel corrected with the smallest possible stroke. He leaned over and ran his fingers along the inside seam by his seat. Water in the bottom, but not rising. A few cups from wet gear and feet. Nothing pouring. Nothing breathing through the hull.

Will pointed ahead. His face said: continue?

Joel nodded.

Will pointed at the boat, then the water, then made a flat falling motion with his hand.

If it fails.

Joel nodded again. Yes.

Will took the notebook and braced it on the food barrel. Writing in a canoe was awkward and slow. The pencil tore at the damp page.

IF BOAT FAILS WE LOSE EVERYTHING.

Joel read the line while the canoe drifted. He could hear water sliding along the hull, each small touch precise and unhelpful.

He wrote:

BOAT FAILS HERE OR BACK. FORWARD HAS CAR.

Will stared at the word CAR.

It looked childish on the page. Not Highlander. Not take-out. Not North Road Bridge. Just CAR, the object reduced to its promise. Doors. Engine. Heat. A road. A machine that could carry a body without asking him to paddle.

Will wrote:

IF ROAD STILL THERE.

Joel remembered Mike's map spread on the hood before the trip, the North Road Bridge drawn with the same printed confidence as lakes and portages and the cabin that had given them nothing. Human marks aged badly. Roads became brush. Cabins became mouse nests. Bridges washed out, or did not, with no interest in men's plans.

He wrote:

IF NOT, STILL FORWARD.

Will did not answer.

The next bend gave them the print.

Will saw it first. Two taps on the hull.

Joel looked right.

Below a wall of alder roots, in a small tongue of exposed mud, a depression held brown water. Broad at one end, long at another. Not clear enough to name and too clear to ignore. The edges had softened in the rain. One side showed something like a heel. The other splayed into torn mud. Bear if you needed bear. Human if you needed worse. A kneeling place if you needed a third category and had no word for it.

Will pointed to the mark. Then to his eyes. Then lifted both hands slightly from his paddle, palms up.

Joel shook his head.

Will pointed again, harder. Evidence.

Joel pointed at the channel ahead, then at the sinking light.

Will's jaw worked. The old Will would have said there is a difference between not knowing and refusing to know. The old Joel would have told him to save it for camp and meant later, genuinely later, a time when speech was not a gift to the trees.

No time. No answer.

The canoe drifted past the print.

Joel looked at it until the bend took it. He wanted it to become one thing before it vanished. It did not. The mud held its shape without confessing.

Late afternoon made the water look stiller than it was. The channel opened into a slow reach between black spruce, no wider than a narrow road, with a gravel bar shouldering out from the left bank. The bar rose only a foot above the water. A driftwood snag lay across it, silver and smooth where old floods had rolled it. Behind it, spruce grew too tight for tents. Beyond the spruce, nothing Joel could see promised higher ground.

They needed a camp and did not want one.

Camps were where human routine collected. Tarp lines. Food. Zippers. Fire. Bodies turning in sleep. Names nearly spoken. Every camp taught the thing a room and then asked men to lie down inside it.

Joel pointed to the bar.

Will looked at it, then downstream, then back at Joel.

No one liked the decision. That made it likely.

They landed with care that took too long. Joel stepped into ankle-deep water and held the canoe while Will climbed out stiffly. Gravel shifted under both of them with small clicking sounds. They unloaded only what could not stay in the boat overnight or what they needed within arm's reach. Food barrel. Tarp. Sleeping bags. Filter. Notebook. Hatchet. The rest stayed lashed. If they had to move fast, the boat had to be more than a pile of choices.

Joel pulled the canoe up until its front rested on gravel and its back still touched water. Then he pulled it higher, hated the scrape, and pulled again. He tied the shortened painter around the driftwood snag, then around his own wrist with a loose loop he could free if he had to. He sat for a moment studying the knot.

Will watched him.

Joel wrote:

IF BOAT MOVES, I WAKE.

Will took the pencil.

IF YOU SLEEP.

Joel read it. The line had no accusation in handwriting, which meant it had all of them. He had not slept properly since before Mike died. Will had not either. Their faces had become unfamiliar from fatigue, stripped to bone and wet beard and eyes that looked older than their bodies.

Joel wrote:

IF EITHER SLEEPS, GOOD.

Will gave a silent breath that might once have been a laugh.

They made the tarp low against the driftwood and two spruce roots, more cover from dew than shelter. No fire. Smoke would not matter much, but flame made a center, and centers drew old habits. Food stayed beside them instead of hung. Animal risk had not disappeared, but climbing into brush in near dark to hang a barrel felt like obeying a worse animal. Joel set the hatchet where both could see it and neither could reach for it accidentally.

They filtered water at the edge. The pump made a soft, regular squeak.

After ten strokes Joel stopped.

The same squeak continued across the channel.

Will's head came up.

The filter in Joel's hands was still.

Squeak.

Squeak.

Then water dripping from a lifted paddle. Slow drops. Exact drops.

Their paddles lay on the gravel beside the canoe.

Will closed his eyes for one second. When he opened them, his face had hardened into something almost calm.

They finished filtering. Joel hated each pump. The sound went out and did not belong to him once it left.

Dinner was cold food eaten with fingers. Tortillas damp at the edges. Peanut butter from a pouch. A handful of trail mix with grit in it. Will sorted raisins out without seeming to know he was doing it, then stopped and shoved the whole handful into his mouth. Alex would have said something about that. Joel knew the shape of the joke without hearing words. He looked at the water until the impulse passed.

The light lowered. Fog returned from the channel in strips. The canoe sat where they had left it, ugly and necessary, its aluminum side dull, its shortened rope running from the handle to the snag to Joel's wrist. Bad boat. Good boat. Only boat.

Will took the notebook.

He wrote slowly because his hand hurt.

IF IT ONLY REPEATS, IT CAN'T LIE ABOUT NEW FACTS.

Joel read the sentence twice.

Will wrote under it:

WE CAN TEST.

The bar seemed to narrow around them.

Joel took the pencil.

NO.

Will did not look at him. He kept looking at the page, as if the page were the less frightening face.

He wrote:

TESTING IS NOT ANSWERING.

Joel wrote:

YES IT IS.

Will's mouth opened, closed. He pressed the pencil so hard the point broke. He stared at the broken graphite with an anger so tired it had no heat.

Joel found the spare pencil stub in the notebook pocket and handed it to him.

Will wrote:

WE NEED TO KNOW WHAT IT UNDERSTANDS.

Joel almost wrote, It does not understand. But that was faith disguised as caution. They knew less than they needed. They had survived by acting on assumptions and calling them rules because rules were easier to obey than guesses.

He wrote:

KNOWING COSTS SOUND.

Will read it and shook his head once.

He wrote:

WRITING COSTS NOTHING.

Joel looked at the dark line of the opposite bank. The spruce there stood close enough together that dusk gathered between trunks before it gathered on the water. The channel made a narrow mirror of the sky, then broke it around stones and drowned sticks.

Writing removed tone. That was why it had saved them. It made grief blunt and orders plain. It kept Will from making a sentence into a door the thing could walk through. But writing had begun to collect its own false comfort. It felt safe because it was silent. It felt private because the page stayed between their hands. Both feelings might be wrong.

Joel wrote:

WE DON'T KNOW THAT.

Will read it.

His face changed, not toward fear exactly. Toward insult. The last room had lost its lock.

He wrote:

THEN WE HAVE NOTHING.

Joel wanted to deny it. The want rose with the old physical urgency of speech. He could see Will at sixteen, sitting in a church basement on a folding chair, explaining to a youth pastor why the lesson's metaphor did not work if anyone cared about actual shepherding. He could see Will at twenty-six, drunk in Joel's first apartment, correcting Mike about a canoe neither of them yet owned. He could see Will two mornings ago, line in hand, trying to believe fish still existed as fish and not as sound waiting to be used.

Joel wrote:

WE HAVE SIGHT.

Then:

WE HAVE BOAT.

Then, after a pause:

WE HAVE EACH OTHER IF WE DON'T GIVE THAT AWAY.

Will looked at the last line for a long time.

Across the channel, in Will's own voice, someone said, "There is a difference."

Will shut his eyes.

The phrase had been his once, ordinary as a cough, said on the first real day when distinctions still helped men feel clever. There is a difference. Between echo and voice. Between a map and ground.
