He was not being lured by belief. That was the terrible part. He did not think Alex was there. He did not think Mike waited with the key or that another canoe had appeared up the trail. He was not confused in the simple way Joel could fight. He was being drawn by knowledge, by the possibility that the thing's limit could be mapped if a man could stand close enough to the edge and not fall.

Joel grabbed the notebook and wrote:

YOU ARE NOT STUDYING IT. IT IS MOVING YOU.

Will read it. The muscles around his mouth twitched.

He wrote:

BOTH CAN BE TRUE.

Joel remembered the night under the tarp, Mike is dead because we did not go after him, Mike is dead because it took him, both can be true. Will had always been good at refusing false choices. It had made him maddening around campfires and useful at broken gear and impossible now.

Joel took the pencil and pressed hard.

WE GO TO CAR.

Will looked at him and mouthed, without sound, Car if real.

The final portage waited.

They arranged the load because arguing silently did not make the work less physical. Joel would take the canoe first. Will would take the heavy pack and the paddles lashed to the side. The food barrel would go in the pack for the first push, a bad choice that made the pack too round and wrong but kept it off the open landing. The map case went under Will's shoulder strap. The hatchet stayed at Joel's belt. The notebook went into Joel's jacket, then Will held out his good hand for it.

Joel shook his head.

Will's eyes hardened.

Joel kept the notebook.

The decision had the weight of theft. The notebook had become shared shelter, shared mouth, shared law. Taking it back into Joel's jacket said what Joel could not say aloud: I do not trust what you will do with language.

Will bent, picked up the heavy pack with one good hand and one damaged hand, and shouldered it before Joel could help. The straps bit wrong. He adjusted the left strap with his teeth, then used his forearm to shove the pack higher. The paddles knocked against the food barrel inside the lash and made a low wooden clack.

From the trail, the same clack answered.

Will smiled.

Joel hated the smile. It was thin and exhausted and almost triumphant, the smile of a man whose worst idea kept receiving evidence.

Joel lifted the canoe. The world vanished into green Kevlar and scratched inner hull. The yoke settled on his shoulders with an old pain that would once have meant only portage, only work, only the private satisfaction of doing a hard thing without complaint. Now the canoe blocked his upper sight. He could see the trail below the rim in strips: mud, stone, Will's sandals, roots wet enough to shine.

He tapped the hull twice.

Will moved.

The trail accepted them badly. Within ten yards the brush closed around the canoe ends. Alder scraped the hull. Spruce needles raked Joel's forearms where his sleeves had ridden up. The yoke pressed into the knot of muscle at the base of his neck. He adjusted by feel, left hand on the front thwart, right hand steadying the back rim, each step chosen before weight came down.

Will walked ahead for the first stretch because Joel needed to see his legs under the canoe. The pack made him broad and slow. The paddles lashed to it stuck out like broken antlers and caught on brush. Twice he had to stop and back up, not speaking, while Joel balanced the canoe and waited for him to free the blade.

Behind them, Mike cleared his throat.

Will kept moving.

Mike said, "Just keep moving."

Will stopped.

Joel could see only his calves and the bottom of the pack below the canoe rim.

Joel tapped the hull once. Move.

Will did not.

From ahead, in Mike's voice, closer than it should have been, came: "Will. Key's here."

The name struck harder than the lie. The key was in Joel's jacket, zipped under two layers, a hard small fact against his ribs. Mike's voice had no way to know it except through what they had said, what they had checked, what they had made important. Key. Car. Bridge. Road. The thing had learned the shape of their hope, if not the hope itself.

Will shifted.

Joel dropped one knee to lower the canoe enough to see over the rim. Will stood where the trail bent left around a knot of young spruce. He was facing not down the trail but into the trees on the right, where no trail went. His shoulders were high under the pack. His injured hand hung slightly open.

Alex said from the right, "Quit mothering me."

Will closed his eyes.

Joel lowered the canoe off his shoulders. He meant to set it down quietly. Fatigue ruined intention. The hull slid, caught a spruce branch, and crashed through brush beside the trail with a sound like something falling into water.

Will opened his eyes.

Joel stepped over the yoke and grabbed his sleeve.

"That's not Mike," Joel said.

The words came out rough and full-sized. Too loud. A failure with breath behind it.

Will looked back at him.

For one instant he was not the man from the last seven days. He was the boy from church basements, the man with too many hobbies, the friend who could not leave a wrong thing uncorrected. His face held fear, yes. But also apology. Also the old infuriating light of having found the hinge in an argument.

"I know," Will said.

Joel tightened his grip.

Will did not pull away hard. He only turned his wrist with a small practical motion, the way he might free a snagged strap.

Then he turned toward the voice.


# Chapter 25 - Will Answers

Joel reached him before the trees took him, but not before Will spoke again.

"Say something new," Will called into the brush.

His voice cracked on new. It was not a shout exactly. The trail swallowed any full shout before it could travel. It was a harsh, carrying thing, thrown ahead into young spruce and alder and the low black spaces between trunks.

"Come on," Will said. "If you understand, say something new."

Joel had one hand on the back of Will's vest and the other on the notebook inside his own jacket. For a second he was absurdly aware of the small arrangement of facts against his body: the notebook damp at the edges, the Highlander key zipped in the inner pocket, the hatchet at his belt, his own pulse beating against the key as if the car were already trying to start.

The woods answered in Mike's voice.

"We're fine."

Will gave one breath of laughter. No pleasure in it. Only recognition.

"Old," he said.

Joel jerked hard on the vest.

Will twisted out of it with a strength Joel would not have credited to him an hour earlier. The heavy pack shifted on Will's shoulders. The paddles lashed to it knocked once against the food barrel inside, a low wooden clack. The same clack came from deeper in the portage, farther ahead than either man had gone.

Will looked toward it.

Joel pointed back to the trail. Back to the canoe he had dropped half into brush. Back to the strip of mud that meant route, however poor a promise route had become.

Will shook his head without looking at him.

From the left, Alex said, "Hold up."

Will turned his face toward the voice and said, "Old."

It was worse than if he had believed it. Joel could have fought belief. He could have put his hand over Will's mouth and hauled him bodily back, could have written ALEX DEAD until the pencil tore through the page. But Will knew. That was the terrible clarity of it. He was not following Alex. He was following the failure in the imitation, the way the phrase had been placed wrong, the way the woods could put a friend's voice in the air and still miss the person inside it.

Joel pulled the notebook out and opened it against his forearm.

He wrote:

THIS IS HOW IT MOVES YOU.

Will glanced down, read, and jabbed one finger at the woods. Then at the line. Then he made a small, precise cutting motion in the air.

Not moves. Shows.

Joel wrote over his own words, hard enough to blur them:

MOVE NOW.

The reply came from ahead in Will's own voice.

"There is a difference."

The sentence had Will's dry weight, his exact little lift on difference, the mild irritation of a man correcting a term no one else cared about. But it did not belong to the moment. It answered nothing. It made no distinction. It was a hinge with no door on either side.

Will's face changed.

Not wonder. Not fear. Something leaner and more dangerous. The expression of a man who had seen a machine skip a tooth.

"Old," he said again, softer.

Joel clamped a hand over his mouth.

Will's eyes came to him then. Close. Furious. Not only furious. Hurt. The look hit Joel with its oldness. They had been boys once in church basements that smelled of carpet glue and powdered lemonade, standing in lines while adults told them when to speak and when to bow their heads. Will had always mouthed jokes at the wrong time. Joel had always tried not to laugh. Later, when Joel stopped believing and Will stopped caring enough to argue about belief, the old habit stayed: Will talked, Joel withheld, both of them trusting the other to understand the difference between silence and abandonment.

Now Joel's hand was on Will's mouth.

The woods had reduced friendship to force.

Will pulled Joel's hand away with his good hand. His injured hand hung open and swollen at his side. The bandage had gone gray at the palm and red where it crossed the knuckles.

"Listen to it," Will whispered.

Joel shook his head.

"No, listen. It can't answer the question."

The whisper was almost nothing, but it was air. It was material. Joel felt it leave Will's mouth and become part of the place.

From behind them, near the landing they could no longer see, Mike cleared his throat.

Joel turned despite himself.

Nothing. No flash of Mike's orange PFD. No body at the bend. Only alder leaves, a strip of standing water in an old boot print, the dropped canoe half-hidden where its green hull leaned against spruce. The trail behind them looked longer than it had been.

Mike's voice said, "Just keep moving."

The phrase was almost practical enough to obey.

Will smiled. It was small and awful.

"You see?" he whispered. "It contradicts itself."

Joel wrote one-handed:

NO. IT REPEATS USEFUL THINGS.

Will read it and almost laughed. He made a little choking sound instead. Pain, fatigue, triumph, grief, all caught in the damaged gate of his throat.

"That's what I mean," he said.

From ahead, in Joel's voice, came: "Wait. Just wait."

Both men froze.

The sound of Joel's own voice did not feel like hearing another person. It felt like finding a handprint on the inside of his chest. Low, flat, nearly embarrassed by its own urgency. The phrase he had given the water days ago without ceremony. The practical phrase. The little restraint that had become the thing's piece of him.

Will turned toward him at once. Not toward the sound. Toward the living Joel.

For a half second the rule worked. Sight beat sound. Will confirmed him with his eyes. Joel was there, breathing, pencil in hand, jacket zipped over the key. The false Joel had come from ahead, and the real Joel stood beside him.

Will's face softened.

Then the false Joel said again, from deeper in the portage, "Wait. Just wait."

This time the timing was wrong. Too clean after the first. No impatience in it. No body behind the restraint. Will heard the wrongness and leaned toward it as if toward a lamp.

Joel grabbed his sleeve.

Will said, "It knows your phrase but not why you say it."

Joel shook his head. He could not write fast enough. He could not make the page do the work of a hand over a mouth, and the hand over the mouth had already failed.

He wrote:

IT DOES NOT NEED WHY.

Will stared at the line. Around them the portage held its breath in the ordinary way of wet woods. Water ticked from spruce tips. A mosquito moved near Joel's ear with a thin almost electric whine. Somewhere close, a branch settled under its own wet weight. The world kept producing sound without choosing to. That was the unfairness of it. Men could discipline their voices, ration words, turn friendship into marks on paper, but a pack still creaked and water still dropped and a hurt man still breathed.

Will touched the line Joel had written.

"We need why," he said.

The sentence was so quiet Joel almost wished the woods would miss it.

From the right, Alex's hard laugh broke once. It had all of Alex's surface in it, the bark, the big-body delight, the demand that the group turn toward him. It had none of the warmth that used to follow, none of the living preparation for the next stupid thing he would say.

Then Alex said, "Quit mothering me."

Will closed his eyes.

Joel saw the voice enter him. Not as belief. As memory. Alex in the old light at First Camp, making a face over bland food. Alex on the beaver dam, strong and annoyed and funny. Alex at Second Camp, half outside the firelight, wanting everyone to notice his exit. Every dead use of him was crude and exact. The thing did not understand grief and did not need to. It only had to strike the shape grief had left.

Will opened his eyes and said to the trees, "That's not new."

Joel slapped him. Not hard enough to hurt the way he meant it to, but hard enough that Will stopped.

The sound of skin on skin moved out into the brush.

Both men listened.

From somewhere ahead, the same slap returned. Dry, palm-flat, impossible.

Will inhaled.

Joel seized the front of his vest and put his face close enough that no woods should have been able to separate the words from the breath.

"Stop," Joel said.

The word came out before judgment reached it.

Will's eyes went wide. Not because Joel had spoken, but because of what the word gave away. Stop. A command. A need. A sound with Joel's full weight behind it.

From the trees to their left, Joel said, "Stop."

The false voice had no breath on Will's cheek. That was the difference. It could place the word but not the warmth. It could make Joel into air without making him present.

Will turned his head slowly toward the left.

Joel put both hands on him then and drove him back toward the trail. The move surprised them both. Will stumbled, the pack hitting a spruce trunk with a hollow thud. The paddles clattered. The food barrel shifted inside the lash. The portage answered with all of those sounds at once, from ahead and behind and then from the right, a brief inventory of their remaining life repeated by something that had no use for any of it except as bait.

Will shoved Joel off with his forearm.

The shove was weak and badly aimed, but Joel was tired enough that it worked. He stepped back onto a slick root. His heel slid. The notebook fell from his hand and landed open in the mud.

Will looked at it.

So did Joel.

The page had opened to the rules from the cabin, swollen now and smudged by wet: DO NOT ANSWER. SIGHT. RIGHT WORDS WRONG SOURCE. JOEL KEY. TWO MEN.

Two men.

Will bent for the notebook.

Joel bent too.

From directly behind Will, in Mike's voice, came: "Key's here."

Will did not look back. He kept his eyes on Joel.

For one blessed second Joel thought that meant he had won.

Then Will said, still looking at him, "It put key with Mike because we put key with Mike."

Joel held out his hand for the notebook.

Will did not give it to him.

"It's association," Will said. "Not meaning. Maybe not. But close."

His voice had changed. Less wild now. More himself. That was worse. This was the voice Joel had heard over gear spread on garage floors, over maps in bad motel light, over broken stoves and fishing reels and Mike's cheap canoe straps. Will explaining because explanation kept shame out of the room. Will correcting because precision made him useful. Will saying, no, no, no, this part matters.

"Will," Joel said.

The name was out before he could stop it.

Will froze.

So did Joel.

The woods did not answer immediately.

That delay was almost merciful. Almost intelligent. Or only weather, distance, the shape of trees.

Then Joel's voice came from behind them.

"Will."

Not loud. Not a cry. Just the name as Joel had said it, with the small pleading drag he had not known was in it until it came back empty.

Will turned the wrong way.

Joel reached for him and missed the shoulder strap. His fingers caught only the loose end of webbing. The strap burned through his palm.

"No," Joel said.

The woods said, "Will."

Again in Joel's voice.

Will took one step off the portage.

The ground beyond the trail dropped into a shallow runnel hidden by fern and alder. Not steep. Nothing dramatic. Exactly the kind of off-trail dip a man could step into, curse, climb out of, and mock himself for later. Stones showed through the moss there, flat and dark, their faces slick with water. Beyond them the forest thickened, spruce packed close and gray dead branches crossing at knee and chest height.

Joel went after him.

Branches whipped his face. A dead twig scratched across his lower lip and left the taste of blood. The pack on Will's back made a path by force, breaking small limbs, stripping needles. Joel saw the food barrel bulge round under the wet fabric. He saw the paddles swinging loose in their lash. He saw Will's injured hand half-open, fingers useless and white at the tips.

"Say something new," Will called again.

His voice had lifted now. It was no longer experiment only. It was desperation wearing experiment's clothes.

"Say something you didn't steal."

From ahead, Will's own voice said, "Say something new."

Will stopped.

Joel nearly ran into him.

The copy came again, slightly to the right.

"Say something you didn't steal."

It was exact enough to hurt and wrong enough to be clear. The first sentence carried Will's crack on new. The second had no understanding of accusation. It made steal into a shape, not a charge.

Will laughed once.

"There," he said.

Joel grabbed the back of his vest. "No."

"There," Will said again. "It can repeat the challenge but not answer it."

The opening ahead was no more than a wet place where two old trees had fallen and let in a little more light. Stones showed under moss. Alder grew low and dense at the far edge. The air smelled of rot and cold water. Nothing in it should have held a man.

Something moved there.

Joel did not see a body. He saw a pale angle between trunks, lower than a standing man and higher than a crawling animal. He saw a dark wet shoulder or flank, if shoulder and flank were words that could be trusted. He saw limbs, or branches, crossing where no branch should have crossed. The mind tried to make the pieces useful and failed. Bear. Moose. Man. None of the words held. Each one touched part of it and came away dirty.

Will saw it too.

His face emptied.

For the first time since he had stepped off the trail, he looked less like a man proving a theory and more like a man who had reached the edge of one.

He said, very softly, "Oh."

It was small. It was his last living word.

The motion came low and fast.

Not from the place Joel had fixed on. From the left of it. That was what stayed with him afterward. Even in the moment of seeing, sight could be tricked by attention. A pale flash drew the eye. A sound held the head. The real motion used the place between.

Will went down without a scream.

The pack hit stone first. The impact drove the breath out of him in a hard animal grunt. His injured hand struck next, bandage bright against black rock. Joel saw the fingers spread and fold wrong. Then alder took the upper half of him. The brush thrashed once, high and violent, as if a canoe had been shoved through it sideways.

Joel had the hatchet in his hand.

He did not remember drawing it.

For one impossible second the world offered him the old shape of courage. Charge. Swing. Get him. Do not leave another man.

Then Will's legs kicked once in the moss and vanished sideways.

The alder closed.

Joel stood with the hatchet raised and understood, with a clarity that would never forgive him, that the part of him that loved Will was not stronger than the part that understood the rule.

He backed away.

One step.

Then another.

He kept the hatchet up until his wrist began to shake. The opening did not move. The pale angle was gone. The dark wet shoulder was gone. Only alder. Only stones. Only a strip of bandage caught on a low branch, white and then pink where water or blood moved through it.

From the brush came Will's voice.

"No, no, no."

Panicked. Perfect. Empty.

Joel's foot found the runnel. Water filled his sandal. He did not look down.

"No, no, no."

This time it came from the portage behind him, ahead of where the living Will had been. Too soon. Too clean. The panic preserved but not lived.

Joel backed to the trail.

The notebook lay in the mud where it had fallen. He wanted it with an intensity that surprised him. Not for the rules. Not only for the rules. It had become the last place where two men had still been two men. A shared page. A little roof made of paper.

Will's voice said, from the brush, "Joel."

Joel bent for the notebook.

The name came again from ahead, in Will's voice. Then from behind, in Joel's own.

"Joel."

He picked up the notebook and closed it without reading the open page. Mud smeared under his thumb. He shoved it inside his jacket, against the key.

The canoe waited where he had dropped it, half in brush, its green hull catching the weak light. It looked too ordinary to have survived the scene. Scratched, wet, stubborn. A thing made to cross water, now lying on a trail with two dead men's sounds around it.

From the alder: "No, no, no."

Joel put the hatchet back at his belt. His hand did not want to let go of it. He had to open each finger.

The heavy pack was gone with Will. Food barrel, paddles, some of the food, some of the gear. The loss moved through Joel practically before it moved through him morally. That also would not forgive him. He had the canoe. He had the hatchet. He had the notebook. He had the key. He had whatever was still in his jacket pockets and the small dry bag he had lashed under the canoe seat before the portage. He did not have Will.

Will laughed from somewhere to the right.

Not his real laugh from before. A cough-laugh from the old days, from before correcting someone. The entity placed it near the trail, then farther ahead, then behind Joel at shoulder height, as if trying the sound in different rooms.

Joel lifted the canoe.

The yoke came down on the bruised place at the base of his neck. Pain steadied him. The hull narrowed the world to mud and roots and the thin view under green Kevlar. He could not see far. He could not afford not to see. He had chosen a tool that blocked sight because leaving it meant losing water.

This was the kind of choice left.

He took one step.

From the brush behind him, Alex said, "You coming or writing a poem?"

Joel did not answer.

He took another step.

Mike cleared his throat ahead.

"We're exactly where we ought to be."

Joel stopped.

The phrase moved through the hull and into the small chamber around his head. It filled the canoe's interior like breath. Mike's certainty. Mike's old managerial lie. The whole trip reduced to one sentence the woods could now speak better than the living man ever had, because the woods did not have to believe it. It only had to know when it hurt.

Joel lowered his head under the hull until his forehead almost touched the yoke.

He did not answer.

Will's voice came from the right side of the trail.

"There is a difference."

Joel moved.

The portage accepted him badly. The canoe struck alder on both sides. Brush scraped the hull with long fingernail sounds. Twice he had to stop and back up to free the front from young spruce. Each stop opened a place for sound.

Will: "No, no, no."

Alex: "Hold up."

Mike: "Just keep moving."

Joel's own voice: "Wait. Just wait."

The words came from wrong distances, wrong heights, wrong sequences. They made arguments without meaning to. They contradicted and instructed and pleaded. They were almost human because humans had made them.

Joel kept his mouth shut.

His breath grew loud inside the hull. He hated that too. The living body was an instrument. Fear played it. Pain played it. Cold played it. He tried to breathe through his nose, slowly, and failed. His foot slipped on a wet stone and his knee struck root. He stayed upright because dropping the canoe would make a sound large enough to gather everything.

At the next bend, the portage widened enough for him to set the canoe down without crashing it. He did not set it down. He shifted the yoke on his shoulders and kept moving.

Behind him, Will said, "Oh."

Joel nearly fell.

The sound was too small. Not the panic phrase. Not the old correction. The last living word, returned before it had cooled. It came once from behind, from the place where Will had seen enough to stop speaking.

Then again from ahead, too clean.

"Oh."

The copy held no discovery. No end of argument. Only the vowel, lifted and placed.

Joel understood then that Will had been right about the limit and wrong about what a limit offered. The thing did not understand. It did not need to. It could fail at meaning and still kill men who needed meaning to save them.

He walked.

The trail began to slope down. He could smell water before he saw it, cold and mineral, with the faint rotting sweetness of leaves at a lake edge. The spruce thinned. Light widened under the canoe. The final lake lay somewhere ahead, the road beyond it, the bridge, the Highlander, the hard little key against his chest.

Will said, far behind him, "No, no, no."

Joel put one foot down, then the next, and carried the canoe toward the last lake alone.


# Chapter 26 - Alone

Alone simplified the arithmetic and ruined everything else.

Joel had one canoe, one small pack cut down to what could still be called useful, one map too soft at the folds, one Highlander key, one hatchet, one filter, one emergency paddle too short for real travel, one wet sleeping bag he did not plan to unroll, and daylight draining through low cloud.

He also had three dead men behind him who would not stop speaking.

Mike told him to just keep moving.

Alex asked if he was coming or writing a poem.

Will coughed from ahead, behind, and once from so close that Joel felt the old expectation of breath on the back of his neck, though no breath came with it.

Joel kept the canoe on his shoulders and did not turn.

The portage bent through black spruce and alder, then dropped and climbed in small unnecessary humps that had seemed minor when there had been two men under load, three men before that, four before the world had begun spending them. Now each rise forced him to stop and set his feet. The yoke pressed into the bruised base of his neck. The hull narrowed his sight to mud, roots, low branch, his own hands on the gunwales, and a thin strip of trail under green Kevlar.

The canoe had become both rescue and blindfold. He needed it to cross the last water. He hated it for the way it took the sky away.

He reached a place where the trail widened around a fallen cedar and set the canoe down as quietly as he could. Quietly was not silent. The hull touched wet duff with a soft, broad sigh. A branch sprang loose and ticked along the side. Water inside the boat shifted toward one end and clicked against a rib.

From the brush, the same water-click answered.

Joel stood over the canoe and waited. Not because waiting was safe. Because movement without knowing what had moved was worse.

The click came again from ten yards off the trail. Then farther away. Then behind him.

Not a voice. Not a command. Only the boat making itself somewhere the boat was not.

He took the notebook from inside his jacket. Mud had dried along the spine and cracked when he opened it. The pages near the middle were swollen into one another. Earlier rules showed through later pages like bruises: DO NOT ANSWER. SIGHT. RIGHT WORDS WRONG SOURCE. TWO MEN.

He crossed out nothing. He did not let himself look long at TWO MEN.

On a cleaner space at the bottom of the page he wrote:

NO NAMES.

NO ANSWERS.

ONLY SEEN IS REAL.

SOUND IS BAIT.

He tore the page halfway, failed to separate it, and stopped. Tearing was sound. Everything was sound. He folded the notebook open instead and pinned the page under the frayed map cord on the canoe thwart, where he could see the first two lines when the boat was on the ground and remember the rest when it was on his shoulders.

The pencil shook in his fingers. He put it away before he wrote a name by accident.

Names had become openings. Not magic. Not superstition. Something simpler and therefore harder to bear. A name made a shape in the air. It asked for a body to answer. It gave grief a handle.

He wanted to say Will's name.

He wanted it with the stupid, animal part of him that still expected Will to come stomping up behind him angry and filthy and triumphant, carrying the heavy pack wrong because he had found some technical reason that the obvious way was not the best way. He wanted to hear the cough before a correction. He wanted to hear, no, no, no, from a living mouth, irritated at Joel's assumptions and not emptied by brush.

He closed his teeth until his jaw hurt.

From the portage behind him, Will said, "There's a difference."

Joel lifted the canoe.

The next quarter mile took the rest of his old life apart in pieces small enough to carry.

He abandoned weight in stages. At first he made a practical pile: the damp tarp folded once and left under a spruce, the extra pot set inside it, the stove wrapped in a sock because the metal ticked when loose. He stood there too long looking at the stove. It had been ordinary as a hinge all week. Click, fuel, flame. Coffee, oats, soup, something hot enough to tell the body it had not been discarded yet.

He left it.

Ten minutes later he left the wet sleeping bag.

That was harder. Not because he believed he would sleep. Sleep had become a thing that belonged to another species. But a sleeping bag meant a plan extending past dark. It meant a man was not only moving but expected to stop and still continue. It was a promise of a body worth warming.

He unstrapped it from the small pack, water heavy in the insulation, and pushed it into a hollow under balsam limbs. He did not hide it well. He did not have enough hope to hide it well.

Alex laughed from somewhere above him, wrong height, wrong place, caught in spruce like a thrown object.

"Quit mothering me," Alex said.

Joel's hands paused on the strap.

He saw Alex at the first camp holding a mug like it was a microphone, making everyone look at him, making Joel resent the need to laugh and then laugh anyway. He saw Alex at the beaver dam with mud up his shins, strong and complaining, one arm out to steady a pack that was not his. He saw the cracked headlamp and the red place in the alder.

The sleeping bag lay under the tree like something already dead.

Joel tightened the remaining strap and moved.

The map was next to fail him, though he could not leave it. The case had grit in the seal. Water had gone in through a corner days ago and made the paper soft. Contour lines blurred where his thumb had pressed them too often. The blue shapes of lakes still held. The black portage line still appeared in pieces. The final lake was there, narrow at its south end, spreading north, then pinching toward the outlet and North Road Bridge.

The map said the take-out was beyond the lake.

The map had also said there was a cabin.

Joel folded it along a crease that no longer wanted to fold and slid it back under the cord. The Highlander key pressed cold against his sternum in the inner pocket of his jacket. It had become absurdly intimate. More intimate than a key should be. A little cut piece of another man's confidence, carried against Joel's heart.

He touched the pocket once to confirm it.

From ahead, Mike cleared his throat.

Joel stopped before the reflex completed. He had almost looked for Mike. Not because he believed. Because a body learns before the mind can forbid it. Mike cleared his throat before a decision. Men who had followed him for years turned toward that sound.

"We're exactly where we ought to be," Mike said.

The words moved through the tunnel of the canoe and filled the space around Joel's head. Mike's route certainty, preserved without Mike. The whole trip reduced to one sentence the woods could now speak better than the living man ever had, because the woods did not have to believe it. It only had to know when it hurt.

Joel looked at the mud under his feet.

One sandal strap had torn at the side. He had tied it with cord and the knot pressed into the soft place above his arch. His left knee had begun to swell from the root he struck after Will died. His hands were pale and dirty around the gunwales. Blood had dried in the cracks of his knuckles and turned almost black.

Those were facts. His feet. His hands. The trail he could see.

He moved.

The thing changed tactics when the voices did not turn him.

A boot scraped stone behind him. One scrape, then another, spaced like a tired man following. Joel kept walking until the scrape came again, closer. He stopped and turned with the whole canoe, slow, because the hull would catch if he spun.

Nothing stood in the trail.

The mud held his own tracks, deep at the heel where fatigue made him careless. A strip of alder leaf trembled. Water fell from spruce tips. The portage behind him curved out of sight as if the world had been folded there.

A boot scraped stone ahead.

Joel faced forward again.

He did not write a new rule. The old one covered it. SOUND IS BAIT. He repeated the words in his head without moving his mouth.

The portage dropped into a wet section where old planks had once crossed the mud. Only three remained, black, slick, and half sunk. He set the canoe down before them and made himself plan the passage. One man under a canoe could not see where the back end swung. One man could not have another voice say left, easy, hold up, your end's catching. One man had to become both ends and still remain only one body.

He dragged the canoe sideways around the worst mud, then lifted the front over a fallen branch, stepped into the muck to mid-calf, and pulled. Mud closed cold around his sandal. The sucking sound was obscene in its familiarity. It had been funny on Day 2. Alex making a performance of losing a sandal. Will explaining that peat behaved differently from clay. Mike telling everyone to keep moving because mosquitoes did not care about footwear.

Now the mud made the same wet mouth around Joel's foot and the woods answered with another pull, another release, somewhere out of sight.

He leaned his weight on the canoe and freed himself slowly.

From the right, Will coughed.

Not words. Worse, maybe, because the cough had no argument in it. It was Will before speech. Will about to explain. Will alive in the half-second before language armed him.

Joel closed his eyes.

Only briefly. Too briefly for the dark behind his lids to become anything. Then he opened them and looked at the canoe, the planks, the mud, the line of the trail. He had no room for reverence. No room for farewell. Grief wanted ceremony and ceremony wanted words.

He lifted the canoe again.

The emergency paddle was a joke of a thing by canoe-trip standards: aluminum shaft, black plastic blade, two pieces that screwed together with a collar. Mike had mocked it when he saw it packed under the front seat, then kept it because even mockery had limits around water. The proper paddles had been lashed to Will's heavy pack. They were gone with him into the alder, with the food barrel and most of the remaining food and the repair kit and the folded tarp line Joel had meant to keep.

The emergency paddle clicked under the seat each time the canoe tilted.

Click.

From the left, another click.

Joel set his jaw and carried it anyway.

He thought of the group before sound had become poisonous. Four men took up space differently than one. Four men made ordinary noise around an object until the object almost disappeared inside use: jokes over lashings, argument over load order, a paddle knocked loose and cursed at, Alex asking if anyone had seen his sunglasses while the sunglasses sat on his hat. The canoe had belonged to all of them then. It had been lifted badly, corrected, balanced, shoved into water, bumped against rock, cursed into usefulness.

Now it was just weight and green hull and the fact of the last lake.

At the next rise, the trail narrowed under young spruce. Branches clawed both sides of the canoe. He had to turn sideways, lower the bow, back up, lift again, and push through by inches. Each touch made the hull speak. Scratch, knock, scrape. The portage repeated the sounds ahead of him and behind him until the whole trail seemed to be carrying a canoe badly.

He stopped once to drink.

The filter squeaked when he loosened it from the side pocket. He had half a bottle left, cloudy with grit, warm from his body. He let himself take three swallows. His throat wanted more. He capped it and put it away.

The filter squeaked from the trees.

Then Alex said, "Jesus, listen to this guy."

It was almost funny. The wrongness of it, the timing, the old line dropped into a moment with no guy left to listen to. Joel felt laughter rise in him as nausea. He bent over with both hands on his knees and kept his mouth shut until the impulse passed.

If he laughed, it would have his laugh.

He had not thought of that before with enough force. The entity had Joel's voice. It had "Wait. Just wait." It had "Stop." It had Will's name, said with pleading in it. It might not have his laugh. Joel had not laughed much after the first day. He could not remember the last time. The possibility made silence feel not only tactical but possessive. His remaining sounds were the last private things he owned.

He stood.

The trail changed near late afternoon. He knew it by light before map. The spruce thinned. The ground began a long, steady drop. Air moved differently ahead, cooler and wider, carrying the mineral smell of open water and wet stone instead of only rot and needles. A raven called once from somewhere high. He waited for the call to repeat wrong, but the sky gave him nothing.

The ordinary world had not stopped. That was one of the cruelties. Some sounds were still only sounds. Water still dripped from branches. Birds still moved through black spruce. Wind still rubbed the lake ahead, indifferent to whether men reached it.

The final stretch of portage was rockier. Granite showed through the moss in gray backs. His sandals slipped twice. The canoe's weight had become a fact beyond pain, a number too large to use. His shoulders went numb and then sharpened again. The yoke seemed to settle into bone.

Will said from behind him, "I can make it contradict itself."

Joel nearly answered in his head. No.

He bit the inside of his cheek and tasted blood.

"No, no, no," Will said.

The phrase came lower this time, more private. Not the panic from the brush after he died, but the old Will before panic had taken him: correcting a gear mistake, resisting a bad explanation, trying to keep the world from being stupid. Joel saw him in Will's garage years ago with a headlamp strap around his forehead in daylight, both of them half-drunk, Will taking apart a cheap lantern because the battery contact offended him. No, no, no, that's not how that works. Joel had laughed then. Will had looked pleased and pretended not to be.

Joel stopped. The canoe rocked on his shoulders.

He did not know how long he stood there.

The woods waited with him. Or did not wait. It was impossible to tell the difference between patience and absence when the thing had no face.

He lowered the canoe carefully at the edge of the landing.

The final portage ended without ceremony. No sign. No blaze. No sudden widening into safety. The trail simply gave up into a sloped mix of gravel, mud, and flat stones where the last lake took the land in small dark licks. Alder crowded the left side. Low black spruce ran down the right shore. The water stretched north under dusk-colored cloud, a long narrow reach between dark banks, wrinkled by wind moving along it rather than across.

That mattered.

Wind across the lake would have ended him or made him wait for dark, which was another way to end. Wind along it was trouble but not refusal. He could paddle that if his hands kept working and if the short emergency paddle did not come apart and if the map meant anything and if he did not fall asleep sitting up.

He stood at the landing and listened.

The woods behind him assembled the group.

Mike cleared his throat.

Will said, "I can make it contradict itself."

Alex laughed.

The sounds did not overlap. They took turns like men around a fire, each waiting for the old rhythm to receive him. The arrangement hurt more than noise would have. It made a circle out of absence.

Joel crouched beside the canoe and inventoried what could go forward.

Small pack: map, filter, bottle, headlamp with weak batteries, knife, two energy bars crushed into meal, first-aid roll reduced to tape and dirty gauze, lighter in a plastic sleeve, pencil, notebook when it was not in his jacket. Hatchet at belt. Key in inner pocket. Emergency paddle under the front seat in the blue dry bag with a pair of socks, the torn corner of the old repair sleeve, and the little orange whistle nobody had used because whistles were sound and sound had become meat.

He took the whistle out and threw it into the lake.

Not far. His throw was bad with fatigue. It struck water five yards out and floated for a moment, bright and ridiculous, before a small ripple turned it over. He watched until it drifted beyond reach.

Alex said, "Hold up."

Joel loaded the canoe.

He did it without hurry because hurry made noise and mistakes. The pack went behind the middle thwart, close enough to reach, low enough not to shift. The blue dry bag went under the front seat where he could see it. He screwed the emergency paddle together and tightened the collar until his fingers slipped. The joint flexed. He tightened it again. He turned the canoe parallel to the water and checked the painter knot. The shortened rope was stiff with dried mud.

Each action arrived with the ghost of the man who should have argued about it.

Mike would have told him the trim was wrong.

Will would have said there was no point in calling it trim when it was one exhausted idiot in one ruined canoe.

Alex would have asked if this was the part where Joel became rugged and insufferable.

Joel put one hand over his mouth until the almost-smile died there.

He needed direction. That was the hard fact beneath all the others. The map showed the lake running north, outlet at the far end, bridge beyond, Highlander waiting if the key still mattered and the road had not swallowed itself in rain. But the shoreline near the landing split into two dark possibilities. A left-hand channel tucked behind a reed point. A broader right-hand reach held the wind and looked more like the main lake. In daylight with Will and Mike and time, they would have read the shore, checked the map against slope and contour, argued, corrected, chosen. Alone at dusk, with voices in the trees, every direction could be made to sound like help.

He could not ask the map to speak.

He could not ask the dead.

He needed bait with no grief in it.

The thought came without drama. It was not bravery. It was procedure. The entity could use what it heard. It used old things because old things were what the men had given it. So give it one new thing. Give it a stone with no blood on it. A fact so flat it could not tug unless the wrongness of its use exposed it.

He looked around for an object.

The blue dry bag sat under the front seat. It was visible. Its color had no memory except inventory. Not Alex's food complaints, not Mike's route certainty, not Will's correction habit, not Joel's own pleading. A bag. Blue. Under the front.

He opened the notebook to the wet rules page and wrote under SOUND IS BAIT:

GIVE IT ONE THING.

Then, on the torn corner of the map margin, he wrote:

BLUE BAG UNDER THE FRONT.

He stared at the words until they became only words.

The woods stayed quiet.

Quiet was not consent. Quiet was not safety. Quiet was only the absence of a sound he had noticed.

He put the map margin under the thwart cord beside the rules, facing up. He wanted the words where his eyes could find them after his mind began to slip. Then he stood at the edge of the landing, facing the lake, not the woods.

His throat did not want to open. It had learned too late.

He waited until the wind moved along the water and covered the tremor in his breath. He looked at the bag. He looked at the right-hand reach. He looked at the left-hand channel. He held both possibilities in his mind and gave the woods nothing from either one.

Then he said clearly, once, "Blue bag under the front."

The sentence went out over the lake and came back smaller.

Behind him, nothing answered.

He waited until waiting became danger.

Mike said, very softly, "Just keep moving."

Joel did not obey Mike. He obeyed the daylight leaving the water.

He slid the canoe in. Gravel ground under the hull. The sound traveled along shore and returned as a second scrape from the alder, then a third from the black trees to the right. He stepped into the cold shallows, one sandal flooding, and held the canoe until it floated free. The lake took the weight with no interest in him.

He climbed in awkwardly from the side, too tired for elegance, and nearly tipped when the pack shifted. His hand shot to the opposite gunwale. Water slapped the hull.

From the left shore, the same slap answered.

He steadied the boat and did not look.

The emergency paddle felt wrong at once. Too short, too flexible, the blade fluttering when he pulled. He made it work by shortening the stroke and keeping the shaft close. The canoe moved. Slowly. Enough.

He pushed off from the last land that had held Will alive.

The landing receded.

From the trees to his left, Alex's voice said, "Blue bag under the front."

It was perfect and useless.

Joel paddled right.


# Chapter 27 - The Planted Phrase

The planted phrase saved him by being stupid.

No memory hung from it. No old trip. No argument. No dead man's way of handling shame. Blue bag under the front. A fact, and not even an important one now that the bag was in the canoe and Joel could see it.

The first time the thing used it, Alex said it from the left shore.

"Blue bag under the front."

Teasing. Almost amused. As if the bag had done something funny and Alex had found the smallest possible way to make Joel look at it.

Joel did not look left.

He looked at the bag.

It sat under the front seat, the blue dulled by mud and last light, the rolled mouth buckled twice, one corner pinched under the aluminum tube of the emergency paddle before Joel had pulled the paddle free. Its presence was not an answer. It was not rescue. It proved only that a voice in the trees could repeat a sentence about an object it did not need and could not see from there.

That was enough.

The canoe drifted while he made himself understand the difference between enough and safe. Wind pushed along the lake in narrow gusts, lifting small black wrinkles off the water. The right-hand reach opened ahead of him, darker than the shore but less dark than the alder-choked left channel where Alex's voice had placed itself. The map under the thwart cord showed a north-running lake, then a pinch, then water beyond. But the map had already been right in shapes and wrong in mercy. A blue line could be true and still not save anyone.

Joel set the short paddle on the right side and pulled.

The canoe answered late.

Everything about it was wrong now. Too little load in the bow, too much human weight in the stern, no second paddle correcting small mistakes before they became large ones. With Alex in front, the boat had jerked and argued but had still had another body reading the water. With Will, after Alex, it had been overloaded and miserable and alive with disagreement. One man in the back made the canoe lift its nose as if sniffing for trouble. Each stroke swung the bow wider than Joel wanted. Each correction cost more water than it gained.

He moved anyway.

From the left shore, Alex laughed once.

The sound ran along the reeds, struck the open water, and came back thinner, in the old bridge way. Joel saw, without wanting to, Alex leaning over the rail at North Road Bridge on the first morning, pleased with his own echo, not knowing he had given the woods a rehearsal. The memory arrived bright and useless. He let it pass without giving it his mouth.

The second use came ten strokes later.

Will said it from behind him.

"Blue bag under the front."

Not amused. Urgent. Technical. Will's voice made the phrase into an instruction Joel had failed to obey correctly. He could almost hear the sentence that should have followed: No, no, no, you're shifting the trim, that's not how that works.

Joel's shoulders tightened. The canoe yawed. The left gunwale dipped toward a little slap of chop.

He corrected too hard and the paddle blade fluttered in his hands. Aluminum flexed at the joint. The cheap collar gave a small dry click.

From behind him, the same click answered.

He wanted Will there with a fury so sudden it felt like nausea. He wanted the correction. He wanted the fight. He wanted Will in the bow, cold and furious and alive, complaining that Joel's steering was turning them into a physics demonstration. The wanting came before thought and nearly became speech.

He pressed his tongue against the roof of his mouth until it hurt.

The boat steadied.

He wrote no new rule. He could not write while moving, and stopping in open water was another kind of answer. The old rules sat in the notebook under his jacket and on the page pinned at the thwart behind him: NO NAMES. NO ANSWERS. ONLY SEEN IS REAL. SOUND IS BAIT. GIVE IT ONE THING.

He had given it one thing.

It had not become his friend.

It had not become a map.

It was only a mark on the water, a colored rag tied to a trap, a way to see the trap spring.

The lake narrowed by degrees. Low reeds began to show in clumps to the left. The right shore lifted into black spruce and small granite shelves, all of it sliding past too slowly. Dusk thickened under the cloud. The water stopped reflecting sky and began holding it in broken, darker strips. Every paddle stroke put a small wound in that surface and closed it again.

Joel tried to count strokes to keep himself from thinking.

One through twenty on the right.

One through fifteen on the left, because the left shoulder had less left in it.

Switch before the bow wandered.

Do not reach. Short stroke. Close to the boat.

Do not listen for meaning.

He counted wrong almost at once. The numbers became men. Four had left the motel. Three had left Second Camp. Two had left the cabin. One had left Will.

He stopped counting.

Something knocked from the right shore. Paddle on gunwale.

Not a voice. Older than the voices. A trip sound. A boat sound. A sound from before the woods had learned grammar.

Joel looked at the blue bag.

It sat under the front seat, visible, ordinary.

The knock came again from the right. Once, then twice, the rhythm of a man tapping for attention because shouting across water was rude before it was dangerous.

He paddled left.

Not because left was safe. Because the sound wanted his right side. Because the right shore, which should have been quiet if it was only spruce and stone, had chosen the language of men and gear.

The canoe slid toward the center. Wind took the bow and pushed it back. He leaned forward, though leaning forward hurt his stomach and made the key in his inner pocket press hard against his breastbone.

The key was still there.

He checked it with the heel of his free hand between strokes, a small push against the jacket. Metal under cloth. The whole planned exit reduced to that cold tooth. Mike's key. Mike's Highlander. Mike's confidence broken out of his dead pocket and carried by the man who had learned not to believe confidence just because it had a voice.

Ahead, Mike cleared his throat.

Joel kept paddling.

The throat-clear came again, from the right-hand reach, then from farther ahead, exactly where the lake seemed to open.

"Blue bag under the front," Mike said.

Managerial. Certain. The phrase became inventory, became route call, became an item checked off in Mike's head before the next instruction. It belonged in his mouth more easily than Joel liked. Mike had been good at making objects into control. Boat. Pack. Key. Map. Cabin. Even dead, his voice tried to put the blue bag into a system Joel could obey.

Joel almost did.

The right-hand reach looked broader. The wind ran there, and running wind meant depth, travel, main water. The left stayed brushy, broken by reeds and a small point that could hide a dead channel. A living Mike would have gone right unless the map said otherwise. A living Mike would have been loud about it. A living Will would have argued him there and maybe been correct. Alex would have asked if anyone else found this team-building exercise spiritually rich.

Joel held the paddle across his knees and let the canoe glide.

He made himself use his eyes.

The right-hand reach had open water, yes, but its far end rose dark against the surface. No break in tree line. No lower seam where water passed out. The wind made it look like a way because wind preferred open length. The left channel looked worse because the reeds masked it, but beyond the reed point he could see a darker cut, a long narrow absence between banks.

He looked at the map.

The map was nearly useless in the failing light. The page had swollen under its plastic, the ink blurred at old folds, but the north end of this lake narrowed before opening again. The pinch could be hidden behind reeds. The broader right-hand reach could be a bay that map scale made cruel.

Mike's voice came from the right again.

"It opens up after this."

Joel closed his eyes for one breath, then opened them because closed eyes gave sound too much room.

The entity had not used the planted phrase to reveal the correct direction. It had used the phrase the way it used everything else: to make a body move before the mind could finish looking.

He turned the canoe left of center, not into the reeds but toward the visible cut beyond them.

The wind dropped near full dark.

That should have helped. Instead the silence grew large enough to contain every voice the thing had learned. Without wind, the little sounds of the boat separated from one another and became suspicious. Water ticking from the paddle. The hull's quiet rub against a floating stem. His breath catching when the blade found mud instead of depth. The collar of the emergency paddle creaking under his lower hand.

Then the voices came at intervals, never all at once for long.

Mike's throat-clear.

Will's cough.

Alex's laugh.

Mike saying, "We're exactly where we ought to be."

Joel's own voice saying, "Wait. Just wait."

That one nearly turned him.

It was not command that hurt. It was familiarity. His own restraint used against him. The voice sounded careful. Reasonable. Alive in the way he was alive, alone in the back of a canoe trying not to become sound for something else.

He let the paddle rest for half a second. Too long. The canoe lost its line and began to slide sideways toward the reed point. A stem touched the hull with a faint dry scrape.

From the reeds, Joel's voice said, "Wait."

Only the one word.

Less was worse. Less sounded more like him because he had never been a man of big speeches. He had given the woods so little that it could make a meal out of crumbs.

He did not answer. He did not wait.

He planted the paddle and shoved away from the reeds. The blade struck bottom. Mud took it and held. For one bad second the paddle would not come free. The canoe kept moving. The shaft bent. He saw himself losing it, saw the emergency paddle sliding out of his hands into black water, saw the remaining route become a joke too stupid to be called fate.

He twisted, pulled straight up, and the blade came loose with a wet pop.

The same pop sounded from behind him.

Then to the right.

Then under the canoe.

Joel froze with the paddle across his lap.

Under the canoe was not a direction. Under the canoe was a thought his body had before he could stop it: something beneath him, pale, close, waiting for the shape of a man to lean too far over the gunwale.

He looked down only as far as the blue bag.

It was still there. The bag. The pack behind him. His knees against the ribs. The dull gleam of water inside the hull, where earlier spray had pooled and carried a spruce needle back and forth with each small rock of the boat. These were facts close enough to see.

The planted phrase came again from open water to his right, in Will's voice.

"Blue bag under the front."

There could be no speaker there. No shore. No body. Just water, dark, a few pale chips of reflected cloud.

Joel understood then that the thing could throw sound across emptiness or move where he could not see. Understanding did not change the rule. It sharpened it.

Sight had never meant safety. It meant only that sound alone could not vote.

He kept the bow toward the black cut in the trees where the map promised an outlet.

The cut did not resolve quickly. It lengthened and shortened with the angle of his approach, sometimes visible between reed stalks, sometimes gone behind the shoulder of the point. The water shallowed. Twice the paddle struck submerged stems. Once the hull slid over something soft that gave under it, not rock, not wood, and Joel's whole back tightened. The canoe slowed as if a hand had taken the stern painter.

He turned enough to see behind him.

No hand. No rope. Only the small wake opening in a V, black on black, closing itself as if the canoe had never passed.

From the shore, Alex said, "Hold up."

The old reflex went through Joel's hands. Hold up meant obstacle, pack strap, sandal caught, a body needing ten seconds without having to confess the need. Hold up had been useful before it was fatal. He had answered it for days. He had answered it in spirit before the trip even began, whenever Alex performed helplessness as a joke and someone else paid the small cost.

Joel did not hold up.

He paddled.

The left shore pressed closer. Alder leaned out over the water, black stems combing the surface. The canoe moved inside the smell of mud, cold vegetation, and the sour rot of reeds. Mosquitoes lifted from the bank in a thin late swarm and found his face. He let them. Swatting was sound. Swatting was a hand off the paddle. One landed at the corner of his eye and drank there while tears from the irritation blurred the cut ahead.

He blinked it clear.

The blue bag showed less in the dark now. It was not blue so much as a remembered blue, a slightly different darkness under the front seat. Joel reached forward with the paddle shaft and touched it once. The plastic crinkled.

From the bank, the crinkle answered.

"Blue bag under the front," Alex said again.

This time Alex sounded bored. That was almost worse than teasing. The phrase had already become nothing to the thing. It could use a new sentence until it wore the newness off. It did not care what the sentence meant. It cared what a living body did when a familiar mouth held it.

Joel knew then the tactic would not last.

It had bought him direction once. Maybe twice. It would not carry him to the bridge. It would not protect him from his own voice. It would not make the entity stupid enough to defeat itself like a puzzle in a book.

The thought should have discouraged him. Instead it steadied something small and mean in him. He did not need a solution. He needed the next hundred feet.

The cut opened.

Not wide. Not welcoming. A seam of darker water between reeds, with black spruce crowding both sides beyond. A little current touched the bow and changed the feel of the canoe. Not much. Enough that the water seemed to have a preference. Enough that the lake's flat holding became a pull.

Joel let the bow take it, then checked the map one last time.

He had to use the headlamp. He hated that. Light made him visible in a way darkness did not. Light also made the near world honest for as far as it reached. The lamp sat in the small pack behind him. Getting it meant twisting, risking the boat, making zipper sound.

He waited until the canoe drifted into a small lee behind the reed point, then set the paddle across both gunwales. He turned slowly. His spine cramped. The pack had shifted; he pulled it forward by one strap. The zipper was gritty and loud in the stillness.

From three places at once, zippers answered.

Tent zipper. Gear shelter zipper. Pack zipper. The old sound of people opening and closing little human weather.

Joel found the headlamp by touch, closed the pack without zipping it, and settled back before the canoe could swing broadside. His hands shook too much to fix the strap around his head. He held the lamp against his chest, cupped the beam with his fingers, and clicked it on.

Weak yellow light leaked between his knuckles.

The map appeared in pieces: blue lake, black line, wet wrinkle, his own dirty thumb, the written phrase on the torn margin. BLUE BAG UNDER THE FRONT. The pencil letters had softened at the edges. They looked older than they were, as if all writing aged quickly here.

The line matched enough. The lake narrowed into a channel. The channel bent north and east before opening into the next water. North Road Bridge beyond. Road beyond. Highlander beyond, unless the road had washed out, unless the vehicle would not start, unless the key had bent, unless help was another human mark the world had let decay.

Enough.

He clicked the lamp off.

Dark returned thicker for having been cut.

Mike said from ahead, very softly, "Just keep moving."

For once the dead voice gave useful advice.

Joel hated that and obeyed the living need under it, not the sound.

He stowed the headlamp loose between his knees and took up the paddle. The current gathered him by inches. He gave two small strokes to keep the bow from the reeds.

Something rose ahead.

Low. Pale. A shape of water and wrong joint. Not an animal he could name in the time he had. Not a log, because it bent once against the direction of current. Not a man, because a man would have made the water tell a different story. It broke the surface without a splash and slipped under before the canoe reached it.

The lake closed over with barely a ripple.

Joel's arms stopped.

The canoe did not.

It moved toward the place where the shape had gone under, current and last momentum carrying him, reed shadows sliding along both sides. The paddle lay half raised in his hands. If he dug too hard, he might turn broadside. If he stopped, he would drift over it anyway. If he looked for it, the dark water would become all the places it was not.

From the right bank, Will said, "There's a difference."

Joel put the paddle in.

Small stroke. Close to the boat. Another. Another.

The hull passed over the place where the pale thing had been. Nothing struck. Nothing touched. Water made its ordinary small talk along the ribs. His own breath sawed in and out through his nose because his mouth was closed and would stay closed.

Behind him, in Mike's voice, "Blue bag under the front."

Ahead, in Joel's voice, "Wait. Just wait."

To the left, in Alex's, "You coming or writing a poem?"

The voices no longer tried to be plausible. They tried to be many. They tried to make the world all invitation and correction. They made old friendship into a net, each strand a habit a living man would once have honored without thinking.

Joel looked at the channel.

He looked at the water he could see.

He looked once at the blue bag, dark under the front seat, stupid and faithful only because objects did not answer when called.

Then he chose against the voices again.

He set the bow into the black cut and committed to the crossing beyond it.


# Chapter 28 - The Crossing

The last crossing was not large on the map.

On paper it was a thumb's width of blue between two marks a man had made for convenience. Lake, outlet, bridge. A route reduced until it could be folded and slipped into a plastic case. On the water, in dark, alone, after seven days had been stripped down to one body's remaining heat, it was a country.

Joel entered it through the black seam at the end of the channel.

The current took the canoe before he was ready. It did not grab. It gathered. The flat holding of the lake changed under him to a narrow pull, a decision made by water against stone. Reeds closed at both sides and brushed the hull with dry fingernail sounds. Alder leaned over the channel, wet leaves touching his shoulders when the canoe slid too near the bank. The emergency paddle felt short and cheap in his hands. Each stroke came with a click from the collar, a small mechanical confession he could not stop making.

Behind him, Alex said, "Blue bag under the front."

Joel kept the paddle moving.

The bag was under the front seat. He did not need to look. He looked anyway with only the lower half of his vision: dark lump, rolled mouth, buckle dulled by mud. A fact still there after being named. A fact no voice could turn into rescue.

The channel bent left and dropped.

He heard the drop before he saw it. Water working over stone, lower than wind, not loud enough to be rapid and too loud to be ignored. The sound came through the hull into his knees. The canoe's front began to hunt, nosing from side to side in the narrow current. There was no room to stop. No place to land that did not put him inside the alder where every branch had learned to sound like someone moving parallel to him.

Something moved parallel anyway.

On the right bank, out of sight behind the hanging brush, a body kept pace at walking speed. Not crashing. Not rushing. Step and stop, step and stop, the rhythm adjusted to the canoe's drift. Joel saw nothing except the black comb of alder stems and a little pale scratch of exposed root. The movement remained beside him for six heartbeats, close enough that if he had reached out he could have touched leaves wet from whatever had passed behind them.

Will coughed there.

Not behind him, not ahead. Beside him. The small dry cough that used to announce correction, annoyance, intelligence gathering itself for the pleasure of being right.

Joel set the paddle blade on the left and pulled hard.

The canoe slid away from the bank. The current caught the front crooked and turned it toward the stones.

"That's not how that works," Will said, still at walking speed.

The phrase struck where it was meant to strike. Will would have said it here. He would have said it because the boat angle was wrong, because Joel was fighting current with exhausted arms and a half paddle, because water had rules even terror did not cancel. The voice used correctness as bait. It did not have to understand water. It only had to put a living man's reflex back in him.

Joel did not correct it.

The first stone hit under the hull with a flat crack. The canoe lifted, slewed, dropped. Cold water slapped over the left side and filled the space around Joel's sandals. His legs flinched before he could stop them. The paddle blade struck rock and skated. Aluminum rang through the shaft into his wrists.

The second stone spun him.

For a moment the canoe went broadside to the run, the right side lower, current piling against it with a pushing hiss. Broached. The word came from some old trip, from Mike or Will, from a day when words were tools and not invitations. Joel threw his weight downstream without thinking. The hull tilted. Water came in over his thigh. The blue bag slid forward, struck the rib under the front seat, and stopped.

Mike cleared his throat from directly ahead.

"Just keep moving."

It was the first useful thing a dead voice had said all day.

Joel hated it and obeyed the water, not Mike.

He dug the paddle against stone and shoved. The blade bent. The canoe scraped free with a sound like a chair dragged across concrete. He took two fast strokes, both ugly, both enough. The current straightened the boat and shot him through the last tongue of black water into a wider dark.

The final lake opened without ceremony.

No moon. No visible far shore. Only the feeling of the banks falling away and the water under him losing its urgency. The current spread and vanished into a surface that did not tell him where it ended. Wind moved across it from the north in low bands. Not strong. Enough to lift small waves that slapped the canoe's front and came aboard as cold beads on his knees.

Joel turned back once.

The outlet had already narrowed to an absence between reeds. The channel did not look large enough to have produced him. It looked like a place a man could vanish into by mistake and never convince anyone there had been a way through. The thought of the map folded in the pack was almost funny. Blue lines, black ticks, contour, bridge. A whole argument with the world conducted in ink, and the world under no obligation to answer in the same language.

He faced forward.

The lake had no forward. That was the first problem.

He had a softened map, a weak headlamp, a compass he had stopped trusting except as a habit, and the memory of the route from the morning before it all became one continuous strain. The final lake ran north. The bridge lay beyond the north end. The outlet he had entered from should have put him near the lower left of the basin. He needed to angle right of the wind, not fight straight into it, not drift left into a bay if there was a bay. He needed to paddle without stopping and still save enough body to land.

He needed Will's math and Mike's certainty and Alex's refusal to let misery be silent.

He had none of them.

He set a rhythm small enough to keep.

Dip. Pull. Breathe.

Dip. Pull. Breathe.

The paddle was too short for the reach he wanted. He had to lean forward more than was safe, then correct with a short pry that made the canoe's empty front wander out of line. Each correction stole distance. Each pause let wind take the boat and turn it broadside. The water inside the canoe ran from rib to rib, touching his ankles with each rock of the hull. His feet were past cold now. They existed as pressure and weight.

From the left, far out on open water where no bank should have been, a food barrel latch clicked.

Joel's hands stopped.

The click came again. Small, plastic, exact. The morning sound of breakfast being opened. The camp sound of human supply, of calories counted and divided, of Mike saying they had enough if nobody was an idiot about it. The food barrel was gone with Will. The actual latch lay somewhere in brush or mud or water behind him, tied to the last time Will had been alive.

The sound came a third time, now from the right.

Joel looked only at the water he could see. Not left. Not right. The near surface held a few broken strips of cloud and the black notch of the paddle shadow. Sound alone could not vote. He had written it. He had believed it less than he had used it. Now use was all belief meant.

He paddled.

Alex laughed behind him.

The laugh carried across the lake in pieces. Bark, break, smaller echo. It had been born in rooms and parking lots and on ordinary water where a sound came back because concrete or granite returned it, not because anything listened. The first morning at North Road Bridge, Alex had leaned over the rail and enjoyed the echo of himself under the concrete. Joel could see him again too clearly: shoulders loose, coffee breath, expensive rain shell unzipped because he ran hot, one hand drumming the guardrail as if the whole trip had already become a story he would tell better than the rest of them.

"You coming or writing a poem?" Alex called.

Joel's throat tightened around the answer his old self would have given. Something dry. Something small enough to keep Alex going and make Will snort and Mike tell them to move. The old friendship had been built from these permissions: answer the tease, obey the useful call, argue the correction, accept the authority when someone had to decide. The thing on the water had not stolen their love. It had stolen the shapes their love took when none of them wanted to name it.

He kept his mouth closed.

The wind freshened.

Small waves now came at an angle across the front quarter. The canoe slapped, lifted, dropped. Spray struck his face. One bead ran into the raw corner of his bitten cheek and tasted of lake and blood. His hands ached where the paddle shaft rubbed skin already blistered soft. He could not feel the Highlander key until he pressed his forearm to his jacket and found the hard point of it under cloth.

Still there.

That was the exit. Not voices. Not map promises. A piece of cut metal in a dead man's vehicle key, zipped into Joel's inner pocket because Mike had died trying to keep control of a world that did not recognize control as a category.

The key had weight.

So did the notebook. Damp against his ribs. Pages full of refusals. Rules that had not saved Alex, Mike, or Will, but had narrowed Joel's death down to something he might still step around. He thought of the penciled lines softening in their own moisture: NO NAMES. NO ANSWERS. ONLY SEEN IS REAL. SOUND IS BAIT. GIVE IT ONE THING.

He had given it one thing and the thing had made a chorus of it.

"Blue bag under the front," Mike said from ahead.

Managerial. Route voice. Inventory voice.

Joel almost smiled from exhaustion. Mike could make anything sound like an item to be checked off before blame began. Bag. Key. Boat. Body. Weather. The phrase in his mouth became proof that the system had not failed if everyone would just follow the system correctly.

The voice shifted left.

"We're exactly where we ought to be."

That one hurt differently.

It came not loud, not taunting. Almost conversational, as Mike had said it on the first morning with the map spread against the hood of the Highlander, treating confidence like a tarp he could throw over uncertainty. Back then it had been irritating. Now it was obscene. They were not where they ought to be. There was no ought here. There was water, weather, a road somewhere beyond the black, and three men gone into sentences that would keep walking without them.

Joel's paddle stroke shortened. The boat slowed.

The voice waited.

He understood the trap only because Mike would not have waited. Mike would have filled the pause with instruction, irritation, a throat-clear, his hand slicing toward the route. The waiting belonged to the other thing. It had learned Mike's certainty but not Mike's impatience.

Joel pulled again.

The lake lifted under him.

The far shore appeared and vanished with the waves. At first he thought it was only cloud thickening at the horizon. Then a darker dark held steady between the rises: tree line, low and uneven. He fixed on it. The canoe swung. He fixed again. There was no star, no moon, no clean point to steer by. Only the knowledge that a shape had remained in the same part of the world for more than one breath.

Will said, "No, no, no."

From the front of the canoe.

Joel did look then, because the front was inside his small circle of fact. There was no one there. Empty aluminum ribs, blue bag under the seat, headlamp loose between his knees throwing no light, black water past the front. The voice had come from where Will's back should have been if Will were alive and angry in the front, if his injured hand were wrapped in filthy tape, if he had turned to say Joel was taking the wrong line and this was exactly why words mattered.

"No, no, no," Will said again, flatter.

The voice had Will's panic without Will's mind. It kept the rhythm and lost the man. That was what the thing did best, Joel thought. It preserved the part that could be used and let the rest rot.

He moved the paddle to the right side and corrected the canoe.

The waves grew steeper near the center. Not large enough to be called dangerous by men who had slept, eaten, and trusted the day. Large enough for one exhausted man in a light, badly trimmed canoe with a toy paddle and no second body to recover the mistake. The front lifted too high and landed crooked. Water came over. The canoe rocked left. Joel leaned right, late, then overcorrected. For a second the lake showed him its inside: black water close to the gunwale, a floating spruce needle bright as wire, the pale oval of his own knee under the surface that had pooled in the boat.

He froze.

"Wait. Just wait," Joel said from the dark ahead.

His own voice did not sound monstrous. That was its damage. It sounded careful. Tired. Reasonable. The voice of a man stopping someone before a strap snapped, before a canoe slid off a truck, before a friend stepped where the footing would not hold. A life of small caution compressed into three words and sent back from a place where caution meant drift, and drift meant losing the line.

Joel did not wait.

He placed the blade close to the hull and pulled a stroke so small it felt useless. Then another. The canoe settled one inch at a time. His body wanted to shake but could not spare the motion. He kept the paddle in. Small stroke. Close. Small stroke. Close.

From behind him came a tent zipper.

Long, patient, opening.

Then a dry bag buckle clicked beside his left ear.

Then paddle on gunwale, once, polite.

The nonverbal sounds were worse than the voices now. A voice could be refused as dead. A zipper carried no death in itself. A latch did not grieve. A knock on a canoe had been useful for years before it became predatory. These sounds entered under thought and began setting up camp in him: first night granite shelf, second camp rain tarp, Mike's hands on straps, Will's rod case, Alex dropping a mug too hard and laughing before Mike could complain. Whole rooms of the trip opened with each small sound.

Joel let them open and did not step inside.

The far shore gained shape.

A low black wall. Spruce, maybe. A gap, maybe. To the right, something paler cut across the dark and vanished. At first he thought lightning had moved behind trees. Then the pale returned, not light but absence: sky under something straight.

Bridge.

Maybe.

The thought weakened him more than fear had.

He had not believed in the bridge as a place. He had carried it as a function. Take-out. Vehicle. Road. Help. The mind did this when distance was too much. It turned the world into uses. Now the object itself began to gather in the dark: a horizontal line where no branch should hold that straight, a squared darkness beneath it, one dull fleck that brightened when a wave lifted him and disappeared when he dropped.

Reflector.

One small human eye in the dark, amber and flat.

Joel stared until it vanished behind the rise of a wave and came back.

His body tried to laugh.

The sound rose in his chest as a dry, cracked thing, more pain than humor. He bit the inside of his cheek before it found his mouth. Blood came quick. Good. His teeth had made a fact. He tasted metal and lake and kept paddling toward the amber fleck.

The voices stopped.

For three strokes he thought he had gone deaf.

The lake had sound: water at the hull, paddle drip, wind low over the surface, the cheap joint of the paddle creaking under his lower hand. But the men were gone. Alex, Mike, Will, Joel's own borrowed voice. All of them absent at once.

The silence did not comfort him.

It opened space.
